HEADLINE: Through Trade and Aid, Iraqi Official Says, Embargo Is Eroding

BYLINE: By BARBARA CROSSETTE 

DATELINE: UNITED NATIONS, April 25

 BODY:
With sanctions against Iraq likely to be extended next week for another six months, Iraq's Foreign Minister said today that the embargo imposed in 1990 is eroding and with it Baghdad's isolation. He predicted that this process would accelerate.
"This is happening whether the Americans like it or not," the official, Mohammed Said al-Sahaf, said in an interview. He said cross-border trade by both Iraqis and outsiders as well as the arrival of donated supplies have increased substantially in recent months. 
 Iraq is barred from selling oil, except in limited quantities to buy civilian goods like medicine and food, so the volume of trade is not huge, diplomats in Baghdad say.
But Mr. Sahaf said he views increased trade and donations, including from Americans, as evidence of a general distaste for indefinite sanctions because of their effect on the Iraqi people. He reiterated Baghdad's demand that all sanctions be lifted immediately, though he did not repeat direct threats against the United States made in recent days by other Iraqi leaders.
The Foreign Minister also renewed Iraq's criticism of the United Nations Special Commission, which is charged with dismantling Iraq's prohibited weapons and production facilities, and of its executive chairman, Richard Butler. He accused the commission, known as Unscom, of withholding information gathered on recent inspections of eight Iraqi presidential properties.
"They took samples in the presidential sites," he said. "They took air samples, earth samples, dust samples and so on, in order to be tested in the Unscom laboratory in Baghdad. But when they presented their report, they didn't include what are the results of the tests." Iraq argues that no weapons were ever hidden on the properties.
Unscom officials declined to comment.
Mr. Sahaf said the United States was largely responsible for blocking any change in policy toward Iraq in the Security Council, and he added that Secretary General Kofi Annan and his Secretariat staff were much more "neutral" and easier to work with.
"We know very well that the majority on the Security Council wishes to be fair with Iraq," he said, in an interview at the New York residence of Iraq's United Nations representative, Nizar Hamdoon. "There are only two countries -- the United States and the United Kingdom -- for their own policies. They stand against the majority."
Today in Baghdad, an official newspaper said Iraq would break out of sanctions by the end of the year if the Security Council did not lift them. "We are a sovereign nation, and we have to regain our rights," Mr. Sahaf said.
"Trade has already started," he said, skirting the question of whether the commodities being exchanged directly violate Security Council controls.
"Nobody can isolate Iraq," he said. "It is a foolish policy. And the most foolish policy is the so-called dual containment," a reference to the Clinton Administration's policy of attempting to isolate both Iraq and Iran.
Mr. Sahaf said one development of special importance to Iraq was the thawing of relations with other Arab nations, and to some extent Iran, with which it fought an eight-year war in the 1980's. He said nearly every Middle Eastern country had joined in the movement to deliver help to the Iraqis for both humanitarian reasons and as a demonstration of opposition to American policy.

HEADLINE: Our Towns;
No Rush Here To Help Lure The Yankees

BYLINE: By EVELYN NIEVES 

DATELINE: FORT LEE, N.J.

 BODY:
IT'S rush hour in this on-off ramp of a town, and it is vicious.
The cars barreling off Interstate 95 just before the entrance to the George Washington Bridge are cutting each other off in a mad scramble for first place at the exit ramp stoplight. The light turns red, the cars keep going. Some head to the Palisades Interstate Parkway, others to the signs for "N.J. Highways," but everyone zips past the pedestrian, a middle-aged man, caught on the yellow lines. One block later, approaching the roads to Routes 80 and 95, a white Nissan rear-ends a black sport utility vehicle. Cars behind them pass in a huff, with angry jerks of the wheel. 
 Still, besides the relentless traffic, this town on the western ramp of one of the busiest bridges in the world, funnel to seven major highways, is rather subdued. The 6 o'clock chimes ringing through loudspeakers over the Fort Lee Methodist Church sound muffled, almost depressed, and several shops are shuttered for the night. Those that are open, like the Zion Kosher Deli, are catering to a handful of locals. The rush-hour cars, as always, are just passing through.
It has been like this since the bridge opened in 1931, and Fort Lee is beyond tired of it. Is it any wonder the town is leading the "Yankees Stay Home" movement? Already, 100 million cars cross the bridge every year, 136,000 cars each way each weekday, and a great deal of them try a short cut to the tolls through Main Street. And rush hour doesn't come just twice a day, five days a week. It comes every time the Giants play in the Meadowlands, every time construction cobbles a lane on the bridge, every time a holiday prompts the world to go see Mother.
So no sooner had a chunk of Yankee Stadium crashed down and the Mayor of New York started griping about the sky falling and the Yankees moving across the Hudson if a new stadium wasn't built, than Tom Meyers, a Fort Lee Councilman, started drafting a resolution:
Whereas, the Borough of Fort Lee urges the New Jersey State Legislature and Governor Christine Todd Whitman to refuse to entertain any overtures by the New York Yankees' organization to build a baseball stadium in New Jersey. . . .
The resolution passed Thursday, 6 to 0.
"We'd like to help the Yankees stay in the Bronx," said Mr. Meyers, a third-generation Fort Lee resident who grew up dodging cars. He remembers when the 2.5-square-mile borough still looked like a sweet little suburb with a big bridge attached, before the high rises turned Fort Lee into a crash pad for harried commuters.
He also remembers what the opening of Giants Stadium, in the Meadowlands, did for Fort Lee. It brought more cars. The borough, with 35,000 residents, has a police force of between 90 and 100, much larger than police departments in towns of similar size that don't have a hundred million traffic headaches. "We really can't afford any more cars," Mr. Meyers said.
The Fort Lee Borough Council plans to send its No Yankees resolution to Trenton, muster support from other municipalities, and see what happens.
But whatever Trenton says, the Yankees Stay Home movement seems to be catching on. Along Main Street here, residents and business owners interviewed all agreed that the Yankees belong in the Bronx, not in Manhattan, and definitely not in the Garden State. And it isn't just because of the traffic issue, which is huge just about everywhere in this most densely populated state in the nation. Or the fact that the economic boon stadiums are supposed to create doesn't seem to have hit home.
This is also a matter of pride. When the Giants crossed the river, the team was loath to call itself the New Jersey Giants. It's the New York Giants.
"The New Jersey Yankees doesn't sound the same," said Barry Peck, a transplanted Bronxite heading off a bus from Manhattan.
Besides, he added, New Jerseyans aren't stupid. "Steinbrenner is just using New Jersey as an excuse to try to get a better stadium in Manhattan," he said. "So, please, let's just blow the whistle on that right now."

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Military Group Finds Doors Closed for a Fair in Europe

BYLINE: By RAYMOND BONNER 

DATELINE: VIENNA, April 25

 BODY:
As if caught in a time warp from the Vietnam War era, a private, military-oriented American organization is having trouble finding a home for its European fair.
First, peace campaigners forced the fair out of Brussels, where it had been held for the last 18 years. Then the group found it was not welcome in The Hague. 
 So the organization, the Armed Forces Communications and Electronics Association, decided to gather in Brno, in the southeastern corner of the Czech Republic.
But even that plan may be in danger. The organization is being pursued by Reginald Moreels, director of the development assistance office in the Belgian Foreign Ministry. In a letter to the Czech President, Vaclav Havel, he wrote:
"You are known as an ethical person concerned about human rights issues and the well-being of the world population. Therefore, I dare ask you to use all of your influence to avoid the fair to take place in your country."
Mr. Havel, who has been hospitalized for two weeks, will respond when his health permits, a spokesman said.
The protests have stunned the American organization. "It amazes me," Maurice Padden, the association's vice president, said in a telephone interview from its headquarters in Fairfax, Va. "It reminds me of when I got back from Vietnam."
"We don't display weapons," said Mr. Padden, a retired Air Force general. "We don't sell anything at these fairs."
The group, founded in 1946, has 36,000 members, both individuals and companies.
It holds exhibitions and conferences around the world so that military officers, leaders of industry and the merely curious can view and discuss the latest developments in military communications, electronics and intelligence systems.
Mr. Padden said Dr. Moreels was politically motivated and acting on behalf of "pacifists."
Dr. Moreels, a founder of Doctors Without Borders-Belgium, the medical relief group, does not deny this.
"I have supported the various actions against this arms fair organized by the Belgian peace movement," he wrote the Dutch Minister of Development Cooperation, Jan Pronk, pleading that the the fair not be held in the Netherlands.
"The negative influence of the weapons trade in unstable and economically less developed regions is very clear," wrote Dr. Moreels, a surgeon who has worked with the civilian victims of war in many places, including Cambodia, Somalia and Yemen.
"Weapons that are easy to use and, thanks to technology, user-friendly weapons, reinforce the violence, in which more and more children are victims," he added.
The Hague municipality decided to cancel the fair, fearing demonstrators.
Whatever happens in Brno, Dr. Moreels is planning another conference for this fall, in the same building that the association would have used for its fair.
It will gather advocates of curbing the trade in small arms and light weapons like assault rifles and grenade launchers, which are the major causes of casualties in today's wars.
"We threw them out and put something in place that goes in the other direction," said Wouter Vermeulen, an assistant to Dr. Moreels.

E Pluribus Euro: The Next Big Step
Germany, France and nine other European countries are soon to surrender a central pillar of national sovereignty: control over their currencies. The first big step comes this week, when European leaders are to set the exchange rates at which currencies will be locked together. 1

The Federal Government is taking a new look at the terrorist threat of germ weapons. And officials are disturbed to find that the United States is not prepared for a devastating new type of biological terror. 1

The director, whose comments set off a controversy, was suspended for three days and ordered to develop a new sensitivity-training program for city employees. 19

Dick French Jr. is the Ted Turner of the Hudson Valley -- in four years, he has expanded Regional News Network, a 24-hour news operation based in Kingston, N.Y., from a station that broadcast to 250,000 homes upstate to one that reaches 2.4 million homes through cable systems in New York, New Jersey and Connecticut. Now he has gone to court to expand WRNN into New York City. 29
 
The Gotti Tapes
Movie-like dialogue is typical of the remarks captured on 7,000 surveillance tapes that are the heart of the racketeering case against John A.Gotti, sometimes called Junior, that prosecutors are preparing to bring to trial here. They say the son of the dapper Mafia leader who is in prison for life is the new Gambino boss. From what is known about the tapes, it is clear they include enough expletives, veiled threats and sarcasm to seem like a mob-movie parody. On the tapes, people actually use names like Tommy Sneakers. 34

HEADLINE: 11 NATIONS TAKING NEXT PIVOTAL STEP TOWARD THE EURO

BYLINE: By EDMUND L. ANDREWS 

DATELINE: FRANKFURT, April 25

 BODY:
Nearly 50 years after European leaders dreamed of uniting their nations to prevent another world war, 11 countries led by Germany and France are soon to surrender a central pillar of national sovereignty: control over their currencies.
Despite some remaining feuding and recrimination, leaders from all but 4 of the 15 nations in the European Union are expected to approve final plans next weekend for the introduction of a single European currency on Jan. 1. 
 Financial institutions and a few big companies will shift in 1999 to euro-based electronic transactions, although the actual bills and coins will not be in general use until 2002.
But in a real sense, the new order begins in Brussels next week, because European leaders are expected to spell out the exchange rates at which their individual currencies will be locked together. As a practical matter, their countries will immediately subordinate individual monetary policies in favor of a common European policy.
Most economists say the euro's long-term prospects are better than they dared imagine a few years ago. Inflation is low. Growth is accelerating across the Continent. And a world barometer for financial credibility, the currency markets, increasingly behave as if the euro were already a fact of life.
"The euro will be to the dollar what Airbus is to Boeing," said Norbert Walter, chief economist at Deutsche Bank Research. "We have better airplanes as a result of competition between Airbus and Boeing, and the euro will give us better reserve currencies."
Never before has a group of countries tried to introduce a common currency on such a scale without also forming a political union. Nor have political leaders worked together for so long to overcome historical divisions born of language barriers, cultural differences and war.
Yet with less than a week before European leaders reach their point of no return, the two most important nations are squabbling like jealous siblings. France and Germany are still fighting over who will run the new European central bank as well as about basic economic principles.
"There is still a difference in philosophy in France about the role of an independent central bank and about whether the central bank should look only at the question of price stability," said Otto Lambsdorff, a former German Economics Minister.
Huge gaps separate rich but sluggish economies like Germany's from poorer but booming countries like Spain and Ireland. Italy and Belgium continue to carry twice as much accumulated debt as most others and are obligated to make deep cuts in spending for years to come.
If the euro maintains credibility, it could transform Europe into one of the world's biggest and most efficient marketplaces. A failure would be an economic and political disaster.
For all the uncertainty about the new euro market, one thing seems clear: it will be huge. The cohesive Europe will have an economy almost as big as the United States' with combined annual output of $6.28 trillion, versus $8.1 trillion. It will be the world's biggest single exporter and importer. And it will be the center of a $2 trillion bond market, virtually equal to the market for United States Treasury securities.
And currency traders, who a year ago expressed their fears about the euro by fleeing from German marks to dollars, have remained calm as the final decisions have been made.
"We are convinced that the euro will seriously threaten the dollar as the world's reserve currency," said Avinash Persaud, a foreign exchange analyst at J. P. Morgan in London. "This process will be far quicker than many imagine, and it will be under way in a matter of months, not years."
Financial analysts predict that a unified European bond market will benefit from economies of scale and become a much stronger competitor to United States Treasury bonds, notes and bills.
"It will be a major market, and it will appear on the first day of business in January," said Graham Bishop, an adviser to Salomon Smith Barney in London. Indeed, governments and financial institutions are already preparing the structure for bond trading in euros.
Robert Portes, a professor at the London School of Economics, estimates that the rise of the euro could cost the American economy about $40 billion in benefits that derive from the dollar's role as world currency.
Most ordinary Americans are not likely to notice those effects, however. Compared with the normal and sometimes violent swings in exchange rates, which will still be a fact of life, the pressures created by the euro would be barely discernible. "The Deutsche mark is already a reserve currency, and every country in the world already uses it as a surrogate for Europe," said Stephen Bell, a senior economist with Deutsche Bank in London.
An unanswered question is whether the member nations can adhere to a one-size-fits-all monetary policy beyond the first few years. It is no accident that the euro is wildly popular in Spain and Italy, and grumpily opposed by more than 60 percent of all voters in Germany.
Thanks largely to the euro, long-term interest rates in Spain and Italy have plunged from more than 13 percent in 1991 to less than 6 percent today. Though part of the credit belongs to the central banks of the two countries, which imposed years of strict monetary policy to wring out inflation, the forthcoming reality of the euro played a major role.
Under the European currency union, interest rates in different countries must converge because the risk of the underlying currency would be no different in Spain or France or Germany.
Low rates have helped ignite an economic boom in Spain and Portugal and are expected to do it for Italy as well. Spain's economy is expected to grow as much as 3.7 percent this year, compared with about 2.5 percent in Germany. Spain has added 65,000 jobs in the last year, while Germany has lost 400,000. Such disparities present difficult problems.
Cesar Molinas, an economist in Madrid with Merrill Lynch, warned in a recent report that the euro could in fact create a boom-bust cycle in both Spain and Portugal. Under such a script, rising prices would make Spain's products uncompetitive and force an economic downturn.
At present, countries can protect their international competitiveness by devaluing their currencies to offset inflation. But that would be impossible under the single policy of a European central bank.
Italy faces hard problems, too. Like Spain and Portugal, it has received a windfall in the form of lower interest rates. But Italy also has one of the highest levels of Government debt in Europe, and under the Maastricht treaty of 1991 it must reduce that sharply. Prime Minister Romano Prodi will have to cut Government spending enough to start generating annual budget surpluses. But Mr. Prodi came close to losing control of his Parliament once last year, when he faced rebellion over proposed budget cuts.
The practical task of adapting to a single currency will be anything but cheap or simple. Daimler-Benz AG., the manufacturer of Mercedes-Benz cars and trucks, is spending $120 million to convert entirely to euro accounting by Jan. 1.
Company executives hope to save $60 million a year on currency transactions and on instruments to protect themselves from changes in exchange rates. Smaller companies, though, have barely begun to think about the conversion and are loath to face the added cost.
The euro is also expected to increase competitive pressure on companies in high-cost countries because they will no longer be able to conceal cross-border price differences easily. "I call it the Motel 6 effect," Mr. Bell of Deutsche Bank said. Just as it is hard to charge widely differing prices in the United States, it will be hard for companies to sell products across Europe with sharply different price tags. "It's likely to cost many companies a lot of money," Mr. Bell added.
"The French!" snapped Klaus Engelen, international correspondent at Handelsblatt, Germany's leading business newspaper. "Their strategy is one of containment -- they want to contain Germany."
Few Germans speak out as angrily as Mr. Engelen, but many share his fears. Less than a week before the Brussels meeting, France and Germany are battling over rival candidates to run the central bank. The French insist that the job should go to Jean-Claude Trichet, president of the Bank of France. The Germans, and most other governments, have long supported Wim Duisenberg, former head of the Dutch central bank.
The fight has little to do with personalities, since both candidates have locked their own monetary policies with those of the German central bank. But it has everything to do with a deep reservoir of suspicion that has bedeviled the euro project since it was envisioned.
German leaders insist that the European central bank should be independent of political pressure and focus exclusively on fighting inflation. French political leaders have argued that the bank should have some accountability to political leaders and place at least some priority on job creation. And they want a Frenchman simply because he is French.
"If the first act of European construction is a co-opting by central bankers, that means the single currency will start with a kind of coup d'etat," remarked Jacques Attali, a former adviser to the late French President, Francois Mitterrand.
Mr. Lambsdorff, who opposes concessions to the French, said: "I see a political threat from political intervention that would damage the independence of the central bank. The risk is that the countries with strong economies will be expected to transfer resources to those with weak economies."
Despite the months-long feuding and several failed attempts at compromise, financial analysts say the credibility of the future euro remains surprisingly strong. That is because the basic laws governing the European central bank and its priorities represent overwhelmingly German views. Even if Mr. Trichet is chosen to run the new bank, he has a record as a dogged ally of Bundesbank policies.
"The idea that the euro will be a weak currency is rubbish," Professor Portes said. "The central bank governors of Spain, Portugal, France and Italy are the toughest hawks in Europe. To get the idea that that they will suddenly turn into monetary lax-ists when they move to Frankfurt is bizarre."

He is Richard Portes of the London Business School, not Robert Portes of the London School of Economics.

HEADLINE: New Wave of Building for Shore Resort Near Seattle

BYLINE: By HARRIET KING 

DATELINE: OCEAN SHORES, Wash.

 BODY:
A NEW spurt of development is under way on this windswept six-mile peninsula a three-hour drive southwest of Seattle, whose beaches now draw 3.5 million visitors a year.
Since 1995, four new lodgings with 310 rooms have opened in this city with a permanent population of only 3,500. Now, with a $40 million infrastructure project due to get under way this year, construction of housing is expected to pick up steam. 
 The infrastructure funds will pay for a new sewage treatment plant, upgrades of the existing plant, extension of sewer lines to all existing lots and improvements to water and storm drainage systems.
At its current pace, officials say, there will be perhaps 120 housing starts this year, up from 104 in 1997 and 87 in 1995.
"The city has had more new housing starts than in all of Grays Harbor county combined in the last 12 years," said Ron Thomasson, past president of the Ocean Shores Development Association, adding that he projected 800 housing starts in the next five years.
Since 1992, shortly after the timber industry here went into a tailspin, the civic group has been a leader in promoting the investment of nearly $100 million in residential, hotel and commercial space.
Perched on the Pacific, the city is a major focus for construction on the coast, much of which is covered by national parks and rain forests, state parks and tribal land.
Despite the large number of visitors and the construction, there is often an air of tranquillity. Along the ocean, only sand dunes stand between homes and the surf. When the tide is out, the beach is pavement hard and motorists on what is officially known as Beach Highway pass strollers, whale watchers and horseback riders.
The peninsula had once been a cattle ranch. In 1960, developers bought the property and built roads, an airport, marina and an 18-hole P.G.A. golf course and platted 12,000 residential lots, on average 60- by 150 feet. They were sold for an average of $500.
Lots with views were plentiful along the ocean, the Grays Harbor jetty and bay, the golf course, and 23 interconnected freshwater canals and two lakes. About one third of the lots now have dwellings and the sewer extension will be to the remaining 8,000 lots without service. "For Sale" signs hammered into sand dune-covered lots indicate that parcels with 60 feet of oceanfront are available at about $70,000. New and remodeled primary and vacation homes cost from $75,000 to $350,000.
Larry Locke, owner of Locke Construction, a leading builder in Ocean Shores, said a waterfront home with as much as 3,000 square feet could be built for $350,000, including the lot. For a comparable waterfront home in Seattle, he added, "you're talking a million bucks."
When Mr. Locke moved his family and business to Ocean Shores 19 years ago, "things were bleak," he said. "The city was just getting off the ground," he recalled.
Ocean Shores was incorporated as a city by its 1,000 residents in 1970 after the original developer of the peninsula, MacPherson Realtors, who built the airport, marina and golf course, among other things, went bankrupt. A business district was forming then on two streets running parallel to the ocean, with a few motels perched near the surf. In 1983, a 23-unit time-share lodging called Windjammer Resort rose adjacent to the golf course. Housing, however, was still scarce.
BUT in the last five years, Ocean Shores has started to boom as a trendy place for vacation and permanent homes, especially as the economy improved and employment grew in the Seattle metropolitan area at Microsoft and Boeing.
Since 1992, more than 100 businesses have opened in construction, tourism and retailing, bringing the total number to over 500. Some residents work in nearby towns such as Aberdeen, the county seat. About half the people living here are retirees.
A couple of weeks ago Mr. Locke, who builds more than two dozen custom homes a year, finished a two-story oceanfront home with 1,600 square feet, three bedrooms and garage that he has sold for $185,000, including lot.
A number of unbuilt lots still remain on the golf course, where this spring he finished a 1,000-square-foot home on the fairway costing about $78,000, not including the $25,000 price of the lot.
A few blocks away in the sandy interior, 60- by 150-foot lots are priced as low as $7,000.
Many without sewer connections are currently used for recreational vehicle camping. "I have four houses on hold that we have to wait to build until the sewer comes in next year," said Mr. Locke.
The county seat, Aberdeen, is 20 miles away on Grays Harbor. But Tom Casey, a Grays Harbor County Commissioner, says "the executives the county recruits don't want to buy homes in Aberdeen, they want to commute from Ocean Shores over a nightmarish road."
When the Ocean Shores Development Association began a campaign in 1992 to lure new businesses to the city its initial thrust was hotels, says Mr. Thomasson, who manages the local cable-TV company and is a former economic development director for the city.
Existing motels and hotels were jammed in the summer and turning people away, he said, and lodging was heavily booked as well on winter weekends by those who wanted to see storms with 25 foot high waves rolling in from the ocean.
THE civic group first brought in the Best Western Lighthouse Suites with 76 rooms, followed by a 113-room Shilo Inn and a Holiday Inn Express with 72 rooms, with construction costs totaling well over $20-million. A $1 million, 46-room Days Inn motel opened last year, and Mr. Locke was the contractor.
The association now wants to bring in light industry and computer software companies. Commercial property is priced between $120 and $130 per square foot.
Residential values in Ocean Shores have been rising as its popularity spreads. Bonnie Reeves, who manages the Lighthouse Suites hotel, and her husband, William, bought a home here in 1975 when he retired from the Port of Tacoma "because we wanted peace and quiet and tranquillity." The assessed value has now gone up 300 percent.
"We just wanted a little home on the golf course," she said. "We never dreamed the place would grow."
Pat Kuntz, a sales agent at Windermere Real Estate, said the Oregon Coast was "saturated, so people are settling here."
"We are full-service, with events, conventions and we have supermarkets, pharmacies, a fire department and a police force," she said.
Last February, the Quinault Indian Nation broke ground just outside the city limits for a major casino that could cost more than $35 million and employ 300 people. And Quinault Land & Timber Company, another tribal business group, is looking for developers to build a resort costing in excess of $100 million on a 27-acre waterfront site on the southern end of Ocean Shores.
Recalling the problems of the original developers of Ocean Shores, Charles Donohoe, manager of the Windjammer Resort, said that they "were 20 years ahead of their time."

CORRECTION-DATE: May 3, 1998, Sunday

 CORRECTION:
An article last Sunday about development in Ocean Shores, Wash., misidentified a developer of the area that went bankrupt. That developer was Wendel West & Associates, which had purchased the property from the original developer, MacPherson Realtors. The article also misidentified the seat of Grays Harbor County. It is Montesano, not Aberdeen.

HEADLINE: Office Growth Finding Its Way To Rockland

BYLINE: By JOHN HOLUSHA 

 BODY:
MERCEDES-BENZ created something of a stir last fall when it announced it would move its North American corporate headquarters from Montvale, N.J., to a new 400,000-square-foot building on 91 acres of rolling, wooded land in Pearl River, N.Y., just four miles north.
Officials of Rockland County were also cheered that the development of Class A commercial office space, which has thrived to the south in New Jersey and to the east in Westchester and Fairfield Counties, was finally beginning to find its way there. According to the real estate company Cushman & Wakefield, about 100,000 square feet of Class A space was available in the county in the first quarter of 1998 compared to 250,000 square feet a year earlier, producing a 5.9 percent vacancy rate. 
 The car company would join other corporations, such as Fujitsu Network Communications and The Dress Barn, which have opened up operations in Rockland, and Novartis Pharmaceuticals and American Home Products, which have recently expanded existing operations. Another newcomer is the Palisades Center, a nearly 2-million-square-foot shopping center in the process of opening.
Although Mercedes-Benz purchased the land for the new building in December, it has since backed away from its plans to build the 400,000-square-foot building, saying that costs were proving higher than expected. Real estate sources said the company had told New York officials it was at least $25 million over its planned budget of $50 million.
A company spokesman said a decision was expected within 90 days whether to go ahead with a scaled-down project in Pearl River, stay in Montvale or do something else.
"The thought was that it is better to face the problems now, rather than wait until we were six months into construction," said Steven Rossi, general manager for corporate communications for Mercedes-Benz. "We would still love to make it work."
Development and government officials in Rockland say they still hope that Mercedes-Benz will make the Pearl River project work. But they say the decision by such a high-profile company to locate there, even if the actual project is foiled by budget issues, shows that the county has arrived as a corporate headquarters.
"There are several large-space users looking in Rockland County right now," said James B. Tully, a vice president of the CB Commercial real estate services company and head of its Northern New Jersey office. "Mercedes-Benz really kicked things into gear. It went through a lengthy selection process before deciding on that location. It is internal problems that are holding things up."
Jeffrey Babikian, a managing director of the Insignia/ESG brokerage company who specializes in New Jersey and Rockland, agreed that the Mercedes-Benz announcement, regardless of the eventual outcome, had people looking north of the New Jersey border. "The state line was some kind of a Chinese wall, but now that people are looking at Rockland County, they can see that there are no negatives to being there," he said. He added that several companies, which he declined to identify, were considering putting up headquarters in Rockland, but that the area was still not ready for speculative construction.
C. Scott Vanderhoef, the county executive, said much of the commercial activity recently involved filling vacant space, but said some specialty projects are in the pipeline. Last week, Database American Company, a business information firm, announced that it would lease a 200,000-square-foot building to be constructed on 26 acres of land in Montebello, starting in October 1999.
Both geography and history have contributed to the lack of commercial development in Rockland County as compared with other New York suburban areas.
It was largely a farming community until the Tappan Zee Bridge was opened in 1955; a mental hospital, the Rockland Psychiatric Center, was the largest single employer. After the bridge opened, the county became known as a haven for people willing to exchange a fairly punishing commute for housing that cost considerably less than what was available in closer-in or more accessible suburbs.
COMMERCIAL development was largely limited to light industrial and distribution operations and strip shopping centers. An attempt at Class A office development was undertaken in the 1970's by the Uris Organization, in hopes of luring the New York Stock Exchange from the then-beleaguered city. Blue Hill Plaza, the Uris development, is a 1.1 million-square-foot, two-building development near the Mercedes-Benz land in Pearl River. To appeal to the exchange, one building was built low and wide to accommodate a trading floor, while the other is high-rise and was intended for back-office operations of the member brokerage firms.
The stock exchange, of course, is still at Broad and Wall Streets, and Blue Hill Plaza remained thinly occupied for years, despite the modern office space and ample amenities that include a fitness center.
"This property had a long and troubled history," said Kenneth M. Krasnow, director of business development in the New York area for Cushman & Wakefield. It went through several owners, including Olympia & York, the American arm of the Canadian company that went bankrupt in 1992.
The development was subsequently purchased by Robert Martin, a Westchester builder and investor, and is currently owned by the Mack-Cali real estate investment trust, as a result of Mack-Cali's acquisition of Robert Martin. The property now has a vacancy rate in the single digits, brokers report, and is filled with tenants such as Bell Atlantic and Fujitsu Network Communications, a developer of fiber-optic telecommunications equipment.
High-tech companies like Fujitsu, and pharmaceutical companies like Novartis and American Home Products, are exactly the type of operations the county needs, said Richard M. Struck, director of business expansion for Orange & Rockland Utilities, the area's provider of electric power and gas.
"Obviously, we benefit from increased electrical and gas sales when new companies move to the county," he said. He said economic development in Rockland had been upbeat for the last few years and that 1998 appeared on track as well. According to the company, its economic development activities last year helped induce three companies to relocate to Rockland and two others to expand, adding over 200 jobs.
TO help lure new industry, the utility offers a 10 percent discount on power and gas to qualified companies. This is in addition to the incentives available from towns, the county and state.
These incentives can add up. Mercedes-Benz could collect as much as $9.4 million if it moves its 750 employees to Pearl River and hires 400 more, as had been planned. What makes the company so attractive to local officials is that the new location is so close to the old one, so that employees are not expected to move and no children will be added to a crowded school system.
In addition to Fujitsu, which invested $122 million to equip its 35,000-square-foot research and development center, which opened last year, Novartis Pharmaceuticals Corporation invested $165 million to expand its manufacturing operations at the old Ciba Pharmaceutical plant complex in Suffern.
Ciba, which was absorbed into Novartis with the merger of its parent Ciba-Geigy and Sandoz, has been manufacturing drugs at that location for 33 years. With the expansion, employment is expected to increase from 600 to 750 people.
Another relatively recent arrival is the headquarters of the 690-store Dress Barn clothing chain, which moved to Rockland from Stamford, Conn., in 1994. The company employs about 300 people in a 500,000-square-foot complex once occupied by AT&T in Suffern.
Rockland economic development officials say that as the smallest county in the state outside of New York City it tends to be overlooked.
"To the people upstate, we are part of the New York metropolitan area," said Holly Freedman, president of the Rockland Economic Development Corporation. "But in the city, they think we are upstate and too far away." In fact, the center of Rockland county is less than 25 miles from midtown Manhattan.
Some strip shopping centers and the 120-store, 900,000-square-foot Nanuet Mall remain but the retail focus of Rockland County has clearly shifted to Palisades Center, a mammoth shopping center just off the New York State Thruway two miles west of "the Tee Zee," which is how the Tappan Zee Bridge is referred to locally.
Situated on 170 acres, the center has close to 2 million square feet of store space and may expand to 3 million. Designed to be "all things to all people," according to one broker, the center has 118 stores in operation, ranging from Victoria's Secret to the J.C. Penney Home Store to Sunglass Hut.
There are 63 more in various stages of completion, with most due to open soon, including Home Depot, Target, Banana Republic and Barnes & Noble.
The center follows the current retail credo of mixing food and entertainment with shopping to attract families and keep them there as long as possible to boost sales. The entertainment at Palisades Center includes a 21-screen Loews Theater with a separate, large-screen Imax theater due to open at the end of July. A large indoor Ferris wheel operates at the end of one of the large food courts with multiple vendors; an ice rink is on the top floor of the four-level mall.
IN keeping with its scale, the mall has an industrial feel. The common areas have floors of sealed concrete and structural steel beams, with air ducts and electrical conduits in plain sight. There are no restrictions on Sunday shopping in Rockland County, so the mall will have an advantage on its competitors in Bergen County, which must close on Sundays.
Development of the mall has been highly contentious locally, and the recent opening came after well over a decade of wrangling. Most of the argument was over whether the new mall would generate unacceptable amounts of traffic on local roads and lure shoppers away from local stores. Many of those in opposition recalled how the opening of Nanuet Mall over 20 years ago hurt family stores in downtown Nyack.
The dispute appears to have generated strong feelings on both sides: the townspeople and local businesses who fear added traffic and competition, and the builder, Robert J. Congel of the Pyramid Companies, who ultimately got most of what he wanted.
Mr. Congel, who is based in Syracuse, announced recently that he was putting his privately-owned group of companies up for sale for estate planning purposes. On the block are 29 existing or approved malls throughout the Northeast with 28 million square feet of leasable space and the potential for adding 17 million more. Conspicuously not for sale: Palisades Center.
With its location in Clarkstown on the Thruway and state routes 303 and 59, Palisades Center is not hard to get to at most times. But transportation is an issue that comes up in most conversations about Rockland County.
INDEED, some real estate executives say the recent completion of Interstate 287 from New Jersey to the Thruway is a big part of the current interest in Rockland County locations. The highway provides a direct route between fast-growing areas like Morris County, in New Jersey, and Westchester and Connecticut.
"The completion of 287 combined with the decline in good sites in Westchester and New Jersey are the reason why multinationals are starting to look at Rockland," said Leonard T. Miller Jr., a director of Cushman & Wakefield in its New Jersey office.
Mercedes-Benz chose the Pearl River site because of its proximity to the existing headquarters; no one would have to relocate. But for Rockland commuters who work in Manhattan, commuting can be a daily struggle, marked by twice-a-day jams on the Tappan Zee Bridge as well as the entrances to the city.
Two New Jersey Transit rail lines, the Bergen and Pascack Valley, extend into parts of the county, but they go only as far as Hoboken. From there, passengers must switch to the PATH trains to get into Manhattan and seek other forms of transportation if they work a long distance north from the PATH terminus at 34th Street.
Proposals to ease the commuters' plight reveal a split, with development-minded officials and executives of the county's inland areas on one side of the issue, and residents of gentrified communities such as Nyack and Piermont along the banks of the Hudson River, who want to keep a less hurried way of life, on the other.
One proposal on the table is to run high-speed ferries from a dock in Nyack to Manhattan. Another is to operate water taxis across the river to Metro North train stations in Westchester. (There already is bus service across the bridge as well as buses that go through New Jersey to the city, but both have to contend with traffic.)
In Nyack, a village of Victorian homes and dozens of antiques shops, many residents fear that construction of a parking structure near the waterfront, as proposed by the Thruway Authority, would clog the narrow streets and drive away customers attracted by Nyack's quaint charms and river views.
Nevertheless, Mr. Vanderhoef, the county executive, said that some way had to be found to ease traffic congestion. "It is critically important to keep people off the Tappan Zee Bridge," he said. "We are very anxious to find new ways to move people, and a ferry from Rockland to Manhattan has great merit."
He said it was ironic that some residents of Piermont, just south of Nyack, are opposed to development, because for many years Piermont was a blue-collar factory town dominated by the Continental Can company. "The arty people don't want more tourism because of the parking, but the shop owners like it," he said.
A better solution for commuters, he said, would be to revive the passenger line along the west shore of the Hudson, which is now operated as a freight line by Conrail. "It was a one-seat ride that ran from West Kingston into the city," Mr. Vanderhoef said. "I remember my father taking it." But the Metropolitan Transportation Authority, he said, had been reluctant to undertake the project, arguing that it would be expensive and serve relatively few people compared to the lines on the eastern side of the river.
EVEN if Mercedes-Benz does not move to Rockland, the effort to attract the automaker will be a benefit, said Thom Kleiner, supervisor of the town of Orangetown, which includes Pearl River.
"We gained a lot of experience with three levels of government, town, county and state working with an international company," Mr. Kleiner said. He said the experience would be useful in seeking to attract companies that do not want to have to deal with different levels of government working at odds with each other.
Although Rockland switched to a county executive system in the 1980's, some of its governmental structure dates from the pre-Revolutionary era. There are no cities in the county. Instead there are five towns, Clarkstown, Haverstraw, Orangetown, Ramapo and Stony Point, with 19 villages within those towns. (There is even an Orangeburg within Orangetown.)
One of the biggest development opportunities in the county is the old mental hospital, much of which is a scenic but depressing vista of abandoned buildings with barred windows. The state has signaled its plan to sell 400 of the 600 acres, part of which fronts on Lake Tappan.
Mr. Kleiner said the town was interested in acquiring some of the land for use as a park and possibly a recreation center. "This land will not be developed for the highest economic return," he said. "That may be a close second, but it is important to retain part of it as a passive park."
One reason the town would welcome Mercedes-Benz as a neighbor, he said, was the company's demonstrated esthetic sense. "We know they won't chop down all the trees," he said.
Mr. Vanderhoef said that residential development in the county needed to be controlled to avoid overloading the school system. He said the county was working on its first comprehensive development plan in 25 years.
"This county has a history of growth with little planning," he said. "We have a solid infrastructure and a well-educated work force. Now we are going to see better planning."

PUB-DATE: April 26, 1998

SECTION: Section 1; Page 6; Column 1; Foreign Desk

LENGTH: 1909 words

HEADLINE: For Better or Worse, Israel Is 'Special' in U.S. Eyes

SERIES: ISRAEL AT 50: American Voices

BYLINE: By CAREY GOLDBERG with MARJORIE CONNELLY

 BODY:
Its image has been tarnished, its need for billions in aid questioned in Congress. But to most Americans, Israel remains a worthy recipient of support, and to almost half it is a "special place," rather than just another country, the latest nationwide New York Times poll shows.
American attitudes toward Israel have dipped and risen over its 50 years with the roll of events, but never by much. This month, 57 percent of 1,395 people polled across the country said they had a generally favorable opinion of Israel. 
 And for the first time in the 20 years that various pollsters have asked Americans which side in the Mideast conflict they favored, a strong majority, 58 percent, said they sympathized with Israel over the Palestinians -- although a plurality have always done so.
Moreover, despite Israel's modern face and the censure brought by its occupation of the West Bank and Gaza Strip, 49 percent still saw it as a "special place," while 27 percent said they did not and 18 percent said they had once but no longer did.
Joe Smith, 66, a retired telephone company service manager in Memphis, Tenn., is a Protestant. As he put it after the poll: "I'm not a person who believes Israel is O.K. no matter what they do. I just think we're obligated to support Israel with money or troops because of who they are. It's the Holy Land. It's the place that our Christianity is based on."
Among the 232 American Jews polled, support and concern for Israel approached double that of the general population on some issues. But Jews were also were likelier to favor a Palestinian homeland, apparently because they have delved more deeply into the issue, and fewer are still undecided on it.
Only 16 percent of respondents knew that Israel is the largest recipient of United States aid. It receives about $3 billion in economic and military assistance a year. But after hearing as part of another question that Israel was first on the aid list, 58 percent still favored maintaining the current level of support.
"They are a fairly small state," said Diana Appel, 37, a Clinton, Iowa, nurse who is a Protestant, "and they always seem to be under duress from the fact that everyone else wants their land."
Such widespread support indicates that political backing of Israel comes from much more than the influence of American Jews, Jewish advocates say -- even if the poll did find that 19 percent say Jewish Americans have too much influence on American foreign policy, and 30 percent say Israel has too much.
The telephone poll had a margin of sampling error of plus or minus three percentage points; it was conducted from April 15 to 20.
"The support in Congress is not simply the result of an effective lobby but is much more deeply rooted in public attitudes," said Rabbi Ismar Schorsch, chancellor of the Jewish Theological Seminary of America.
But some also pointed out that public opinion is itself a product of diligent opinion-forming efforts.
"There are an awful lot of people eager to insure that the relations between America and Israel remain a very strong one," said Jonathan Sarna, professor of American Jewish History at Brandeis University. "So in a sense, the poll is a tribute to the efforts of a lot of folks." He said such groups ranged from Jews to evangelical Christians.
When the topic of war arises, however, the broad support for Israel betrays a certain shallowness. If Israel seemed in danger of being defeated by Arab armies, only 22 percent would favor sending troops; 24 percent would favor sending arms and equipment only, and 44 percent would want to stay out of the conflict. In one of the starkest contrasts between Jews and the population at large, only 7 percent of Jews advocated not sending troops.
Support for Israel reached a low in 1989 during the heat of the intifada, the Palestinian uprising in the occupied territories. Then, only 45 percent of respondents viewed Israel favorably. Backing hit a high of 79 percent when Israel was the target of Iraqi missiles during the Persian Gulf war in 1991.
While support for Israel has swelled recently, it has remained consistently low for the Palestinians. Asked whether they sided more with Israel or the Palestinians, 58 percent chose Israel, up from 48 percent in 1997, while 13 percent in both years said they sided with the Palestinians.
In a somewhat surprising contrast, Jewish Americans backed the idea of a Palestinian homeland more heavily than non-Jews did. Part of that gap may stem from greater knowledge of the subject: asked whether they favored the idea of a homeland, 34 percent of the general public, as compared with 13 percent of Jews, said they did not know.
"The general American public has not had to think the issue through," said Arthur Hertzberg, a visiting professor at New York University. "The Jews have had to." Mr. Hertzberg is author of the forthcoming "Jews: The Essence and Character of a People" (Harper/Collins).
"Across the board of the Jewish world now," he said, "you have an almost even division between those who, having thought it through, think it's better to have a Palestinian state with safeguards and hopefully end the problem, and those who say, 'You can't trust those guys.' "
The poll found signs of frustration over the slow pace of the peace effort in the Middle East: only one-quarter of respondents said Benjamin Netan yahu had mostly kept Israel's peace agreements with the Palestinians; similarly, one-quarter of respondents said Yasir Arafat had kept the Palestinian side of the bargain.
In general, the sampled American Jews supported Israel at a rate about 20 percentage points above that of the general population. American Jews came close to unanimity on such questions as Israel's importance to the interests of the United States and its reliability as an ally.
Israeli Jews overwhelmingly return the affection they receive from America. A recent poll run in Israel by Public Opinion Research of Israel for the United States Information Agency shows that 87 percent of Israeli Jews describe United States-Israeli relations as good.
And relations with American Jewry remain healthy as well, despite bitter disputes recently over the power of the Orthodox rabbinate in Israel and its refusal to accept as equals rabbis of the Reform and Conservative movements, which predominate in the United States.
A poll last month for The Los Angeles Times and the daily Yedioth Aharonoth found that 79 percent of Israelis think American Jewish views should be taken into account in Israel's policies.
The disputes have taken a toll, however. The Los Angeles Times poll found that 43 percent of Israelis thought their relations with American Jews had deteriorated.
When the New York Times poll explored why Americans at large support Israel, it found a combination of religion, history and contemporary politics. Seventy-six percent said they believed the United States has a vital interest in Israel, while only 15 percent said it does not. A majority said they did not think Israel exaggerated the military threat from the Arab countries.
Sixty-four percent agreed with the following statement: "Because of what happened to the Jews during World War II, Israelis are right to defend their homeland at any cost." Older people were more likely to agree than younger -- 76 percent of those over 64, compared with 66 percent of those 30 and under.
"I see the Jewish society as a whole as people who have overcome huge obstacles," said Sharon Barnes, 30, a Grand Rapids, Mich., saleswoman who is Protestant. "I don't think you can put into words what they went through during the war."
Israel, she said, "is a special place as a homeland for the Jewish people as well as the birthplace of Jesus Christ."
For all their emphasis on the past, however, it is more recent times that Americans think of first. Asked what the word Israel brought to mind, 26 percent -- the largest group -- said war. The next largest, 13 percent, mentioned the Holy Land. American Jews were most likely to describe Israel first as the Jewish homeland and then in the context of war.
Arthur S. Berger, spokesman for the American Jewish Committee, said, "Americans are aware that here is a country in many respects like the United States, also based on democratic principles, and it still doesn't have peace."
In some ways, said Mr. Hertzberg, the N.Y.U. professor, the relationship between Israel and the United States is "a marriage 50 years later."
"And in a marriage 50 years later," he added, "there are no longer violent transports of emotion, but you have a history together and you will continue.
"A long marriage works when all the things that got you married have receded into the background, and what keeps you going is a sense of shared history, knowledge of each other's foibles, of shared or unshared annoyance. It's what Henry Higgins says to Eliza: 'I've grown accustomed to your face.' "
 
The Series So Far
These articles focus on facets of Israel as it nears the anniversary of its independence, observed this year on April 30. The series, which began on April 6, has examined the country's social diversity; the controversy surrounding a history television series; the transformation of the economy, and the Palestinians.
 
On The Web
 
The complete "Israel at 50" series and a historical look at Israel's statehood from The New York Times's archives are available on the Web:
www.nytimes.com/library/world/israel-index.html
 
How the Poll Was Conducted
The latest New York Times Poll is based on telephone interviews conducted April 15 through 20 with 1,395 adults throughout the United States.
The sample of telephone exchanges called was randomly selected by a computer from a complete list of more than 42,000 active residential exchanges.
Within each exchange, random digits were added to form a complete telephone number, thus permitting access to both listed and unlisted numbers. Within each household, one adult was designated by a random procedure to be the respondent.
The results have been weighted to take account of household size and number of telephone lines into the residence and to adjust for variations in the sample relating to geographic region, sex, race, age and education. In theory, in 19 cases out of 20 the results based on such samples will differ by no more than three percentage points in either direction from what would have been obtained by seeking out all American adults.
To obtain sufficient numbers of Jewish Americans for analysis, callbacks were placed to every household in which a respondent had identified his or her religious preference as Jewish in Times/CBS Polls conducted during the past two years. Additional calls were also placed using random numbers within the same telephone exchanges as those respondents. The total number of interviews completed with Jewish Americans was 232. These respondents were then weighted down to the usual proportion of Jewish Americans in the poll.
For subgroups of the population the potential sampling error is larger. For example, for Jewish respondents taken as a group it is plus or minus seven percentage points. In addition to sampling error, the practical difficulties of conducting any survey of public opinion may introduce other sources of error into the poll. Variations in question wording or the order of questions, for instance, can lead to somewhat different results.

Correction Appended

SECTION: Section 7; Page 32; Column 2; Book Review Desk 

LENGTH: 2026 words

HEADLINE: Unter den Linden

BYLINE: By Peter Paret; Peter Paret, a professor emeritus of history at the Institute for Advanced Study, is the author, most recently, of "Imagined Battles: Reflections of War in European Art."

 BODY:

FAUST'S METROPOLIS
 A History of Berlin.
 By Alexandra Richie.
 Illustrated. 1,139 pp. New York:
 Carroll & Graf Publishers. $37.95.
 
 BERLIN AND ITS CULTURE
 A Historical Portrait.
 By Ronald Taylor.
 Illustrated. 416 pp. New Haven:
 Yale University Press. $45. 
 For dramatic effect, a city's history is sometimes called a biography, but cities are living organisms only by courtesy. The individuals, social and professional groups and institutions that make them up are not easily treated as one. Generalizations may reveal truths beyond the specific fact, but in the process the fact itself may become blurred. Some writers try to neutralize the drawbacks of generalizing by personifying the city around a central trait: New York is brash, Paris is romantic, Berlin is -- or was -- militaristic. Like national character, however, the personality of a city is a doubtful explanatory device. If the trait has any substance, it needs to be explained.
Cities not only are amorphous, they may also experience extreme discontinuities in their history. Much of Berlin was destroyed in World War II, after which it was divided for decades. At times during these years it seemed that whatever unity the place possessed consisted of little more than its name.
Still, in one form or another Berlin has existed at least since the 13th century -- a further challenge to its interpreters. Not everyone can feel the same interest in such diverse topics as medieval trade policies and the municipal budget for the arts in the 1920's or 80's.
To mention one last problem: The question of what and what not to include, common to all historical writing, may be particularly troublesome in accounts of cities that are capitals of their countries. Because events elsewhere constantly affect this aspect of the city, historians may be tempted to make excursions into national history rather than remain focused on the subject.
In "Faust's Metropolis" Alexandra Richie surmounts some of these obstacles and stumbles over others. She has written a very long, pear-shaped book. About 165 pages of text and notes deal with the first 600 years of Berlin. Slightly more than 200 pages cover the next 70 years or so, and nearly 700 pages, almost two-thirds of the book, the years from the Weimar Republic to reunification. This allotment may reflect the interests of the author, who knows a great deal about Berlin's recent past. But it is more difficult to write a good concise account of hundreds of years than an expansive study of a much briefer period, and with a few exceptions -- for example, a fine discussion of the deplorable working-class housing built in Berlin from the 1850's on -- the first third of her book is not very good history.
For one thing, it is out of balance. The horrors of the Thirty Years' War are treated at length and somewhat repetitively, but an event of great significance in Berlin's history, the acquisition by the Hohenzollern dynasty of the Duchy of Prussia at the beginning of the war, goes unmentioned. Several paragraphs are devoted to a confusing discussion of the Prussian or German Army -- it is not made clear which -- under Emperor William II, but nothing is said about the Berlin garrison at the time, its strength, its impact on the population and the economy, its political functions, its links with the police. The interpretations of some of the Hohenzollern rulers border on caricature. Generalizations and stereotypes abound.
An example is the book's treatment of Berlin under the empire. It is shaped by two ideas: The Hohenzollern system was authoritarian, militaristic and class-ridden; and the Berlin middle classes were raw upstarts, boastful, arrogant to outsiders and subservient to authority. These traits were certainly present and probably grew stronger in the years before 1914. But they coexisted with other, healthier forces, not only among the general public -- a large part of which, whether socialist, liberal or conservative, wanted neither war abroad nor ideological strife at home -- but also in the city administration, the press and the Prussian bureaucracy, which often, if not often enough, kept even the Kaiser in check. It was these elements that gave the city much of its energy, creativity and economic and cultural sophistication. It is no criticism of the city to say that this was often the work of newcomers, who were attracted by opportunities in Berlin, as elsewhere people sought careers in London or Paris, and very likely a systematic comparison with other capitals is needed to gain a balanced picture of Berlin's strengths and defects. Setting Berlin next to London would, for instance, show that although William II blustered against the modern stage while his nephew King Edward VII maintained a discreet silence, Berliners could see modern plays that the Lord Chamberlain kept from Londoners. That the aggressive nationalism and racism infecting parts of German society grew to terrible proportions during and after World War I, and ended by destroying much of Europe, does not eliminate the positive aspects of Berlin before 1914, which any account of the city's past should analyze and place in context.
Factual errors are to be expected in a work of this size and sweep. But their number in the early chapters seems rather large. Especially in a book that levels much criticism at the privileges of the nobility under the empire, Gen. Erich von Ludendorff and Gen. Wilhelm von Groner should not be called nobles, nor was Ludendorff a field marshal. Ernst Arndt was not Brentano's co-editor of the collection of folk songs "Des Knaben Wunderhorn." Justus Moser was not a conservative critic of late-l9th-century Berlin; he died in 1794. Ernst Barlach and Max Klinger, both major figures in modern German art, did not serve "at the front" in World War I. Adolf von Menzel's painting "Traveling Through the Countryside" (1892) is not "one of the first pictures to depict rail travel," but his "Berlin-Potsdam Railway" (1847) is. And so forth. Historical writing is a dialogue between large themes and the details that constitute and illustrate them, and here the dialogue too often breaks down.
With the end of World War I, the book takes a decided turn for the better. The author has a good sense of the social, political and cultural forces at work in Berlin in the 1920's and under Hitler, and her perspective deepens.
She continues to take a critical view of her subject, not only of the Berliners' attitudes and politics, but also of the legends that have developed around these years. The cultural efflorescence of the 1920's was certainly remarkable; but, as she points out, much of it had its roots in the empire, and it affected only a small part of Berlin: "Even when avant-garde culture was at its height the majority of Berliners were ignorant of it, even frightened by it." Richie is equally critical of the ideological naivete of many of the artists and intellectuals of the time, and even more so of the Communists and their destructive politics. That in November 1932, in the last free elections held before the Nazis took power, only 30 percent of the Berlin electorate voted for Hitler she rightly considers less significant than the fact that with the Communist and right-wing nationalist vote, 70 percent of the voters supported enemies of the republic -- not a firm basis for opposition to the Nazis, either in 1933 or later, when the nature of the Third Reich should have been apparent to all.
The Nazi years and their pathetic climax, the Battle of Berlin, are not so much analyzed as dramatically described. It seems excessive to condemn President Franklin D. Roosevelt's order to halt American forces on the Elbe as "criminally stupid." But the account of the Russian occupation and the beginning of the cold war is good, as are the chapters on the divided city. Here the author is at her best, perhaps because she has lived in both East and West Berlin, and her treatment of the politics and ways of life on the two sides of the wall is sharpened by personal experience. Characteristic are shrewd discussions of the physical and psychological isolation of West Berlin, for decades a pampered outpost of the Western alliance, and of the encompassing corruption in the East, which pushed millions of East Germans into yet another morass of lies, denunciations and persecution, and created one more difficult past for Berliners to overcome.
In Richie's account of Berlin over the centuries, culture is one strand among many. For Ronald Taylor in "Berlin and Its Culture," the city's "cultural spirit" and activity since the Middle Ages form the principal theme. Taylor takes a rather narrow view of his subject. He largely ignores popular culture and even his view of high culture is somewhat restricted. He says almost nothing, for instance, about the historians of the University of Berlin in the 19th and early 20th century, whose writings strongly influenced German thought and art and were themselves works of high literary quality, companions to novels and drama on the bookshelves of the educated public. His book does include a brief discussion of the Berlin film industry; but it centers on the same few works -- from "The Cabinet of Dr. Caligari" to "Metropolis" and "The Blue Angel" -- that appear in every history of the period, while the far larger output of popular love stories and comedies, with their important social and political implications, goes unnoticed. Essentially "Berlin and Its Culture" is limited to the art, architecture, literature and music for upper-class patrons and a middle-class audience.
The result is neither a comprehensive guide nor an analytic interpretation, but has features of both. Some subjects are treated at length; others, often of comparable importance, are summarized or barely mentioned. When the author allows himself space to discuss a particular artist or stylistic development he can be informative and interesting. The 100 pages on the culture of Berlin since 1945 are especially good. Short essays on major figures are scattered through the text; usually they present a brief general account and a more detailed discussion of one or two major achievements. This works well enough, especially with novelists and dramatists.
But often Taylor proceeds differently. His approach then resembles that of a tour guide through the museum of the city's culture, who identifies an object, perhaps briefly characterizes it, and moves to the next. If nothing else, the reader is given an overview; but extreme brevity leads to ambiguity, and even inaccuracies. Much ground is covered with little more than lists of names, the curse of the textbook, and it is almost axiomatic that these lists contain mistakes.
It is the strength of "Berlin and Its Culture" that it brings together many subjects that are usually discussed separately; its weakness that despite good sections it isn't more consistent and it remains a chronicle rather than a comprehensive interpretation. Certainly Taylor has not been able to identify the "cultural spirit" that he mentions at the beginning of his work.
But most likely that spirit varied from one to the other of the city's many lives. The sparse, cool, ironic, critical sense that marked some of Berlin's greatest cultural achievements in the 19th century surely was replaced by very different traits before World War I, and by others again during the Third Reich, with only a trace of former styles touching a few receptive minds in later years. Today, as the city is in a slow transition to become the capital of Germany once again, a still different cultural spirit will emerge, together with new social and political attitudes. How Berliners deal with the city's fragmented past, especially how they come to terms with the behavior and experiences of the immediately preceding generations, will help shape these attitudes. Alexandra Richie's and Ronald Taylor's books, though they do not succeed in everything they set out to do, at least indicate, each in its own way, the riches, disasters and challenges that are part of Berlin's past.
