HEADLINE: PATAKI USES VETO TO CUT 1,000 ITEMS FROM 1998 BUDGET

BYLINE: By RICHARD PEREZ-PENA 

DATELINE: ALBANY, April 26

 BODY:
In his first use of the power to veto individual items in the state budget, Gov. George E. Pataki made a broad assault today on the budget drafted by the Legislature, cutting $760 million in spending this year and more than $800 million in borrowing for future programs.
Mr. Pataki, who is running for re-election, eliminated one of the Legislature's most cherished initiatives, $500 million in school construction and repair money, a top priority for both Sheldon Silver, the Democratic Assembly Speaker, and Rudy Crew, the New York City Schools Chancellor, whose system is troubled by overcrowding and crumbling buildings. He also vetoed more than $100 million in education spending, most of it earmarked for New York City, for teacher training and to enhance teachers' salaries. 
 The so-called line-item vetoes apparently conclude the budget-making process, since it appears unlikely that the Legislature will be able to summon the two-thirds vote in both houses needed to override a veto.
But after saying for weeks that the Senate would not even try to override, the Senate majority leader, Joseph L. Bruno, a close ally of the Governor, took a different position today, saying the Senate would review the vetoes "and we will make our judgments accordingly."
In all, the Governor said he had vetoed more than 1,000 separate spending items, in a sweeping reassertion of his authority over the budget after the Assembly and Senate, for the first time in history, drafted a budget through conference committees without the Governor present.
Calling the legislative budget "an election-year spending binge," Mr. Pataki reduced its size to $71.4 billion, which still represents a 6.9 percent increase from last year, the largest rise in a decade.
The scope of the vetoes left legislators of both parties stunned and angry. Only a few weeks ago, many had predicted that Mr. Pataki would let most of the budget stand because he would not want to alienate voters in an election year. Instead, he appears to have chosen to demonstrate his fiscal conservatism.
Mr. Pataki said he understood that many of his vetoes would be politically unpopular. "Obviously, in an election year, I would have liked to have not vetoed these," he said at a news conference this afternoon.
But aides to the Governor say he understands just as clearly that he needs to enhance his credentials as a tough conservative to have a chance at becoming his party's nominee for President or Vice President in 2000. They say they have little concern about his prospects this year, with Mr. Pataki leading all his challengers in the polls by a wide margin, and in fund-raising by $10 million.
The Governor's actions are certain to worsen his already rocky relations with the Legislature, potentially making it harder for him to win passage of other initiatives, like charter schools and tougher criminal penalties for juveniles. "I think that certainly the relationship is going to be tense," Mr. Bruno said.
Mr. Silver said the vetoes "display an astonishing arrogance of power," and called Mr. Pataki "a captive of the radical right."
Mr. Silver would not discuss the $500 million in school construction money specifically, but several of his Democratic colleagues said they expected the Speaker to file a lawsuit challenging the way the veto was carried out.
The Governor cut $36 million the Legislature had added for the State University and City University, including money to hire hundreds of professors and finance a $65-a-student textbook credit. He vetoed $45 million in environmental programs, including money to clean up hazardous waste sites and to create a state map showing cancer rates.
There were vetoes in mental health spending, vetoes in programs for people with H.I.V., even a veto of money to hire 100 new correction officers, a position that Mr. Pataki has traditionally been kind to.
He eliminated hundreds of items, worth a total of $158 million, in the long list of pet local projects that legislators had inserted into the budget, including $7 million to increase purses at the Saratoga race track, a favorite project of Mr. Bruno, whose district includes Saratoga.
But Mr. Pataki did not veto any of the $743 million a year in tax cuts that the Legislature passed.
The Governor's vetoes were especially striking because he began the year by proposing a 7.6 percent election-year spending increase, to $71.9 billion in the fiscal year that began April 1. That plan drew harsh criticism from conservatives, independent fiscal monitors and some of the Democrats running against him, and led Mr. Pataki to reduce the size of his own proposal even before it reached the Legislature. With his vetoes, he insured that the budget put into law would be smaller than his original plan, something that had not happened since Nelson A. Rockefeller was Governor.
Today, Mr. Pataki portrayed himself as holding the line against a profligate Legislature. "We have got to have budgets that are soundly balanced and that meet the needs of the future," he said.
The Legislature's public budget wrangling this year gave the Governor less influence over the details of the plan than in years past, when he, Mr. Silver and Mr. Bruno negotiated a deal in secret. But it also freed him to veto whatever he wanted, a luxury he did not have when the budgets passed by the Legislature were the result of compromises he had helped draft.
Mr. Pataki's predecessor, Mario M. Cuomo, used the line-item veto sparingly in his 12 years in office. No governor's veto of any kind has been overridden by the Legislature since 1981, when Hugh L. Carey held the office.
Mr. Bruno said the vetoes were embarrassing, and potentially politically damaging, to legislators who had claimed credit for spending items and who are also running for re-election this year. "Here, in many instances, we've announced things and now those announcements are not valid," he said.
Even if Mr. Bruno allowed a motion to override the vetoes to come to a vote, several Republican Senators said today that the motion could not get the necessary two-thirds vote. And in the Assembly, the Democrats are three short of a two-thirds majority, and the Republican minority is expected to be in lockstep with the Governor.
Despite the vetoes, the budget grew at a faster clip this year than at any time since before the 1990-91 recession. It increases aid to school districts by $850 million, to almost $12 billion.
But Mr. Pataki appears to have succeeded in eliminating a handful of school programs that have long been among his favorite targets. The largest, called teacher support aid, provides $77 million a year so that urban school districts, which pay smaller wages than suburban systems and have a hard time hiring enough teachers, can increase teachers' salaries. More than 80 percent of that money goes to New York City.
Randi Weingarten, president of the United Federation of Teachers, called the cuts "inexcusable," and said, "As far as we're concerned, the fight for these programs will now intensify."
Mr. Silver's proposal to add $500 million in construction money came after voters rejected a $2.4 billion bond act for school building and repair last November. The Governor said he vetoed the measure because the voters had spoken on the subject, because the budget already included enough money for school building, and because most of the money would have had to be borrowed.
 
Items Range From Colleges to Prisons
By The Associated Press
Here is a sampling of items from the new state budget that were vetoed yesterday by Gov. George E. Pataki:
*A $500 million school construction fund that would have been largely financed by borrowing.
*$158 million in pet projects added by legislators.
*$1 million to help create the nation's first statewide cancer map.
*Language that the Governor said could have prevented the state from spending $180 million to build a maximum-security prison in the Finger Lakes area. He also vetoed $10 million for a recreation facility at the maximum-security prison being built in Franklin County.
*An $11 million plan to increase state revenue sharing to localities by 2 percent.
*$90 million in additional bonding for local highway construction.
*$4.6 million for upstate transit systems.
*$77.5 million in teacher support aid.
*Money to provide students at the State University of New York and the City University of New York with $65 each in aid annually for textbook purchases.
*$5.4 million for new faculty members at CUNY.
*$2.5 million for new faculty members at SUNY.
*A $13 million increase in base aid for SUNY's community colleges.
*$3.1 million for rehabilitation work on the State Capitol and other nearby buildings.
*$5.8 million for 100 new prison guards and sergeants.
*$5.25 million for local AIDS and H.I.V. programs.
*$17.2 million for a 2.5 percent cost of living adjustment for employees of nonprofit providers in mental health and mental retardation.
*$4.08 million in extra aid for arts programs.
*$100,000 for the Syracuse Symphony.
*$125,000 for the Westchester Arts Council.
*$50,000 for the Rochester Philharmonic.
Source: State Division of the Budget.

 CORRECTION:
An article on Monday about Gov. George E. Pataki's use of his line-item veto power to make budget cuts misstated the last year the New York State Legislature overrode a governor's veto. It was 1996, not 1981.

HEADLINE: LABOR V. LABOR: A special report.;
Garment Union Battles Its Own Workers

BYLINE: By DIANA B. HENRIQUES 

 BODY:
As recently as five years ago, the sprawling Brooklyn plant operated by Mademoiselle Knitwear Inc. was a showcase -- clean, air-conditioned, with a cafeteria for workers and a day-care center for their children.
Nearly 780 people tended the computer-guided knitting machines and the sewing and pressing stations, turning out sweaters for the prestigious Liz Claiborne label. Union leaders cited the plant as proof of what management and labor could achieve together. And the workers, mostly Chinese and Hispanic immigrants, rejoiced to be out of sweatshops where weeks elapsed without a promised paycheck. 
 Now, the rainbow piles of Liz Claiborne sweaters are gone -- most are being made in cheaper nonunion factories abroad. A few orders from other labels barely keep 175 workers busy, and Mademoiselle is in bankruptcy.
Mademoiselle is hardly unique in the struggling domestic garment industry, where the number of union jobs fell by 54 percent between 1989 and 1996. But its workers are fighting back. They have sued not only Claiborne, one of the nation's largest clothing manufacturers, but their union, saying that it stopped fighting on their behalf in return for a multimillion-dollar payment.
The suit amounts to the first serious legal challenge to a decades-old system approved by Congress that allows unions to penalize garment makers for shipping jobs abroad. But instead of protecting jobs, critics contend, the system is accelerating their loss, providing a way for garment makers to cut labor costs without risking strikes while putting money into union pockets even as membership dues dwindle.
If successful, the workers' case could change the dynamics of labor relations throughout the garment business, forcing the Union of Needletrades, Industrial and Textile Employees (Unite), the successor to the International Ladies Garment Workers Union, to take a harder line.
But the highly complicated, years-long fight could also have broad implications for other industries, where unions are struggling to get a foothold.
"It just reinforces the negative public image that is one of the biggest obstacles to labor's efforts to unionize white-collar fields like technology and health care," said Charles B. Craver, the author of "Can Unions Survive?" and a law professor at George Washington University.
The workers at Mademoiselle contend that Unite, the key union in the industry, betrayed them last year by accepting a $750,000 settlement of a promising $30 million arbitration claim that it had filed against Claiborne, which now orders most of its sweaters from Britain, Australia and the Far East. The settlement came to just less than $2,900 for each eligible worker. In return, the workers contend, Claiborne signed an agreement that gave the union $13 million up front, with $7.5 million more likely to be paid over the next three years.
Both Unite and Claiborne insist that the payment to the union was both legal and routine, representing money that Claiborne owed for violating a contractual promise to order only from unionized factories. That promise, and the damages paid, covered all orders that Claiborne placed in nonunion shops, not just those withdrawn from Mademoiselle. In the industry, such payments are called "liquidated damages," and they are negotiated during contract talks with manufacturers every three years.
As for the arbitration settlement, the union said that it had done the best it could for the Mademoiselle workers. And both the union and Claiborne insist that there was no quid pro quo -- the settlement for the workers and the payment to the union, they say, were unrelated matters that were negotiated separately.
 
'Union-Imposed Tariff'
Industry Payments Called a Conflict
The use of liquidated damages dates back to the 1950's. At that time, a new Federal law forbade unions to require employers to do business only with other unionized companies. But Congress exempted the garment industry from the rule, concluding that organizing efforts would be impossible otherwise because designers often farm out their work to small, scattered subcontractors.
Thus, Unite legally can, and does, demand in its collective bargaining agreements that designers use only unionized subcontractors like Mademoiselle. Claiborne and other manufacturers that violate the agreement are liable to the union for liquidated damages based on the volume of nonunion work. Several labor economists described the payments as "a union-imposed tariff" aimed at discouraging garment industry imports.
But Unite's practice of accepting such payments -- rather than fighting imports through arbitration or on the picket line -- has helped fatten industry profits and lower management risks, critics say. Using cheap factories abroad can save manufacturers far more than they pay in damages, and the payments spare them the possibility of costly labor battles at home.
Unite says collecting such damages is essential to its mission. The practice has generated millions in annual revenue that Unite, which represents 300,000 workers, uses to pay for its organizing efforts, administrative overhead, retraining programs and political lobbying. Between 1994 and 1996, the union collected $25.3 million in liquidated damages, about half of that from Claiborne. One union official said such payments had typically averaged about $10 million a year, or 15 percent to 20 percent of Unite's annual budget, which is also supported by "significant investment income."
"Liquidated damages are used to defend fair labor standards in the industry," said Brent Garren, the union's associate general counsel. "That is the only way to counter the harm created by sending work to nonunion shops."
Howard I. Rhine, the lawyer for the workers, disagreed. Hundreds of jobs were lost at Mademoiselle, Mr. Rhine said. "Even if they say that the money was used for the greater good, that's not a defense for the way our clients were treated," he added. He hopes to have the workers' lawsuit approved as a class action on behalf of all the current and former Mademoiselle workers who were affected by the loss of Claiborne work. The suit seeks to have the $20.5 million in liquidated damages, or the estimated $30 million value of the lost Claiborne work, put in a trust fund for the benefit of the displaced Mademoiselle workers. The case is pending in Federal District Court in Manhattan.
In essence, the suit says that the damages system creates a conflict of interest for the union because it generates money for Unite even though its members' jobs may have vanished.
The workers are not alone in scrutinizing the dispute. Federal prosecutors in Manhattan are checking whether any violations of Federal law arose during this tangled battle. And a House panel is concerned that liquidated damages may allow the union "to gather great profits for itself while providing little or no service to its members," said Representative Pete Hoekstra, Republican of Michigan, who is head of the oversight panel of the House Committee on Education and the Workforce.
 
Trading Accusations
Claiborne Files A Trademark Suit
As the anger escalates, so have the stakes. Claiborne, for one, has much to lose. Despite its heavy reliance on imports, the company says it strives for fair labor practices -- indeed, Roberta S. Karp, its general counsel and a key player in this fight, is co-chairman of the industry council set up by President Clinton to improve foreign sweatshops used by American designers.
But Claiborne faces some stinging accusations relating to its conduct at various stages of the Mademoiselle dispute. In court documents, Mademoiselle contends that Claiborne executives testified falsely in union arbitration hearings. It also charges that the clothing giant wrongly accused Mademoiselle of trademark infringement and then conspired with union leaders to scuttle an adverse arbitration ruling. Claiborne's goal was to get out of its expensive, union-enforced manufacturing deal with Mademoiselle, Mademoiselle's lawyers say.
Ms. Karp denied those allegations. Any inconsistencies in Claiborne testimony or documentary evidence were minor and inadvertent, she said, and the payment to the union was entirely unrelated to the arbitration settlement.
The trademark infringement case, which could destroy Mademoiselle and render the workers' fight futile, is "very strong," she said. That case involves allegations that Mademoiselle sold excess Claiborne goods without permission.
But she acknowledged that Claiborne offered in September 1996 to withdraw the trademark case if Mademoiselle would simply let Claiborne out of its production commitment and promise not to sue for malicious prosecution. Harley Lewin, a company lawyer, also confirmed that Claiborne made another settlement offer in late 1997, one that involved a cash payment by Claiborne. Mademoiselle insists that it fully complied with Claiborne's resale policies. The case is awaiting a decision from Federal District Judge Robert Sweet, who is hearing the matter without a jury.
Shraga E. Newhouse, Mademoiselle's combative president, says the accusation was fabricated by Claiborne to shed its obligations to Mademoiselle and its employees. He further contends that Unite, once his ally, has sold out for money that by rights should go to his employees.
For his part, Mr. Garren, the union's associate general counsel, says that Mr. Newhouse "is a scoundrel and that's why we're in a fight with him." But the fight has already drawn the attention of garment makers on the West Coast, who are likely to use it against Unite in its dogged effort to unionize workers there.
Ultimately, the people with the most at stake are the Mademoiselle workers, who have defied their union to file a lawsuit that raises some untested theories of labor law.
Chun Hua Mui, a native of China who was hired at Mademoiselle in 1986, was outraged that the union made the workers promise not to sue Unite before they could share in the $750,000 severance fund. Mr. Garren, the union's lawyer, said the demand was routine. Ms. Mui and about four dozen others refused and were denied their share.
The settlement covered any current or former union members affected by the loss of work. But Mr. Rhine, the workers' lawyer, said that the union had set eligibility standards that limited payment to just 260 employees, most of whom are still on Mademoiselle's payroll.
Josefa Gantes, a dignified middle-aged woman born in Spain, paused over her sewing machine to explain why she has joined this fight. "This time it was us," Mrs. Gantes said softly. "But next time, it may be someone else."
 
Roots of Dispute
Factory Expands, But Alliance Sours
The roots of the dispute go back to 1992, when Mademoiselle was operating in bankruptcy because of cost overruns on its new factory in Brooklyn's Bushwick section. That year, Mr. Newhouse said, Claiborne said it would order sweaters from him if he would invest in the improved machines and larger work force that Claiborne required and if his workers would join Unite's Local 155.
In sworn testimony, Mr. Newhouse and a union official both said the designer had promised a separate Unite local, 23-25, which represented Claiborne's union workers, that it would order 1.4 million sweaters a year from Mademoiselle for at least three years. Local 23-25 was involved because its contract gave it the right to negotiate with Claiborne, even on issues affecting other Unite locals. Ms. Karp denied that Claiborne had made such a promise to its local.
In any case, Mademoiselle expanded its operation, its workers became union members and, for a shiny moment, the experiment seemed to be working.
But by 1995, the relationship was at an impasse. Claiborne was not providing the steady flow of orders needed to meet Mademoiselle's expanded overhead, Mr. Newhouse said. Mademoiselle was not meeting its delivery deadlines, Ms. Karp countered. And Mr. Newhouse was refusing to sign a new three-year contract with Local 155 unless he had some firm commitment of future work from Claiborne, Mr. Garren said.
The deadlock was resolved in August 1995 when Claiborne bowed to the union and promised Local 23-25 that it would order 600,000 sweaters a year from Mademoiselle over the next two years, if Mademoiselle performed "at satisfactory levels of quality and timeliness." In turn, Mr. Newhouse signed a new agreement with Local 155.
But when Claiborne once again complained that Mademoiselle's late deliveries justified canceling the new arrangement, Local 23-25 took Claiborne to arbitration in December 1995, telling the arbitrator that it was acting on behalf of its sister local at Mademoiselle. In May 1996, the arbitrator ruled that Claiborne's delivery deadlines were unreasonable and that Mademoiselle's performance was more than satisfactory. Claiborne was told to meet its 1995 commitment by renewing its orders.
By then, Claiborne had sued Mademoiselle for trademark infringement, which Mr. Newhouse said forced Mademoiselle into bankruptcy again in late March 1996. But Unite still backed the knitting factory. It filed a second arbitration case against Claiborne in September 1996, contending the company had not complied with the earlier arbitration order and demanding an estimated $30 million in actual damages.
In the spring of 1997, while that second arbitration was under way, Local 23-25 was negotiating a new three-year contract with Claiborne.
People familiar with the negotiations from both sides of the table agree that Claiborne was determined to resist Unite's demand that it deal only with union shops. Without such a condition in its contract with the manufacturer, the union would have been unable to demand liquidated damages from Claiborne in the future. Given Claiborne's size and influence, that could have undermined the payment system in the rest of the industry. Claiborne had clearly been squeezing the union on the issue in prior years. In negotiations in 1990, the company agreed to pay the union 70 percent of the amount it demanded in damages; by 1993, that figure had fallen to less than 36 percent.
 
Preserving Payments
Claiborne Reaches Deal With Union
In the first half of May 1997, two things happened: Local 23-25 agreed to drop the arbitration case on behalf of the Mademoiselle workers and to nullify the previous arbitration victory in exchange for Claiborne's agreement to finance the $750,000 severance fund; and Claiborne and Local 23-25 reached a new three-year collective bargaining agreement that awarded the union $13 million in liquidated damages for nonunion work done in the previous three years, 21 percent of what the union had demanded.
More important than the money, Claiborne also agreed to continue to pay liquidated damages if it violated the union-only rule in the next three years, thus preserving the payment system. Still, the cost was high for the union: Unite agreed to limit those future claims to $2.5 million a year regardless of how much nonunion work Claiborne ordered.
Mr. Garren, Unite's lawyer, called the arbitration settlement "a major, major victory" for the workers and "entirely reasonable in light of the strength and weaknesses" of the case and the deep animosity between Claiborne and Mr. Newhouse.
The lawyers for the Mademoiselle workers think otherwise. Mademoiselle's remaining workers "now face the loss of their jobs by reason of Unite's acceptance of what can only be described as a $20.5 million payoff," they contend in their complaint.
Meanwhile, Mademoiselle is awaiting the trademark infringement decision and promising its creditors in bankruptcy court that it will soon sue Claiborne and Unite for damages. And Unite is accusing Mademoiselle of numerous unfair labor practices before the National Labor Relations Board, which ruled last week that the complaints had enough merit to go forward.
Scholars of labor history can only shake their heads over the shattered relationship that held such hope six years ago.
"The enemies of organized labor are going to seize on this," said Daniel E. Diamond, a labor economist at New York University. "And that is so unfair, because it actually shows the tragic side of what's happening to workers in the United States."

HEADLINE: Home Resales Jumped 2.5% in March

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 27

 BODY:
Home resales in the United States boomed to a new record in March, unexpectedly jumping 2.5 percent, to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 4.89 million units.
The level breaks the record of a rate of 4.77 million units, set just the month before, the National Association of Realtors said today. The rates are the highest since the group began compiling the data in 1968. 
 Based on the first three months of the year, the group's president, R. Layne Morrill, predicted sales for all of 1998 would set a record. "Sales were at extraordinary levels in February and the momentum continued," he said. "This brings us into uncharted territory."
Driving sales were low mortgage rates, which makes home buying more affordable, and healthy income gains fueled by the lowest unemployment since the early 1970's.
By region last month, sales rose 7.4 percent in the Northeast, 5.1 percent in the Midwest and 1.6 percent in the South. They slipped 1 percent in the West.
Nationally, the median price for an existing home, meaning half sold for more and half for less, was $127,000 in March, up 5.8 percent from a year earlier.

HEADLINE: Nguyen Van Linh, Vietnam's Ex-Party Chief, Dies at 82

BYLINE: By PHILIP SHENON 

 BODY:
Nguyen Van Linh, the former Vietcong guerrilla who directed the 1968 Tet offensive and then revitalized Vietnam's economy as the nation's Communist Party leader, died yesterday at a hospital in Ho Chi Minh City, the Foreign Ministry announced. He was 82.
The ministry said Mr. Linh, who had been ailing for several years, had died of liver cancer. 
 The Vietnamese Government announced two days of mourning, with flags at half-staff throughout the nation and all entertainment programming halted on state television. A funeral was scheduled for tomorrow in Ho Chi Minh City, the former South Vietnamese capital that many of its residents still call Saigon.
For most Vietnamese, Mr. Linh will be remembered principally as the Communist Party general secretary who loosened the Government's stranglehold on the economy.
With his appointment to the post in 1986, Mr. Linh, a pragmatist with political roots in southern Vietnam, pushed a program of "doi moi," or economic renovation, that introduced elements of a free market to Vietnam after almost four decades of disastrous state planning.
But to historians of the Vietnam War and of the American Presidency, Mr. Linh is more likely to be recalled as a central architect of the events of Jan. 31, 1968, when small units of Vietnamese Communist commandos struck Saigon before dawn and laid siege to the American Embassy there.
Even though it collapsed within days, the Tet offensive -- the assault began on the second day of Tet, the Vietnamese Lunar New Year -- was the beginning of the end of South Vietnam and of American involvement in the war.
The television images of the chaos in the streets of the South Vietnamese capital were relayed back to the United States and prompted expressions of outrage not just among antiwar activists, but also among Americans who had previously been undecided about the conduct of the war. Two months later, President Lyndon B. Johnson announced that he would not seek re-election.
"The result of that offensive was that it shook the White House," Mr. Linh said years later. "The Vietnamese troops launched an attack right against the den of the Americans and the puppet army. From that fact, the United States realized it could not win the war in Vietnam and that it would not be beneficial economically or politically to prolong that war."
Nguyen Van Linh was born Nguyen Van Cuc -- like many of Vietnam's early revolutionaries, he took a nom de guerre -- on July 1, 1915, in Hai Hung province, near Hanoi.
Official biographies say he began agitating against French colonial rule at the age of 14, when he joined the Vietnamese Revolutionary Youth League, a precursor of the Indochinese Communist Party.
He was arrested in 1930 for distributing anti-French pamphlets and was imprisoned until 1936. On his release, he resumed his political activities, helping to organize Communist Party cells across Vietnam. He was jailed again from 1941 to 1945.
After Vietnam's proclamation of independence in 1945, Mr. Linh moved up through the ranks of the Communist Party and by 1960 was selected to serve on the party's Central Committee. He was named secretary of the region that covered what was then South Vietnam, a post in which he oversaw the Communist insurgency against the American-backed Government in Saigon.
After the Saigon Government fell in 1975 and Vietnam was reunified under the Communists, Mr. Linh was named party chief in Saigon, renamed Ho Chi Minh City, and promoted to the party's Politburo.
He ran into trouble with the ideological old guard because he resisted demands by his colleagues in Hanoi for a complete transformation of the capitalist south. "We want to reach socialism, but we want to go by airplane, not by bicycle," he was fond of saying.
From 1978 to 1981, he was considered a rising star in the party, but those who knew him well say Mr. Linh had a series of heated arguments with Le Duan, who succeeded Ho Chi Minh as party leader in 1969. And in 1982, Mr. Linh disappeared from the Politburo.
Friends of Mr. Linh say he resigned after another furious argument with the party chief over the transformation of the south to Communism and Mr. Linh's desire to preserve the force of private capital.
But with the Vietnamese economy showing signs of collapse by the mid-1980's, the need for more liberal, pragmatic economic policies became clear to all but the most doctrinaire members of the party. Mr. Linh was reinstated in the Politburo in 1985 and was named general secretary of the party the next year.
He quickly set to work, apologizing in a series of speeches for the errors of an ideology-driven economic policy that had marred Vietnam's recovery from nearly a half-century of warfare. He permitted private enterprise and market prices, and farm collectives were largely dismantled. There was an explosion of private shops, restaurants and home building.
Even as he yielded the Communist Party's power in the marketplace, Mr. Linh refused to give up the party's authority in the political sphere and attacked "pseudo-democrats" who called for a multiparty system in Vietnam.
"It is not objectively necessary to establish a political mechanism of pluralism and multiparty government," he said. "Socialism is the only right decision."
He eased Vietnam's international isolation with a more conciliatory foreign policy and sought to improve relations with both the United States and China.
In 1989, he ordered the withdrawal of Vietnamese troops from Cambodia; Vietnam had invaded Cambodia a decade earlier and overthrew the murderous regime of Pol Pot, who also died this month, and his Khmer Rouge followers. The troop withdrawal helped Vietnam regain acceptance in the West and hastened the end of American-led embargo on aid and investment in Vietnam.
Mr. Linh stepped down as party leader in 1991, complaining of poor health, and retired to Ho Chi Minh City.
He lived to regret the effects of many of the economic policies he championed. He startled the audience at a party congress two years ago when he gave a speech accusing foreign investors of seeking to exploit Vietnam and topple socialism.
In letters to newspapers last year, he criticized what he saw as a growing gap between the rich and the poor and a rise of vice connected to the free market. In a newspaper article in a party newspaper in July, he criticized American companies, saying they were dumping consumer goods in Vietnam rather than assisting the country through investment and technology.
"The question is not to beg to the foreign capitalists but to manage and work out policies which are more beneficial to the domestic economy," he said. "I hope that in the future, Vietnam will look for every measure to make our international relations equal."

LENGTH: 945 words

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
Honda Chief to Step Down; Few Changes Are Expected

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER 

 BODY:
Nobuhiko Kawamoto, the engineer who has led Honda to record profits and market shares in the United States and Japan over the last eight years, announced yesterday his retirement as president and chief executive.
Hiroyuki Yoshino, one of the Honda Motor Company's two executive vice presidents and the president of Honda's research and vehicle development division, was chosen to succeed Mr. Kawamoto. 
 Industry executives and analysts predicted that Mr. Yoshino's succes sion to the top job would result in few if any changes because he has been close to Mr. Kawamoto, succeeding him as president of the research division. Both men joined Honda's research division in April 1963, and have spent most of their careers in that division and in Honda's small but prestigious racing division.
Mr. Kawamoto, who is 62 years old, made his reputation within Honda in 1974 by working on the team that designed the first engine by any auto maker that met Federal clean air regulations in the United States without needing a catalytic converter. Mr. Yoshino was the first engineer Honda sent to the United States in the late 1960's to study the need for cleaner-burning engines, and also worked on the new engine in 1974 and other, more recent low-pollution engines. More recently, Mr. Kawamoto has infuriated Detroit auto executives by outspokenly contending that global warming is a serious problem that the auto industry should address.
The choice of Mr. Yoshino nonetheless surprised some auto industry officials because he is 58 years old. Honda has traditionally chosen presidents who are young by Japanese standards, typically in their late 40's or early 50's; indeed, the company has a mandatory retirement age of 60 for all but its highest executives. Mr. Kawamoto's predecessor, Tadashi Kume, retired at 58 in June 1990.
Mr. Kawamoto will remain at Honda as an adviser, but will not become the chairman. At many Japanese companies, retiring chief executives become chairmen. But the post of chairman at Honda is largely ceremonial, and all four chairmen over the years have been former executive vice presidents who were passed over for the job of president.
While the appointment of Mr. Yoshino remains subject to approval by Honda's shareholders in late June, that is largely a formality in Japan.
Seven years of economic weakness in Japan have created serious problems for the auto industry there, but Honda has weathered them better than its rivals. Profits have more than tripled during Mr. Kawamoto's tenure, to $1.78 billion in the fiscal year that ended on March 31, 1997. Results for the year that ended last month are not officially available, but Honda has said it will be another record year.
Mr. Kawamoto made two big decisions that have produced bonanzas for Honda in Japan and the United States, but at a critical time in Europe he made a choice that may haunt the company for many years.
When the Japanese economy began to weaken in the early 1990's, many auto makers, including a big rival of Honda's, Mitsubishi Motors, began slowing investment in new models. But Mr. Kawamoto actually increased Honda's spending on research and vehicle development, and produced such highly popular models as the CR-V sport utility vehicle and the new Accord, both of which went on sale last year. Honda's sales have been rising in Japan even as the overall market there shrinks, while its market share has soared.
"Honda redoubled its investments, and take a look at where it is today -- it has romped all over Mitsubishi Motors and its other Japanese competitors," said Jay N. Woodworth, a consultant in Summit, N.J., who specializes in Asian auto makers.
At the same time, Mr. Kawamoto invested heavily to expand Honda's manufacturing capability in the United States. This insulated the company from the effects of a strong yen in the mid-1990's, which made it expensive to ship cars from Japan.
But Mr. Kawamoto also decided against buying Rover Group in Britain. Honda actually owned a 20 percent stake in Rover, but decided not to bid for the entire company when British Aerospace P.L.C. sold its 80 percent stake in 1994. Bayerische Motoren Werke A.G. of Germany bought Rover, eventually including Honda's stake, for a price that seemed cheap at the time and looks like an even better deal for BMW now that the sport utility vehicle craze has made Land Rovers popular. Rover also sells small cars in Europe, a market where Honda has been weak.
Following a company motto that "Small is smart," Honda has also been slow to respond to the demand for large sport utility vehicles in the United States. Honda, which sells a small sport utility called the Passport, is at least a year away from introducing its first large model.
Mr. Kawamoto has been an iconoclast at a company that has at times seemed to pride itself on audacity, a rare quality in corporate Japan. Japanese business leaders rank Honda alongside the Sony Corporation as a Japanese company in name but a Western company in spirit, a combination that generates pride and criticism at the same time.
Honda's decision to study whether to re-enter the high-stakes, strutting world of Formula One racing this year was considered vintage Kawamoto and greeted by some commentators as unnecessarily flashy and expensive. The company withdrew from competition in 1992 when its financial condition deteriorated.
"Upon close examination, the external environment is no more accommodating now than it was when the company left the circuit," the Nikkei Weekly scolded in March. "And if Honda doesn't get some tangible results for its time and money, fiscal results will suffer again."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Hiroyuki Yoshino, right, answering a question at a Tokyo news conference called to announced the retirement of the Honda Motor president and chief executive, Nobuhiko Kawamoto, left. (Agence France-Presse)

HEADLINE: Shortsighted Medicare Oversight

 BODY:
The Health Care Financing Administration spends about $2 billion a year overseeing the $230 billion Medicare program, or only about 1 percent of revenues. By contrast, private insurers spend more than 10 percent on administration. Yet even while denying the agency the money it needs to do its job, Congress piles on new responsibilities.
For this year, the agency will make do. But if it is to root out endemic fraud and waste, let alone begin to better monitor the quality of health care provided to its 40 million beneficiaries, it will need more money. 
 Government studies document that Medicare is riddled with waste, fraud and inappropriate care, probably in excess of $20 billion a year. Yet Congress has let the agency's administrative budget slip by more than 10 percent, after inflation, over the past four years even as the number of enrollees and medical claims have soared. Congress has also told the health-care agency to expand the list of managed-care alternatives available to enrollees, a complicated task even with adequate resources.
For this year, the agency will cut back money for some of the insurance companies it pays to process claims by doctors and hospitals. That will force the companies to delay payments and offer less good administrative service to providers with minimal impact on beneficiaries.
But the agency makes a compelling case that, starting next year, it needs to impose $165 million in new "user fees" on the doctors and hospitals whose bills it processes. So far, Congress has refused -- an irresponsible response. These fees will amount to perhaps $200 for physicians.
The agency boasts that its tiny administrative budget is a triumph. In fact, the low cost reflects administrative failure. The agency does not, despite strong efforts, root out enough fraud and waste or monitor the quality of care. It spends so little money because it provides so little oversight -- a failure that it could try to reverse if Congress approves the administration's budget proposal.

HEADLINE: Corporate Push To Save Break On Foreign Tax

BYLINE: By DAVID CAY JOHNSTON 

 BODY:
American multinational corporations are lobbying fiercely to preserve a loophole inadvertently created two years ago by the Clinton Administration that allows them to avoid paying foreign governments billions of dollars a year in taxes.
And their campaign is bearing fruit. Yesterday, all of the Republicans and most of the Democrats on the House Ways and Means Committee, which sets tax policy, signed a letter urging the Treasury Department to withdraw regulations it issued in January aimed at closing the loophole. 
 "We strongly urge you to withdraw Notice 98-11 and the related regulations," the letter said, referring to the formal title of the new regulations. It added that Congress, not the Treasury Department, should decide the matter.
The lobbying campaign, which has so far drawn little public notice, centers on tax-simplification measures the Administration announced in 1996 that in essence allow corporations with operations overseas to move profits from one country to another without subjecting them to American taxes. Now, the companies have figured out how to use that flexibility to avoid foreign taxes. Before the 1996 measures, an American multinational that moved passive income -- interest, dividends, rents or royalties -- had to pay corporate income taxes to the United States.
Corporations quickly seized on the tax-simplification effort by creating subsidiaries technically known as disregarded entities but popularly known as tax-nothings.
In one common form of tax-nothing, an American company with a subsidiary that makes a product in, say, Germany will create a shell company in a tax-friendly country like the Netherlands. The German operation will borrow its capital from the Dutch company, paying so much interest that it makes no profits and, thus, avoids German income tax. The shell company in the Netherlands, meantime, pays only a nominal Dutch tax.
Treasury officials say they quickly became concerned about the implications of the loophole. Their primary fear was that American industry would shift more operations overseas to take advantage of the loophole, but they also worried that foreign governments that lost revenue as a result of such maneuvers would retaliate against the United States.
So in January, they issued their regulations aimed at stopping what the Government considers an abuse.
Since then representatives of dozens of big corporations -- including Coca-Cola, DuPont, Hallmark Cards, International Business Machines and Philip Morris -- have been quietly, but intensely, lobbying Congress to block the Treasury directives.
The companies say that multinational companies based outside the United States routinely use such loopholes and that it is unfair to deny them to American companies. They also say the Treasury lacks authority to issue the regulations because the measures concern policy matters only Congress can decide.
Nobody is estimating exactly how much tax money has escaped foreign tax collectors as a result of the loophole. Both Coke and DuPont, which the Treasury Department says are exploiting it, paid more foreign taxes last year than in 1996.
But, specialists say, foreign governments have already lost billions of dollars in revenues that they would otherwise have collected. And Michael McIntyre, a professor of tax law at Wayne State University in Detroit, warned of dire consequences if the loophole was not scotched.
"If this scheme works, it could convert the entire foreign world into one big tax haven," Mr. McIntyre said. "The giant sucking sound we'd hear would be American jobs and investment rushing out of the country in search of tax-free profits."
Joseph S. Tann, an international tax lawyer for Ameritech, the Chicago-based telephone company that owns part of the Danish telephone network, said fears that American companies would move their money -- and many jobs -- overseas as a result of the loophole were overblown. Other factors -- like the availability of skilled labor and the freedom to move profits in and out of a country -- are just as important to corporate planners, he said.
A senior Treasury official, speaking on condition of anonymity, said the loophole could deprive foreign governments of "potentially tens of billions of dollars." And the issue, important as it is, is symptomatic of a wider problem, this official said.
The dispute involves one of the most complex and fuzzy areas in international taxation -- efforts by companies to avoid being taxed repeatedly by different countries. And as the globalization of the world economy proceeds apace, and as ever-larger companies created by the latest wave of mega-mergers in the United States and elsewhere expand their global reach, governments throughout the world will need to coordinate their tax policies more closely to make sure corporations do not hide their profits from the tax man, the Treasury official said.
The lobbying effort is being led by Kenneth Kies, a Price Waterhouse tax specialist who was chief of staff on Congress's Joint Committee on Taxation until early this year and is a confidant of its Republican leaders, Representative Bill Archer of Texas and Senator William V. Roth of Delaware.
Representative Charles B. Rangel of New York, the ranking Democrat on the Ways and Means Committee, has written a separate letter to Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin in support of the companies.
All three lawmakers, in letters or through spokesmen, said that the Treasury Department lacked authority to issue the latest regulations. Mr. Rubin, in a letter to Mr. Archer, promised to tread lightly, but did not withdraw the new regulations.
When the tax-simplification rules were issued in 1996, the Treasury Department said it would hunt for abuses and would issue regulations to thwart any that it found.
A leading lobbyist trying to block the newest regulations, Mark Weinberger of the Washington Counsel law firm, acknowledged in an interview that "in theory companies could sharply reduce or eliminate their" global income tax burden through the use of tax-nothings. But, he added, "in the real world that is not how it works."
Besides, Mr. Weinberger said, "If the Germans lose tax revenue because of this, they're smart enough to figure out on their own what to do about it without help from the U.S. Treasury Department."
Treasury officials say that while audits have yet to begin, they know from other records that tax-nothings are widely used; Mr. Weinberger agreed.
Despite this, Mr. Weinberger and other lobbyists are emphasizing to members of Congress that the Treasury has yet to come up with a specific case of tax-nothings being abused and is instead relying only on hypothetical cases to support the latest regulations.
The Treasury Department could not have any specific cases yet because audits of 1997 corporate tax returns, the first in which tax-nothings could be used, have not begun. Don Roberts, a spokesman for the Internal Revenue Service, said that the first 1997 corporate tax returns were not due until March 15 and that audits of them had not started.

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: BONDS;
30-Year Treasury Is Off Sharply As Fears Re-emerge About Rates

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
The yield on the 30-year Treasury bond reached its highest level in nearly two months yesterday after a report that Federal Reserve officials were pondering an increase in interest rates. As investors sought to price in the risk of such a move, bond prices, which move opposite to yields, fell sharply.
The 30-year bond dropped 1 14/32, to a price of 101 2/32, with the yield rising to 6.05 percent, from 5.94 percent on Friday and the highest level since 6.07 percent on March 3. 
 While the economy has shown glimpses of rapid growth the last few months, inflation has remained benign, allowing the central bank to keep interest rates steady for more than a year. But the Fed's determination to keep monetary policy unchanged may be wavering as the Asian financial crisis fails to slow the United States economy as much as had been expected.
According to an article yesterday in The Wall Street Journal, the Federal Open Market Committee, at its meeting on March 31, indicated that the next move in interest rates would be upward, though not necessarily at the panel's next meeting on May 19.
Maury N. Harris, chief economist at Paine Webber Inc., said he was surprised that the Fed had shifted its bias in favor of monetary tightening. He said that economic data due this week, including the Employment Cost Index and gross domestic product, both to be released on Thursday, would be moderate enough to preclude a Fed tightening.
And Anthony Chan, managing director and chief economist at Banc One Investment Advisors in Columbus, Ohio, said the shift did not necessarily indicate an imminent increase in rates. He noted that after raising short-term rates in March 1997, the Fed has held rates steady. "In other words, recent history revealed that in the case of changing policy directives, the bark might be louder than the actual bite," he said.
Nonetheless, the price of the long bond suffered as investors sought to at least factor in the possibility that the Fed might move.
And elsewhere in the Treasury market, the weekly auction of new three- and six-month bills was lackluster. The three-month issue came in at an average rate of 4.940 percent, down from 4.985 percent last week. The average rate on the six-month bill was 5.115 percent, up from 5.060 percent.
The following are the results of yesterday's Treasury auction of new three-month and six-month bills:

HEADLINE: Few Follow as Giuliani Leads Way on New Site for Yankees

BYLINE: By CHARLES V. BAGLI 

 BODY:
Two years after he first unveiled a proposal to build a stadium for the Yankees on the West Side of Manhattan, Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani finds himself with few allies in an effort to build an expensive new home for the team at a new location.
Gov. George E. Pataki, who holds effective control over the site where the stadium would be built, above the railroad yards near 11th Avenue and 33d Street, has not endorsed the plan, voicing qualms about the cost of the project and the wisdom of abandoning the team's historic Bronx home. 
 The Manhattan Borough President, C. Virginia Fields, and the City Council Speaker, Peter F. Vallone, say the team should remain forever in the Bronx. And while some real estate developers are enthralled with the idea of building a West Side stadium, many business leaders have remained silent on the issue, saying they are concerned about alienating the Mayor, while privately expressing worries about such factors as a stadium's impact on congestion and pollution.
The Yankees' owner, George M. Steinbrenner 3d, has threatened to move his team out of the Bronx when its Yankee Stadium lease expires in 2002, and his threats were given more immediacy by the collapse of a steel expansion joint at the stadium two weeks ago. After that incident, Mayor Giuliani warned about the loss of the Yankees, praised the financial benefits of building a Manhattan stadium and even came up with a plan to finance construction of a replacement ball park, which could cost $850 million to $1.1 billion.
But with Mr. Steinbrenner's deadline looming, the Giuliani administration has done very little to fashion a formal plan for a baseball stadium over the railroad yards, which experts say would take about eight years to build. The architectural and economic consultants who worked for the city said they have been idle for more than a year and had no involvement with the Mayor's recently announced financing plan. And he has not discussed his stadium plans with state officials, who not only have control over the West Side rail yards through the Metropolitan Transportation Authority, but would presumably have to pay a portion of the cost of any stadium.
Charles A. Gargano, the state's Commissioner of Economic Development, said the state has been "in the dark" about the city's plans, and expressed skepticism that the city was laying the necessary groundwork to proceed with a West Side ball park.
"I haven't seen the kind of support you'd need for a new stadium in Manhattan," Mr. Gargano said. "You need to rally business leaders and the community around a stadium proposal, wherever it's going to be. I do know there's a lot of support for the Yankees' staying where they are, for both nostalgic reasons and for financial reasons."
Facing challenges in the past, Mr. Giuliani has overcome many political obstacles by sheer force of personality. But this time, his aides say, he has been hampered by the fact that Mr. Steinbrenner has not stated publicly where he wants to put the ball club when the lease in the Bronx runs out in 2002.
"Day One has not begun yet," said Cristyne F. Lategano, the Mayor's director of communications. "That will happen when a decision is made as to whether the Yankees stay in the Bronx, go to the West Side or to New Jersey, which we would adamantly oppose. Until that happens, you cannot build coalitions."
In recent days, executives who work with Mr. Steinbrenner have stoked the debate, saying that the team owner met last week with Jon Hanson, the former chairman of the New Jersey Sports and Exposition Authority, to discuss "a general proposition coming from across the river." An executive connected with the team said news of the meeting "could put pressure on Governor George Pataki and focus attention on the possibility of losing the Yankees."
Nevertheless, the executive, who spoke on condition of anonymity, praised Mr. Giuliani's efforts to keep the Yankees in the city.
"The Mayor has started the ball rolling," said the executive. "The whole thing is in play. You may never see a united front, but you may see a more united presentation once George Steinbrenner speaks up."
Bankers, consultants and politicians who are veterans of battles to erect stadiums and sports arenas say that such efforts demand an unstinting advocate from day one and a united front of political leaders, business executives and fans.
"There has to be a grass-roots effort where you have the business community, the political community and fans trying to get to the same end, as opposed to having one of those three groups running out there by themselves in front of the other two," said James E. Nash Jr., senior vice president of the sports finance group at Nations Bank in Charlotte, N.C. "There has to be a leader, but part of the responsibility of a leader is to build well-rounded support."
Marc Ganis, president of Sportscorp Ltd., a sports consulting firm based in Chicago that was involved in construction of the Trans World Dome in St. Louis and the MCI Center in Washington, said the challenges of building in New York could make the construction of a West Side stadium all the more difficult.
"Giuliani's going to need some help, especially when one of the lightning rod issues is the site itself," he said. "New York is known as a hotbed for land use advocates who oppose large-scale projects. I suspect that'll be more of a hot button issue than the financing as time goes on."
While conservatives have questioned the use of public money to finance stadium construction, the more traditionally liberal community leaders in Manhattan have already vowed to fight any plan to erect a stadium over the rail yards because of potential pollution and traffic congestion problems. The local community board is worried about the proximity of a stadium to four new office towers and two hotels that are planned nearby in Times Square, as well as to the commercial development planned for the Hudson River waterfront.
"This has all the trappings of a knock-down, drag-out battle that could continue for years," said Eric A. Goldstein, urban program director for the Natural Resources Defense Council. "The West Side plan raises a host of legitimate environmental questions, including traffic congestion and pedestrian mobility, that need to be answered before decisions are made."
But others see merit in the Mayor's arguments about the benefits of a West Side stadium. Meyer Frucher, a developer and an adviser to former Gov. Mario M. Cuomo who first suggested the West Side site for the Yankees, contends that a stadium there would ignite far more commercial and residential development than would a refurbished stadium in the Bronx.
"A Manhattan stadium provides an economic multiplier," Mr. Frucher said. "Every dollar you invest in the Bronx is a subsidy for George Steinbrenner. Everything you put into Manhattan will have a multitude of uses and will pay for itself."
Many other business executives disagreed privately, but had no desire to contradict the Mayor publicly. Some Broadway theater owners, for instance, say they worry that a West Side stadium would choke the area with cars and buses, dampening theater attendance. But with theater owners currently seeking zoning changes that would allow them to sell development rights over the theaters, they are unwilling to tangle with the Mayor.
"Most business people end up silent," said an executive who spoke on the condition of anonymity, "because of concern about what would happen if they speak out in any reserved, not even contrary, way."
The Mayor has also angered some business leaders over his proposal to finance stadium construction by extending the commercial rent tax, which the administration had planned to phase out.
"I was disappointed by the Mayor's backtracking on the elimination of the tax," said Douglas Durst, a developer and a major landlord in midtown who is also a member of the board of governors of the Real Estate Board. "There must be another way of financing the stadium."
Another potentially daunting obstacle to a West Side stadium is the skepticism of Mr. Vallone and Ms. Fields. "It is incumbent on the Mayor to present a plan, but there is no plan," Ms. Fields said. "It's hard for the public debate to move forward absent a concrete proposal."

HEADLINE: Many See Fed's Hand In Report Of Rate Intent

BYLINE: By RICHARD W. STEVENSON 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 27

 BODY:
Decisions made during the Federal Reserve's eight-times-a-year policymaking sessions are usually among the few true secrets in Washington, impervious to leak or spin.
That made it all the more extraordinary for investors, economists, Government officials and journalists to read in The Wall Street Journal today that the Federal Reserve had made what could prove to be a critical judgment at its last meeting on March 31: to lean toward raising interest rates. 
 Such a decision by the Fed -- known in the jargon of the trade as adopting a tightening bias -- would normally not have been publicly disclosed until May 21, two days after the next policy meeting. The Fed releases minutes of each meeting two days after the subsequent meeting, a practice adopted several years ago as a compromise between mem bers of Congress who demanded that the Fed open its deliberations to the public and Fed officials who felt that sensitive judgments about markets and the economy could only be made in private.
The consensus among most Fed watchers today was that senior officials of the Federal Reserve -- possibly Alan Greenspan, the chairman -- had decided to signal to the financial markets that a rate increase might be in the cards and had deliberately leaked the information. But trying to divine what happened proved to be as difficult as parsing one of Mr. Greenspan's elliptical statements.
Fed officials declined to make any comment about The Journal's article today or to indicate in any way whether it was correct. The Journal gave no source for its information, yet stated the policy change as fact, with no qualifiers, suggesting its own confidence in what was bound to be market-moving information.
Mr. Greenspan has proved consistent over the years in warning the markets when a change in interest rate policy is in the cards, although the warning has sometimes proved clear only in retrospect. Mr. Greenspan, however, typically sends such signals during speeches or in testimony to Congressional committees. Although he speaks regularly with journalists, he does so only off the record.
In speeches and interviews over the last several weeks, a number of Federal Reserve governors and Federal Reserve Bank presidents have said they are concerned that the Asian financial crisis was not slowing the economy as much as expected, implying that the central bank may have to raise rates to slow growth somewhat.
One theory popular today among Fed watchers was that officials at the central bank might have been frustrated that the markets were not getting the message that rates might have to rise and resorted to an attention-grabbing leak to get the message across.

HEADLINE: Surgeon General Warns of Rise in Ethnic Smoking

BYLINE: By SHERYL GAY STOLBERG 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 27

 BODY:
In a report that afforded President Clinton the perfect opportunity to renew his call for comprehensive tobacco legislation, the Surgeon General, Dr. David Satcher, warned today that increases in smoking by minorities, especially minority teen-agers, threaten to reverse significant declines in the incidence of cancer.
The study, the first Surgeon General's report to examine the health risks that tobacco poses to minorities, found that American Indians and Alaska natives were particularly vulnerable. Thirty-nine percent of them smoked, compared with 25 percent of the general United States population. From 1990 to 1995, while lung cancer deaths declined among other minority groups, they rose among American Indians and Alaska natives, the study found. 
 Among teen-agers, cigarette use increased among all racial and ethnic groups in the 1990's. But it was rising most rapidly among blacks, reversing declines in the 1970's and 1980's. If the pattern continues, Dr. Satcher estimated, 1.6 million black children will become regular smokers, and 500,000 will die as a result.
"These increases in tobacco use are a time bomb for our minority populations," the Surgeon General said at a ceremony to present the report, on the South Lawn of the White House. He concluded: "Smoking is the most preventable cause of death in America. So let's get busy and prevent it."
Dr. Satcher then presented the 332-page tome to the President, who wasted no time in denouncing the tobacco industry for advertising to young people. Nearly three dozen children dressed in bright red T-shirts with the logo of the National Center for Tobacco-Free Kids, a Washington advocacy group, lined up behind Mr. Clinton as he spoke.
"These are the replacement smokers of the advertisers' strategy," Mr. Clinton said, "but these are our children, and we can't replace them." Moments later, he added: "They're just kids. We're the grown-ups. If we know what the danger is, and we know what the remedy is, are we going to do what it takes to save their lives and their health and their future, or not?"
Rarely are Surgeon General's reports issued with such pomp and circumstance. But today's, the 24th in a series on tobacco use that began 34 years ago, in addition to being the first issued by Dr. Satcher, came at a time of political uncertainty over the prospects of tobacco legislation.
The Senate Commerce Committee has approved a measure, put forward by Senator John McCain, Republican of Arizona, that requires the tobacco companies to pay $516 billion over 25 years and raises the price of cigarettes by $1.10 a pack by 2003.
Three Senate Republicans were on hand for today's White House ceremony; one of them, Senator Bill Frist of Tennessee, shared a spot on the stage with Mr. Clinton, in an effort by the White House to demonstrate, as Mr. Clinton said, that tobacco control "is a medical, not a political issue, and an American, not a partisan issue."
But the issue is very much partisan in the House, where Republican leaders have indicated they will oppose the McCain bill, which is also opposed by the tobacco industry.
The study looked at four major ethnic groups -- black; Hispanic; Native American and Alaska native; and Asian and Pacific Islander -- that together make up a quarter of the United States population.
The study was a review of a vast body of research on smoking and health, based primarily on statistics from 1994 and 1995. It found that no single factor determines patterns of tobacco use among these groups, but rather a complex interaction of factors, including socio-economic status, assimilation, stress, biology, targeted advertising and price, accounts for trends that vary widely from ethnic group to ethnic group.
For instance, the study found that while Asian Americans and Pacific Islanders are the least likely of the four ethnic groups to smoke, several local surveys reported very high smoking rates among recent male immigrants from Southeast Asia.
"This new report leaves no doubt that cigarette smoking impairs and kills people of all racial and ethnic backgrounds," Dr. Satcher said. Tobacco, he said, noting that there were 10 million smokers among the ethnic groups studied, is "holding hostage the hopes for a better life."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Dr. David Satcher and President Clinton at the White House yesterday with children from the Campaign for Tobacco-Free Kids. "These are the replacement smokers of the advertisers' strategy," Mr. Clinton said. (Paul Hosefros/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Chretien Finds Castro Willing to Deal, but Not on Rights

BYLINE: By ANTHONY DePALMA 

DATELINE: HAVANA, April 27

 BODY:
Prime Minister Jean Chretien set modest goals for his first visit to Cuba this week: further Canada's substantial business interests here and prod Cuba into doing something about human rights.
After meeting for several hours with Fidel Castro today, Mr. Chretien had a committment from Cuba to negotiate a foreign investment protection agreement with Canada. Havana also agreed to pay $10 million to a Canadian insurance company that lost its business in Cuba after the 1959 revolution. 
 But on human rights, Mr. Chretien failed to win any concession.
At one point during his meeting today with Mr. Castro at the Palace of the Revolution, Mr. Chretien said he handed Mr. Castro a list of political prisoners that Canada wants released. The dissidents -- Marta Beatriz Roque, Vladimiro Roca, Felix Bonne and Rene Gomez Manzano -- were detained on July 16, 1997, for the "counterrevolutionary" activity of calling for democratic reforms.
"He defended his legal system," Mr. Chretien said, "but he took the list and said he was to consider it."
Mr. Chretien refused to meet with Elizardo Sanchez, one of Cuba's leading dissidents. But his chief foreign policy adviser and other officials met with Mr. Sanchez and other dissidents for over an hour.
Mr. Chretien's 41-hour visit to Havana, the first by a Canadian Prime Minister since Pierre Elliott Trudeau came to skin dive with Mr. Castro in 1976, comes at a delicate moment. Against the wishes of some Cuban-Americans and hard-line opponents to the Castro Government in the United States, the Clinton Administration has moved to ease the 36-year-old economic embargo against Cuba slightly.
Last month President Clinton agreed to lift bans on direct flights to Cuba and cash remittances that allow families to send dollars to Cuba. The President also said he would make it easier for medicine to be shipped to Cuba.
At the same time, Mr. Castro has been more bellicose than ever in his condemnation of the United States embargo, going so far this week as to use the occasion of Mr. Chretien's visit to compare the embargo to "a new version of the Holocaust," and suggest that United States officials should be tried as war criminals before an international court.
Today the White House spokesman, Michael D. McCurry, called Mr. Castro's comments "ample evidence of what an 'outlier' he is in the world community." He also criticized Canada's position toward Cuba. "We certainly understand their desire to achieve change through engagement," Mr. McCurry said. "We do not believe there is evidence that engagement with Cuba has produced any change."
Until now there had been only muted criticism from Washington about Mr. Chretien's trip, mostly from the Cuban-American Congressmen who fiercely oppose Mr. Castro.
Mr. Chretien called Mr. Clinton two weeks ago to advise him of the trip, and said today that Mr. Clinton had only asked him to bring up the question of Cuba's record on human rights. "The only comment he made to me was 'I hope, Jean, that you will raise human rights,' " Mr. Chretien told reporters. "And it was the first item of the presentation I made this morning."
Canada has never tried to enforce the United States economic embargo against Cuba, much to Washington's displeasure. In January 1997, Canada's Foreign Minister, Lloyd Axworthy, paid a brief visit to Havana. Since then, Cuban officials have visited Ottawa, and earlier this month Canada agreed to take 14 political prisoners exiled by the Castro Government.
Canadian officials say that after the Pope's visit in January, and the Summit of the Americas in Santiago, Chile, this month, it seemed to be the right time for Mr. Chretien to visit.
From the moment Mr. Chretien arrived on Sunday night to dedicate a new airport terminal in Havana that was financed, designed and built by Canadians, it was clear that the Prime Minister's modest goals for the trip would be overshadowed by Mr. Castro's attempts to defy the United States.
While Mr. Chretien gently outlined Canada's desire to see Cuba move more closely into "a more dynamic, more democratic, more prosperous hemisphere," Mr. Castro denounced the United States.
"No state should pretend to have the right to starve another people to death," Mr. Castro said as Mr. Chretien stood stiffly behind him. "That is turning a nation into a ghetto and imposing on it a new version of the Holocaust."

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Treasury Yields Surge On Fear of Rising Rates
Investors fearful of a rise in interest rates sold bonds aggressively, pushing up the yield on the 30-year Treasury to 6.05 percent, from 5.94 percent on Friday, and contributing to a selloff in the stock market. It was the highest level in two months.
Bond market strategists attributed the selling to a report that the Federal Reserve was now leaning toward a rise in rates to suppress inflationary pressure. Even so, some said they saw little chance that the Fed would raise rates at the next meeting of its policy-making committee, on May 19. Page D10. 

Networking Deal For Lucent
Lucent Technologies agreed to acquire Yurie Systems, a small but rapidly growing manufacturer of devices that make it easier to send data and video over the Internet, for $1 billion. Lucent has been pushing aggressively into that business. D3.
 
Reed Elsevier To Grow Again
Times Mirror will sell its legal publishing business to the British-Dutch publishing giant Reed Elsevier for $1.65 billion in cash. The deal consolidates Reed Elsevier's strong position in legal publishing. D6.

Deal by Cox Cable Is Expected
Cox Cable Communications is said to be close to a deal to acquire 60 percent of the Prime Cable system in Las Vegas, Nev. D3.
 
Honda Chief to Step Down
Honda's president and chief executive, Nobuhiko Kawamoto, who has led the company to big gains in profits and market share in the United States and Japan, will step down, to be replaced by the head of research and vehicle development. D4.
 
Oxford Health Reports Loss
Oxford Health Plans reported an operating loss for the first quarter, though the numbers were better than expected by analysts. The announcement, which did not include disclosure of the company's income statement or balance sheet, appears to have been made in order to allow Oxford to discuss the results with potential buyers of an offering of high-yield bonds. D2.
 
Home Resales Set Record
Sales of pre-owned homes rose 2.5 percent in March, breaking the record set in February, the National Association of Realtors reported. It attributed to boom to low mortgage rates and rising incomes. D8.
 
A Fund Manager's Balancing Act
Mario J. Gabelli, the well-known mutual fund manager, wants to sell part of his fund and investment empire to the public. But he plans to keep control of the company -- and, perhaps, many of his best investment ideas. Market Place. D9.
 
Pledge on Blood Drug Data
The four largest manufacturers of a life-saving drug, a form of immune globulin that is injected intravenously, announced that they would begin releasing production data so that the public could better monitor the severity of a national shortage. A12.
 
Punishments in Japan
The Japanese Finance Ministry punished 112 ministry officials with salary cuts and some demotions after an internal investigation showed that they accepted what was considered excessive entertainment from banks, brokerage firms and insurers under their supervision. D5.
 
Campbell Ends Plan for Healthy Line
Campbell Soup has dropped an effort to market frozen foods to people with high blood pressure, high cholesterol and diabetes. D2.
