HEADLINE: Jews and Arabs, Painting a Mural Together, Find a Mosaic of Mistrust

SERIES: ISRAEL AT 50: Picturing the Future

BYLINE: By ETHAN BRONNER 

DATELINE: JERUSALEM, April 27

 BODY:
It was a noble plan: Three peace-loving artists from conflicting backgrounds -- an Israeli Jew, an Israeli Arab and a Palestinian -- would produce a mural depicting a future without fear, a symbolic guide to trying to live together in this disputed land. But the process proved far more tortured than any of them imagined, a microcosm of mistrust and betrayal that mirrored the hostilities they had set out to overcome.
There was, for example, the devil incident. 
 The Jewish artist, with approval of the others, had painted in an ethereal, angelic figure to represent his people. But after the mural was unveiled, the Palestinian stunned his Jewish colleague by describing the figure to the audience as a devil that only looked like an angel, "like the Israelis themselves."
The Jewish artist felt betrayed. The Palestinian, for his part, was furious at what he saw as an attempt to make the Jews the heroes of the story.
On a personal level, in private, the men forged a bond, made each other laugh and shared a deep mutual respect. And the artists' determination to pursue further work together provides reason for hope. But burdened by history and communal expectations, their mural became a battleground of symbols.
Their troubled collaboration offers a sobering glimpse into why, 50 years after Israel's founding, the sides remain so fiercely mistrustful of one another, as if their behavior had been scripted and they were unable to imagine new lines for themselves.
The idea for the mural originated abroad. Judith Baca, a Mexican-American muralist, approached an Israeli Jewish artist, Adi Yekutieli, with the proposal to take part in "The World Wall: A Vision of the Future Without Fear," an international traveling exhibit of large, portable canvas murals that she had developed.
Mr. Yekutieli, an intense, soft-spoken man of 39 who is active in trying to improve Jewish-Arab relations, comes from Israel's founding aristocracy. His great-uncle was the first child born in Tel Aviv, his father was a close aide to Defense Minister Moshe Dayan, and his brother is a leftist member of the Jerusalem City Council.
Mr. Yekutieli spoke to Ahmed Bweerat, 45, a founder of the Israeli Arab Artists Guild and himself an enthusiast for Jewish-Arab cooperation. He quickly agreed to take part.
Finding a Palestinian was harder.
To Mr. Yekutieli, this element was crucial, since whatever the problems faced by Israel's 1 million Arab citizens like Mr. Bweerat, the tougher conflict is that between Israel and the Palestinians in the West Bank and Gaza Strip who want an independent state with East Jerusalem as its capital.
And with peace negotiations between Israel and Yasir Arafat's Palestinian Authority stalled, Palestinians have cut off many contacts with Israelis. Several West Bank artists told Mr. Bweerat that they would not take part without the permission of the Palestinian Authority.
Ultimately, Mr. Bweerat came upon Suliman Mansour, 50, founder and director of Al Wasiti, an East Jerusalem art center for Palestinians. A painter who has had long associations with Israelis and Israeli artists, Mr. Mansour is nonetheless seen as a Palestinian patriot for his work before and during the 1987-93 intifada, or uprising. He served as head of the Palestinian Artists League, was on the payroll of the Palestine Liberation Organization and did several short stints in Israeli jails.
With a rumpled yet commanding presence behind a haze of cigarette smoke, Mr. Mansour said he made sure that the project was not Israeli-sponsored but felt no need to ask for official permission.
But Mr. Mansour set several ground rules for their collaboration. He insisted that they communicate in English, not Hebrew -- "the language of the occupier" -- although he speaks both well and Mr. Bweerat's English is limited. Mr. Yekutieli does not speak Arabic. Mr. Mansour also said the work would take place in Jerusalem rather than in the Tel Aviv suburb of Herzliyya, where Mr. Yekutieli runs a school's art department, or Mr. Bweerat's northern village of Arara.
 
Divided Loyalities Of Israeli Arab
The dynamics were complex from the beginning, starting with the question of Mr. Bweerat's loyalties, as an Israeli and an Arab.
As the Palestinian Mr. Mansour put it: "He thought West Bankers were primitive, cheaters, unable to keep their word. It's all stuff he got from the Israelis. But while working, his attitude changed. He saw that he and I like the same things and have much in common."
This is what Israeli hawks say they worry about: Palestinian self-determination in the West Bank and Gaza awakening dormant resentment among Israeli Arabs.
Mr. Bweerat admits to divided loyalties. Until the mid-1960's, he and his village lived under Israeli military rule.
"Every poem, every drawing, every gathering brought soldiers who said we were a security risk," he recalled. "When I was a kid, on Israeli Independence Day they used to force us to raise the Israeli flag and sing this song about the glories of Israel.
"That's over now, and I have good work, a nice car, a big house -- I have freedom of choice, unlike Arabs elsewhere," he said. "But I can never be prime minister or even minister in this country. And our village is not allowed to expand and build. So I'm divided. Throughout the work on the mural, I felt my role was as a go-between, to lower the fires on both sides."
In the mural, in which each artist's group is represented by a separate image, the Israeli Arabs are portrayed by Mr. Bweerat as a split figure sitting on a bridge made up of a combination of the Israeli and Palestinian flags with a black line dividing the two. The bottom of the bridge is broken.
 
Jew Is Accused Of Taking Over
Mr. Bweerat sometimes joined Mr. Mansour against Mr. Yekutieli, whom both Arabs considered somewhat bossy.
Mr. Yekutieli recognized their concern and tried to reassure them. Once he said in exasperation, "I'm allowing you to do whatever you want with the mural."
"You're allowing me?" Mr. Bweerat flashed back. "Permission is something granted to a child or an underling."
Mr. Yekutieli said such moments were sources of enormous frustration for him. All the work of organizing the mural -- getting the financing from the Canada Fund for Dialogue and Development, organizing the trio's trip to Monterey, Calif., for the painting and public presentation of the mural -- fell to him. When he asked the others to get involved, they backed off, saying he dealt with such things best.
"Then they would accuse me of taking over," he said.
He also suffered over the question of what images were supposed to represent, especially the one for Israel. The angel he devised for Israel was one selected by the others; the deal they worked out was that each presented options from which the others chose. But when it was unveiled in April, he found himself listening incredulously as Mr. Mansour told their audience that it was a devil, posing in angel's dress.
"I felt completely deceived," Mr. Yekutieli said. "I kept having to swallow everything, accept whatever they threw at me because I'm the 'occupier.' "
"I'm willing to recognize a Palestinian state," he said, "I'm willing to do all the organizing and fund-raising for us, but I'm not willing to be stepped on."
 
Why He Saw 'An Evil Angel'
Mr. Mansour later explained:
"At first, I didn't give much thought to it. But then I saw that its wings were pointed, its face was blank, and I realized this was an evil angel. I thought it was an image that fit Adi. He's like all Israelis who want to show something that is not there, to give the impression to the world that everything is O.K. Why are we Palestinians complaining about occupation when these artists are working together, everything is fine, there is no problem?
"But there is a problem: For Palestinians, what Israel is doing is bad," he said. "Yet we Palestinians are seen as the bad guys, and they are the good guys, the angels. So this is what it reflects. This thing that looks like an angel is not an angel but evil."
Throughout the project, Mr. Mansour felt the need as a Palestinian to be on guard against Israeli domination.
"I was very aware that Adi thought his cleverness would dominate the whole thing," he said. "He is like all Israelis. He believes he is more clever than me."
Mr. Yekutieli, in fact, has enormous respect for Mr. Mansour, whose defensiveness can be hard to fathom. He was the first Palestinian to earn a degree from an Israeli art institute in the late 1960's. He said he had made Israeli friends, adding, "I accepted Israel and started to see it as a country with real people and a real culture."
Mr. Mansour's Palestinian image in the mural is of a street sweeper.
Explaining why, he said: "If we want a future without fear, we must first deal with the fear, and my fear is for my children, what they can achieve, what kind of life will be open to them. I am afraid they will end up as street sweepers in Tel Aviv."
The chances that such fears will be realized appear low. Mr. Mansour lives in a spacious Jerusalem apartment with his wife, Hanan, a teacher, and four accomplished children. His eldest, a daughter, Sahar, 19, is a medical student at Al Quds University. Yet Mr. Mansour insists that his fears are justified.
"Things here are complex, and it is true that one can feel several things at once which can seem contradictory," he said.
There was another, very telling, dispute over the mural's central image.
It is of a cracked mud figure based on Mr. Mansour's current work, mud sculptures on door-sized pieces of wood. He began such work during the intifada, when an ethic of self-reliance developed among Palestinians who vowed not to buy anything, not even art supplies, from the Israelis.
Mr. Mansour considered the mud symbolic because it came from the disputed land, often gathered from spots where Israeli tractors prepared roads for Jewish settlements. His immobile mud figures, he says, represent the Palestinian people waiting for a touch of life.
 
Trying to Please Only Backfires
Mr. Yekutieli, impressed with the work and wanting to please Mr. Mansour, suggested it as a powerful central figure that could be re-interpreted for the purposes of the mural as a universal image. Mr. Mansour agreed. Yet when it came time to discuss it in public, he referred to it only as a symbol of the Palestinians and their rebellion.
Mr. Bweerat, their Israeli Arab colleague, went further, saying not only that it represented Palestinians but also that "the Jews want to divide Israel, giving the desert to Ishmael and the green part to Isaac," referring to the Arabs and the Jews respectively. He suggested putting a horse inside Mr. Yekutieli's figure, saying: "We were the horse for 50 years, and you rode us. It's your turn."
Mr. Yekutieli felt abused. "I had reached my limit," he said.
When it came time to write about the mural, Mr. Bweerat drafted a statement that said: "Fifty years of armed struggle, no winners, no losers. Fifty years, marking the point of a new beginning is not worth one tear drop of a wounded child."
Mr. Yekutieli felt that this was a direct slap at Israel's existence and refused to sign it. He wrote his own.
Mr. Mansour raised another concern: Why three separate figures for the three groups? If the point of the mural is collaboration, why not create one integrated work just as Jews, Muslims and Christians should live together as equals in one country?
Here another core dispute was exposed. For many Israelis, this would be a recipe for suicide. Without Jewish sovereignty, they feel, they have no security.
"Give me my space, and allow me to be secure in my space," Mr. Yekutieli countered.
Looking back, the artists have concluded that despite the tension, there was still a remarkable peace to the enterprise. They have just secured a Norwegian grant to document the making of the mural and plan to collaborate next on a huge mosaic of children's images to be placed, they hope, on a future Israeli-Palestinian border.
"This was a stage that you have to go through," Mr. Bweerat said, echoing words of the others. "There is hatred and stigma on all sides, but this is the only hope of getting beyond them. It's going to have to happen for everyone here. There is no other choice."
 
The Series So Far
These articles focus on facets of Israel as it nears the anniversary of its independence, observed this year on April 30. The series, which began on April 6, has examined the country's social diversity, the controversy surrounding a history television series, the transformation of the economy, the Palestinians, and American views of Israel.

HEADLINE: Hoffa Gets Clearance to Run, Creating Teamsters Face-Off

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE 

 BODY:
Clearing the way for another struggle for control of the teamsters, a Federal monitor ruled yesterday that James P. Hoffa could remain in the race for the union's presidency, but he also found that Mr. Hoffa had engaged in some improprieties in the 1996 union election.
The decision clarifies the confused picture in this summer's teamster election: it appears now it will be a face-off between Mr. Hoffa, widely considered the front-runner, and Ken Hall, a high-level teamsters official who played a pivotal role in last year's successful strike against the United Parcel Service. 
 Mr. Hall largely has the backing of the forces that long supported the teamsters president Ron Carey, the self-styled reformer under investigation for his role in an embezzlement scheme. The decision by Michael Cherkasky, the Federal election monitor, seems to leave both sides without the ability to run as untainted reform candidates.
Mr. Hall is tarnished because he is considered a protege of the disgraced Mr. Carey, now on leave from the union's presidency, while Mr. Hoffa emerges bruised from yesterday's decision because it fined him, suggested he lied several times and barred a leading aide from participating in the campaign.
Dating back to the 1960's, when Mr. Hoffa's famous father led the teamsters, the Federal Government has struggled to clean up the union, and now the Federal monitor's decision creates a strong chance that Jimmy Hoffa's son will take its reins. The teamsters presidency is important for the labor movement and the nation at large, because the union is one of the nation's biggest, most powerful and most visible and because its 1.4 million members handle goods touching every nook and cranny of the nation's economy.
The Carey forces received another jolt yesterday when a Federal grand jury in Manhattan indicted William Hamilton, the union's former director of government affairs, charging him with helping siphon union money into Mr. Carey's 1996 campaign. Mr. Hamilton's lawyer, Robert Gage, asserted that his client was innocent.
The stage is now set for another campaign in which Mr. Hoffa will pledge to restore the power the union had when his father led it, while Mr. Hall will try to capitalize on Mr. Carey's successes, most notably the U.P.S. strike.
Mr. Cherkasky said Mr. Hoffa was responsible for failing to report almost $44,000 in union campaign contributions accurately and had not testified truthfully about receiving a $1,000 contribution from a former teamsters president, William McCarthy.
Explaining that he was loath to disqualify candidates because it limited union members' right to choose, Mr. Cherkasky said in an interview: "While there were serious violations, they were not sufficient in this context to disqualify Mr. Hoffa. Any disqualification is inherently anti-democratic."
Mr. Cherkasky released his report after another court-appointed monitor had barred Mr. Carey from running again, finding that he had cooperated in an illegal scheme to siphon more than $850,000 in union money into his campaign coffers.
After that scheme was uncovered, Federal monitors ordered a new election and overturned Mr. Carey's narrow 1996 victory over Mr. Hoffa, whose father ran the union from 1959 to 1971.
But Mr. Cherkasky found major violations by several main Hoffa aides and supporters. He barred Mr. Hoffa's chief spokesman, Richard Leebove, from working in this year's teamsters campaign, finding that he vastly underbilled the Hoffa campaign and thereby made $167,675 in improper campaign contributions. Mr. Cherkasky also barred a candidate on Mr. Hoffa's slate, Mary Lou Salmeron, from running again, finding that she had coerced the staff of her union local to make campaign contributions.
His investigation also unearthed evidence that could lead to the expulson of two other prominent Hoffa supporters from the International Brotherhood of Teamsters, which represents truck drivers, warehouse workers, flight attendants and other workers.
Mr. Cherkasky fined the Hoffa campaign $30,910 for improperly receiving donated services from his press spokesman and for failing to itemize more than $50,000 in contributions -- out of some $3.6 million collected by Mr. Hoffa and his support groups. Mr. Cherkasky did not penalize Mr. Hoffa for what he described as sometimes inaccurate testimony.
"We certainly feel vindicated," Mr. Hoffa said in a telephone interview. "As we said all along, our campaign was basically run properly. We intend to pay the fines and move on with the campaign."
In the best news for Mr. Hoffa, Mr. Cherkasky cleared him and his camp of the main charge, that it had failed to detail the source of $2 million in campaign contributions. The Hoffa camp insisted that those contributions came in sums of $100 or less and thus did not have to be reported, while his opponents insisted that much of that money came in large contributions, which have to be reported, or came from employers, who are barred from making contributions to union candidates.
In a statement, Mr. Hoffa said, "This investigation found that I am an honest man who ran an honest campaign."
While the Hoffa and Hall camps both insist their candidates are clean, the recent rulings by Federal monitors have left each side accusing the other of being sullied.
Mr. Carey appointed Mr. Hall to be director of the union's 200,000-member small-package division, and in that role Mr. Hall, a 41-year-old West Virginian, was Mr. Carey's right-hand man throughout the strike last August by 185,000 U.P.S. workers.
Mr. Hall called on Mr. Hoffa yesterday to pull out of the race.
"In the interest of restoring integrity to the union, Hoffa should withdraw," Mr. Hall said in a telephone interview. "First of all, among these findings, Hoffa lied to the election officer. Secondly, he accepted $167,000 in illegal contributions."
In the five-month investigation, Mr. Cherkasky, chief operating officer of Kroll Associates, and his staff audited the Hoffa campaign, subpoenaed information from campaign vendors, and interviewed hundreds of witnesses.
Mr. Cherkasky concluded that it was credible for the Hoffa campaign to have raised $2 million in sums of $100 or less after he examined hundreds of check stubs, scrutinized bank accounts and looked into the tens of thousands of Hoffa hats and T-shirts the Hoffa campaign had ordered and then sold to supporters.
"In sum," his report said, "the Election Officer finds credible evidence that at least a substantial majority of the over $2 million in un-itemized contributions made to the Hoffa Slate Campaigns came from lawful sources. While a significant dollar amount of contributions could not be traced and, therefore, could potentially have come from improper sources, there is no evidence of such improper contributions."
Mr. Cherkasky examined accusations that the leaders of a large teamsters local in Michigan gave each of 14 officials a $2,500 raise and a large Christmas bonus that the officials were said to have contributed to the Hoffa campaign. Mr. Cherkasky found that the money did not go to Mr. Hoffa, but instead went to the re-election campaign of Larry Brennan, the local's principal officer.
Mr. Brennan, seen as Mr. Hoffa's chief mentor, has given Mr. Hoffa a job as an administrative assistant at the teamsters' Michigan joint council, which Mr. Brennan heads.
Mr. Cherkasky said the raise, together with turning the money to a top Brennan fund-raiser, Carlo Scalf -- also a major figure in the Hoffa campaign -- was "disturbing." Mr. Cherkasky said he would turn the evidence over to the Independent Review Board, a three-person court-appointed board that oversees the teamsters, to determine whether Mr. Brennan and Mr. Scalf should be expelled.
Several times in his decision, Mr. Cherkasky asserted that Mr. Hoffa was less than candid in his sworn testimony to the Federal election officer. Discussing the failure to report the $1,000 contribution by Mr. McCarthy, the former president, Mr. Cherkasky wrote: "Mr. Hoffa's testimony on the preparation of" his campaign-finance statements, "and the omission of the McCarthy contribution was not complete or accurate."
The report found that the Hoffa campaign had sought to hide that Kevin Currie, the director of its New York operation, was a felon convicted of grand larceny by having the campaign write paychecks in his wife's name. The report found that the Hoffa camp violated campaign expenditure and reporting rules by paying $20,184.75 to Mr. Currie's wife.
In the indictment handed up yesterday, the Federal grand jury accused Mr. Hamilton of six counts: conspiracy, embezzling union funds, mail fraud, wire fraud, perjury in connection with grand jury testimony and making false statements to the election officer. Three Carey campaign aides have already pleaded guilty to conspiracy charges.
The indictment said Mr. Hamilton approved more than $750,000 in payments to Citizen Action and other liberal groups in an effort to get out the vote to help the Democrats recapture Congress in 1996. As part of an embezzlement scheme, the indictment charged, contributors to those liberal groups were then supposed to give money to Mr. Carey's campaign.
The indictment also accused Mr. Hamilton of approving contributions to the Democratic Party as part of an effort to have Democratic donors give to the Carey campaign.
Robert Gage, Mr. Hamilton's lawyer, said, "Bill is innocent of all charges, and we look forward to our day in court."
In the past, Mr. Hamilton has asserted that he did not knowingly do anything illegal and that the contributions advanced the teamsters' goal to help restore Democrats' control of Congress.

HEADLINE: Restrictions on Iraq Will Stay in Force, U.N. Council Rules

BYLINE: By BARBARA CROSSETTE 

DATELINE: UNITED NATIONS, April 27

 BODY:
The Security Council decided tonight to extend sanctions against Iraq, ignoring threats from Baghdad that it would disrupt future arms inspections if the sanctions were not lifted.
"The international community is one," Bill Richardson, the United States representative, said tonight. "Sanctions will not be lifted because Iraq has failed to comply." He said that on biological and chemical weapons and missile systems, United Nations weapons inspectors had found that Iraq still had a long way to go in accounting for all of its weapons of mass destruction. 
 But in a gesture to the Iraqis, who have recently allowed greater access to arms inspectors, the Council did not limit future sanctions reviews to six-month intervals but reverted to a system of permitting reviews every 60 days. That was the procedure before Iraq instigated a series of crises last year, bringing President Saddam Hussein to the brink of war with the United States a few months ago.
But diplomats do not expect the next big push for a lifting or easing of the sanctions until October, when the next major report on Iraqi compliance is due from the United Nations Special Commission, which has been in charge of eliminating Iraq's weapons of mass destruction and its capacity to make more.
The United States has vowed to veto any lifting of the sanctions, imposed after Iraq invaded Kuwait in 1990, until Baghdad has met a number of requirements. Those include not only verifiable disarmament, but also the return of Kuwaiti prisoners and property seized in the invasion and a general improvement in respecting human rights.
Although there was never a chance that Iraq would be freed from sanctions in this review -- the first since an agreement in February between President Hussein and Secretary General Kofi Annan that defused the most recent crisis -- the atmosphere around today's Security Council meeting was noticeably different from previous sessions on the issue.
Mr. Richardson was somewhat conciliatory in his assessment of Iraq's recent performance.
Facing eroding support for unlimited sanctions, he acknowledged some Iraqi progress in disarmament, including meeting most demands of nuclear inspectors from the International Atomic Energy Agency. But he continues to object to Russian, Chinese and French calls to "close the file" on nuclear weapons in Iraq and shift from an active "search and destroy" phase of disarmament to long-term monitoring.
Russia has proposed a resolution to make the change, which the United States opposes. Iraq's Foreign Minister said in an interview on Saturday that he expected the Russians and Americans to work out a compromise. Diplomats say it will take the form of a Council statement endorsing the finding that there is no evidence of a nuclear weapons program without formally graduating Iraq from the current level of inspection to a less severe one.
"We in the United States acknowledge progress in areas like access to presidential and sensitive sites," Mr. Richardson said. "There appears to be some progress in the nuclear file, but we believe that it is premature to totally close that file without further steps being taken specifically regarding nuclear enrichment, design and nuclear exports."
Shen Guofeng, China's new deputy representative to the United Nations , argued not only for closing the nuclear weapons file now, but also for moving to end sanctions generally, "because the Iraqi people are suffering a lot." But he acknowledged that Iraq still had requirements to meet.
The Council heard today from Richard Butler, executive chairman of the Special Commission in charge of eliminating Iraq's biological, chemical and missile programs, and Garry Dillon, leader of the International Atomic Energy Agency inspectors. Mr. Butler has found no progress on outstanding issues in the last six months and Mr. Dillon, while more upbeat, said he still had some unanswered questions.
Jayantha Dhanapala, who led diplomatic escorts on a tour of eight Iraqi presidential sites being inspected by the commission, also reported to the Council on the generally smooth initial visits to the properties. But it is unclear what will happen when inspectors want to return for further inspections.
The United States, increasingly isolated among Council members who say eight years of sanctions are enough, gets its strongest support from Britain, whose diplomats caution against shifting the debate from Iraqi compliance to Iraqi suffering.
Sir John Weston, the British representative to the United Nations, said in an interview on Friday that the starting point of the debate was the potential that President Hussein still had to produce biological and chemical weapons and missiles. That situation is spelled out in a series of new reports, including several from independent teams of experts whom Iraq thought would be more willing to accept its contention that it was free of prohibited arms.
"We've had a reality check," Sir John said. "From this, I draw the conclusion that we are facing a policy of lying on an epic scale that recalls only the policies in that respect of earlier dictators in this century. While there will always be, as Lenin put it, useful fools who will rush in to embrace people who pump out this kind of nonsense, I don't think that the U.N.'s general standing in the world is so high right now that it can allow itself to be duped by this kind of thing."
Britain recently organized a conference in London to discuss how civilian goods could be supplied more effectively to Iraqis. Baghdad objected, saying the conference infringed on Iraqi sovereignty.
This weekend, Iraqi newspapers reported that the Government was further cutting rations, citing difficulties with the plan under which limited amounts of oil are sold to pay for essential supplies.

HEADLINE: On My Mind;
Gifts For Israel

BYLINE: By A. M. ROSENTHAL 

 BODY:
On Israel's 50th anniversary, its friends can give the country certain gifts of importance. They can recognize Israel's achievements and take joy from them. And they can accept without denial or flinching the fact that after a half-century Israel's neighbors still want it dead.
 So far, Israel has not received many gifts from my crowd -- journalists. Much of the magazine, newspaper and TV coverage and assessment of Israel -- not all, but too much -- has ranged from delightedly doleful to dolefully despairing.
Israel's economic, societal and scientific successes have been mentioned. But not often is it pointed out that they were attained in the face of decades of hatred and attack from Arab nations and movements. 
 The contrary -- almost always Israel's problems are now being presented as if they are entirely self-inflicted. Arabs are presented as if they are always simply reacting to Israeli refusal to accept their reasonable demands that the Jews just clear out of more territory because it does not really belong to them.
American public support for Israel rises and for Yasir Arafat declines. But U.S. and European journalism is increasingly sympathetic to the Palestinians and unpleasant about Israel.
To each his own vision. To my eyes, and to those of the majority of Americans, Israel is one of history's soaring proclamations of mankind's worth to itself and its Creator.
These days it is not said much anymore, which is a pity, but Israel did indeed begin with nothing much more than sand, hope and belief. And yes, 50 years later it is indeed the Mideast's only democracy, a growing center of science, technology, art, music. Israel is not a dirge -- but a country; how happy the thought.
And I find emotion entirely permissible about Israel's ability to maintain life and progress though its neighbors have imposed an absence of peace for a half-century.
But about dangers to Israeli survival, cool is best. And stepping back coolly we see the realities.
One is that Israel may work out agreement with Palestinians -- if they want it enough to agree to conditions that will give Israel security of borders and the end of terrorism. The agreement would bring respite that could grow into a peace of some years.
But another reality is that agreement on Palestine would not bring permanent peace. Ask ourselves, would Mideast rulers, the worker-merchant "street" and religious and intellectual establishments accept an Israel forever growing in skills and strength -- or in their dreams and desires want Israel extinguished, and work toward the day?
Run them through the mind: Syria, Libya, Egypt, Saudi Arabia, the gulf sheikdoms, the Sudan, Algeria, Iraq, Iran.
The hatred against Israel these countries receive, accept and pass on as heritage and religious obligation -- would it vanish with an independent Palestine or would it continue in them, and in Palestine too?
If Iran and Iraq develop chemical, nuclear and biological weapons, will they strike against Israel? Would other Arabs extend sympathy to Israel -- or dance on rooftops and scream their passion to kill Jews? Would the West take the risk of world war to rescue Israel?
We know the answers. Permanent peace in the Mideast will not come until sufficient Arab peoples replace dictatorship -- fundamentalist, religious, military or terrorist -- with democratic religious and political freedoms.
Then perhaps the Muslim governments will end the feuds among themselves that are the central cause of Mideast wars. Then perhaps they will even try to end the hatred of Israeli existence that infests the Mideast with the threat of war against Israel.
Freedom may happen in the Mideast, as in so many other places. But it will come slowly, fitfully.
Meantime, will Israel stand strong at arms, maintaining military power not for victory over another country but for defense?
Will the U.S. remain a friend or become a harassment? Will some foreign and Israeli Jews push their religious and political hostility against Israeli governments so long and hard that they sap Israel's strength, will power and self-belief, as Israel awaits Arab conversion to democracy?
From friends of Israel, cool questions in themselves are gifts to Israel -- and to one another.

HEADLINE: Stocks Tumble On Worries Of Rate Rise

BYLINE: By JONATHAN FUERBRINGER 

 BODY:
The stock market dropped sharply across-the-board yesterday, as a sudden fear that the Federal Reserve may raise interest rates spread doubt about the durability of Wall Street's strong 1998 rally.
The concern, which led to a rise in interest rates in the bond market, was prompted by an article in The Wall Street Journal that said Fed policy makers were leaning toward raising interest rates. That sent the yield on the 30-year bond, which rises when prices fall, to 6.05 percent. If yields move higher, they could further undermine shares by making stocks look too expensive. 
 Long-term yields, as low as 5.79 percent at the beginning of the month, had been moving higher since then as several governors of the central bank indicated they were worried the economy was not slowing down as expected.
But yesterday's report accelerated that rise. The reported decision -- to change from the Fed's previous position that it should be evenly balanced between the need for a rate cut or a rate increase -- does not necessarily mean the Fed will follow through and tighten monetary policy but makes clear that policy makers still see inflation as the main threat to the long economic expansion. Fed officials declined to comment.
If the shift is true, stock analysts said, it eliminates one of the reasons for this year's stock rally, the possibility that the Fed's next move could be a cut in interest rates.
The stock decline yesterday cut more than 220 points off the Dow Jones industrial average before it recovered late in the day to finish down 146.98, or 1.6 percent, at 8,917.64. It is now down 2.9 percent in the last four sessions.
Other major indexes fell more. The technology-heavy Nasdaq dropped 48.65 points, or 2.6 percent, to 1,820.31. The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index fell 21.36, or 1.9 percent, to 1,086.54, while the Russell index of 2,000 smaller-company stocks finished down 11.82 points, or 2.5 percent, at 468.50. All were at record highs less than a week ago.
Drug companies like Merck and Pfizer; banks, including J. P. Morgan and Citicorp, and technology companies from Microsoft to Intel led stocks lower.
The Fed, along with many economists, has expected the economy to slow this year, partly because of the drag from reduced exports to Asia, where many economies are battling to avert serious downturns in the aftermath of the financial instability that rocked the region last year.
But American economic growth in the first quarter exceeded 3 percent by the estimate of many economists. Although there have been tentative signs of some slowing reported since the Fed's last meeting, including a decline in March employment, it remains unclear whether the combination of low unemployment and strong growth will prove to be a recipe for inflation. Still, there are almost no signs of inflation in the economy now.
Some analysts said they could not figure out the reasons for a Fed switch. Maybe it is some kind of signal to the market. But John Lipsky, the chief economist at Chase Manhattan Bank, said things really had not changed that much. "To me the operative thing is that if growth doesn't slow down, the Fed will raise interest rates." And, he added, that was true before yesterday.
For investors the question is once again one that has popped up often during this market's record bull run: Is this dip just another opportunity to buy stocks for less, or the beginning a longer-term slump?
Stock analysts and strategists were not ready to answer that question, though it was clear that recent market collapses -- including the Oct. 27 record 554.26-point drop in the Dow Jones industrial average -- have been halted by opportunistic investors.
But some analysts did say that at least for the time being there are more negatives weighing on the stock market than there had been earlier as the markets' major indexes surged to record highs and quickly beat growth forecasts for the year.
"At the moment the markets are on hold," said Marshall Acuff, equity strategist at Salomon Smith Barney.
Byron R. Wien, the United States investment strategist at Morgan Stanley Dean Witter, said several other factors had contributed to the market selloff, which began last week. He said the breadth of the market's rally -- which carried much more than the blue-chip stocks higher -- was beginning to narrow, with the number of advancing stocks diminishing.
He also said the disappointing efforts to revive the Japanese economy, capped by a new stimulus plan last week, were worrying investors, as reflected in a 2.3 percent drop in the Nikkei 225 stock average yesterday and a further 1.9 percent decline early today. Economic revival in Japan is necessary for helping the rest of Asia recover from a financial crisis that began last summer.
The key to the near-term outlook, Mr. Wien said, will be the course of interest rates. If long-term rates rise toward 6.5 percent, he said, "the market is in trouble."
Investors have been bidding stocks higher for much of this year, pushing the major indexes up 16 percent or more in less than four months.
Abby Joseph Cohen, co-chairwoman of the investment policy committee at Goldman, Sachs, says she thinks the market is in a trading range for a while. But, she said, "I don't think it is the start of something worrisome."
"We have had a very dramatic rise in stock prices in a very short period of time," she said. "I think that many investors have been looking around and saying that this doesn't go straight up." They will now wait, she said, to see that the economic fundamentals like low inflation and healthy earnings support a further rally. She thinks they do.
This kind of pause occurred at the beginning of both 1996 and 1997. Last year, the Dow average was up 9.9 percent before tumbling in March and April. It rallied to a new high in August, then stalled again before the October drop. The Dow average has since been moving mostly up.
For the time being, many strategists expect the market to be volatile, as investors scrutinize every new economic statistic to determine whether the Fed really must raise interest rates. Signs of the economy finally slowing down would surely ease concerns, giving the market a boost. But data showing further robust growth would send interest rates higher and stocks lower as investors bet the Fed will have to move. The next meeting of Fed policy makers is not until May 19, a long way off for skittish investors.
The May 8 employment report for April should be the first big test of market jitters.

HEADLINE: Giuliani to Trim Spending By 3 Percent (See Asterisk)

BYLINE: By NORIMITSU ONISHI 

 BODY:
Since Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani took office in 1994, the fiscal rites of spring in New York City have focused on arguments over what programs and services to cut from its budget. But this April, a curious and politically sensitive new debate has arisen: How fast is the city's spending growing?
The Mayor said not at all. In January, he said that the city's spending would grow by a mere six-tenths of one percentage point. But after unveiling his executive budget on Friday -- which contained half a billion dollars more in expenditures than it did in January -- Mr. Giuliani said he had actually managed to bring spending down. 
 "The reduction in spending from this year to next year is 3 percent," Mr. Giuliani said on Saturday, suggesting that those who disagreed with him were out "to do a political hit."
But after digesting the innards of the city's $34 billion budget over the weekend, analysts in government, private organizations and on Wall Street said yesterday that the city's spending was going up between 3 percent and 7 percent. But they all agreed that the Mayor was right -- though only technically -- in claiming a 3 percent reduction.
What's going on? The dissonance stems from the city's record $2 billion surplus, which has complicated the way the city closes its books at the end of the fiscal year, on June 30.
And Mr. Giuliani's evident passion about the spending rate reflects the importance he attaches to it, even more so nowadays, as he has taken steps to raise his national profile and to solidify his Republican credentials as a fiscal conservative.
Analysts at Wall Street's two major credit-rating agencies praised Mr. Giuliani's recent stewardship over the city's finances. They said that while city spending in Mr. Giuliani's proposed budget was clearly above the local inflation rate of between 2 percent and 3 percent, it was modest, especially given the spending opportunities provided by the $2 billion surplus.
Moody's Investors Service, which recently raised the city's credit rating, said spending was up between 5 percent and 6 percent. Robert A. Kurtter, a senior vice president, described that as "about average."
Its rival, Standard & Poor's Corporation, had yet to analyze spending in the budget. Todd Whitestone, a managing director, said the Mayor had kept the city's finances on a course that would lead to a credit-rating increase by S. & P.
Of Mr. Giuliani's claim that spending would go down 3 percent, he said, "It's not an accurate picture of what the city is spending year to year."
H. Carl McCall, the State Comptroller, said spending was increasing by 6.5 percent; the city's Independent Budget Office, by 6.2 percent; the Citizens Budget Commission, 7 percent, and the City Council, 3 percent to 6 percent.
So why is the Mayor technically right? Under the generally accepted accounting principles that the city has followed since the fiscal crisis of the mid-1970's, the city must spend this year's budget surplus during this fiscal year. But the Mayor wants to use it in the next fiscal year, mostly to pay interest and principal on the city's debt. So the city will prepay these expenses before this fiscal year ends.
And so, while these payments are used for next year's expenses, they are counted as part of this year's spending -- artificially deflating spending for next year. This accounting procedure was never much of an issue because until last year's $1.4 billion surplus, the city had never recorded such a huge surplus, analysts said. But to have an accurate picture of growth, analysts said that the budget surplus should be factored into the next year's spending.
The analysts said such an accurate picture was necessary to understand and deal with future budget problems, which are now masked by the boom on Wall Street.
When Mr. Giuliani presented his preliminary budget in January, he projected that the city would have a $1.2 billion surplus and that spending would rise by six-tenths of a percentage point. By Friday, a continuing bull market had increased the surplus to $2 billion. That further distorted the spending rate, analysts said, and allowed for a technical spending decrease of 3 percent.
The Mayor's budget director, Joseph J. Lhota, said he had followed the same accounting rules used by previous administrations.

HEADLINE: Shortage of Housing for Poor Grows in U.S.

BYLINE: By MICHAEL JANOFSKY 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 27

 BODY:
Despite a robust national economy, a shortage of affordable housing for poor people -- once a problem found only in cities -- is growing in many suburbs around the country, according to a report by the Department of Housing and Urban Development that will be delivered to Congress on Tuesday.
Examining housing needs from 1991 to 1995, the department found that the number of low-income families who live in suburban areas and require housing assistance increased by 9 percent, bringing their total number to 1.8 million, a growth rate slightly higher than that of families living in urban settings, which rose by 8 percent over the same period. 
 Over all, the report found that the nation's economic growth has done little to ease demand for affordable housing no matter where the working poor live. The data suggest that the number of low-rent apartments decreased by 900,000 over the four-year period, while the number of "very low income" families, those with incomes of less than 50 percent of the area median and who pay as much as 50 percent of their income on housing grew by 370,000. That made the nation's total of such families with "worst case" housing needs 5.32 million, about one seventh of the families who rent.
"The days of stereotyping cities as the locus of housing problems, education problems and crime problems are gone," Housing Secretary Andrew M. Cuomo said in an interview. "Maybe that was true of the 1950's and 1960's. It is not true of the 1990's. Urban problems have become suburbanized."
To offset the disparity between supply of affordable units and people who need them, as shown by the report, the Clinton Administration is seeking $1.8 billion for new spending as part of its $25.8 billion housing budget request for the 1999 fiscal year. The additional money would be used for 103,000 rental housing vouchers, half of which would be given to people coming off welfare, and for a variety of housing and economic development programs.
Congress has not appropriated any new money for housing assistance since 1995, and how receptive lawmakers might be to the department's latest request is unclear, especially with both chambers already debating a $200 billion transportation bill.
But Mr. Cuomo said that with many of the largest cities losing population to adjacent suburbs, members of Congress, especially those from suburban districts, might be more sympathic to a budget request that focuses on the growing problems affecting their districts.
In some metropolitan areas, the number of suburban families in worst-case situations has far surpassed those from the adjacent city. By 1995 in Boston, for example, the department found 18,000 families in a worst-case situation, compared with 65,000 similar families in surrounding areas.
Over all, the report found, the largest increases among families in worst-case situations occurred in the Northeast and in the West, regions experiencing the most acute combination of decreasing supply of affordable units and rising demand.
Housing officials say that the rising demand for low-income housing in suburban regions reflects several major trends, including a rate of job growth that is much faster in suburbs than in cities. The problem with many of those jobs, officials say, is that they pay the minimum wage or only slightly higher, levels not high enough to ease the burden of finding affordable housing.

SECTION: Section D; Page 1; Column 2; Business/Financial Desk

HEADLINE: In the Wings: Euro as Potential Rival to the Dollar

BYLINE: By RICHARD W. STEVENSON 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 27

 BODY:
For the better part of this century the dollar has reigned supreme in global finance, both a symbol of American economic might and a contributor to it. But a new rival to the dollar could eventually prove more powerful than the German mark or the Japanese yen: a single European currency.
This weekend, the 11 nations of the European Union that have chosen to participate in the first stage of monetary union will take a big step toward making the long-planned single currency a reality: They will lock their exchange rates together in preparation for the program's formal start on Jan. 1. 
 The international influence of their new currency, the euro, will ultimately depend on the economic strength of these European partners -- 11 of the European Union's 15 members. And given the high unemployment rates and other problems now afflicting the Continent -- never mind the uncertainties about the willingness or ability of the individual countries to subordinate their needs for the common good -- the new system's success can by no means be taken for granted.
Despite those doubts, there is a growing consensus on both sides of the Atlantic that monetary union will have substantial ripple effects on the United States. And the euro could bring profound changes to an international financial system in which the dollar has been the anchor since a system of fixed exchange rates collapsed in the early 1970's and the dominant currency since the British pound started to lose influence and strength in the 1930's and 40's.
"Although the political, cultural and economic challenges are formidable," an International Monetary Fund study said, "the euro has the potential to reshape European and international financial markets and to transform" the world monetary system.
For the United States, the benefits of a successful euro could include greater political and economic stability on a continent that has spawned world wars. It could also make Europe an easier, less expensive region for American companies to sell their products and services.
But the United States could lose some of the prestige and diplomatic influence that goes with having a dominant currency. Borrowing costs could rise for the Government and companies. Economic growth could be reduced.
Some prominent economists in the United States, like Paul Krugman of the Massachusetts Institute of Technology, say the euro's potential impact on American interests is overstated. And no one is predicting that the euro will any time soon displace the dollar as the standard against which other currencies are measured.
But many economists and some American and European officials said the euro, if properly managed, would quickly establish itself as the undisputed No. 2 world currency.
Given that the combined economies of Europe already rival the American economy in size, economists said there was no intrinsic reason why the euro could not one day achieve parity in global status with the dollar.
"We will move from a purely dollar-centered world to a bipolar monetary world," said C. Fred Bergsten, director of the Institute for International Economics in Washington.
"The euro will move up alongside the dollar as the second key currency and will attain market share pretty close to the dollar within three to five years," Mr. Bergsten forecast. "It will shift the whole structure of the international financial system."
The dollar has far greater weight in global finance than is indicated simply by the size of the American economy. The United States accounted for 26.7 percent of world output in 1995 and 18.3 percent of world trade, roughly equivalent on both counts to the combined 15 national economies in the European Union.
But the dollar accounted for 58.9 percent of the foreign-currency holdings of central banks and governments in 1996, according to the International Monetary Fund. The German mark accounted for 13.6 percent and the Japanese yen, 6 percent.
In foreign-exchange markets, the dollar was the currency being bought or sold in 83 percent of transactions in 1995, compared with 37 percent for the mark and 24 percent for the yen, according to the European Union.
European officials, even as they defend their nascent currency's credibility and potential strength, have been careful not to ruffle American feathers.
"I do not expect brutal changes overnight," said Yves-Thibault de Silguy, the union official in charge of the single-currency project.
Hugo Paemen, the European Union's representative in the United States, said it would be naive to think that a successful euro would not have implications for the balance of global financial power. "If you create a single European currency for 300 million people, it is going to be a very important force in the international financial sector," he said.
The Clinton Administration has played down concern about the effect of the euro on the dollar and American interests generally.
"If the euro works for Europeans, it will work for us," said Lawrence H. Summers, the Deputy Secretary of the Treasury. "It will mean a larger, more rapidly growing market for our products and a stronger partner for the U.S. around the world."
The dollar's standing, Mr. Summers said, will continue primarily to reflect the economic performance of the United States.
"As long as we keep our fundamentals strong, the dollar will do fine," he continued. Any shifts in international currency holdings out of the dollar and into the euro "are likely to be gradual and not to pose a threat to our economy," the Treasury official said.
But since the benefits of the dollar's leadership have been considerable, the United States has much at stake as the euro takes its place in the world financial system. The dollar has long been a safe haven for world investors, enabling the United States to attract vast amounts of foreign capital and to finance borrowing needs at lower interest rates than purely domestic demand for dollar assets would have allowed.
Money from abroad, especially from Japan, helped finance the huge Federal budget deficits of the 1980's and early 90's. And financial crises in Asia last summer and fall sent a flood of money into the United States as investors looked for a safe place to park their money, driving down interest rates and helping set off a wave of mortgage refinancings that reduced housing costs for many Americans.
The American trade deficit, though, has kept rising, and the United States thus needs to keep attracting money from abroad to paper over a huge current-account deficit, the broadest gauge of trade. Many economists consider this imbalance the biggest threat to the nation's long-run prosperity.
The dollar's standing in the world has gone hand in hand with the size and sophistication of American financial markets, helping Wall Street retain its role as the center of global finance. International investors buy dollars and dollar-denominated securities in confidence that they can easily sell them. When Japan's central bank needed dollars earlier this month to buy yen in the open market, supporting the yen's exchange value, it was reportedly able to sell $12 billion of Treasury bonds in New York in half an hour without upsetting the market.
The United States also benefits from the desire of foreigners to hold dollars in cash. By some estimates, the majority of American notes are held abroad. In developing countries and in those experiencing high inflation rates -- as well as among drug smugglers and other criminals -- dollars have become the all-but-official currency. Notes circulating abroad are a semipermanent, interest-free loan to the United States -- indeed, if Washington had to instead borrow the equivalent amount of money, say in Treasury bills, the interest payments would run in the billions annually.
Some economists dismiss the idea that the euro will soon be a serious challenger to the dollar because the dollar's status reflects more than half a century of American might.
Even if the euro does come to rival the dollar, they said, it will not necessarily have any substantial impact on the United States, especially since the country is running budget surpluses and does not need as much foreign capital. In an article this month in Fortune magazine, Mr. Krugman of M.I.T. put the potential loss to the American economy at just one-tenth of a percentage point of economic growth.
But another camp of economists thinks that the results will not be so benign for Americans. Richard Portes of the London Business School and Helene Rey of the London School of Economics said in a paper published last week that the United States could lose up to half a percentage point of annual growth from the establishment of a credible euro while the Europeans could gain half a percentage point of growth. The United States grew 3.8 percent last year, while the 11 nations expected to take part in the euro grew 2.4 percent.
Mr. Portes said their analysis assumed that the euro would stimulate the development of Europe's financial markets, creating a bigger and more liquid market for euro-denominated securities and supporting the euro's use in foreign-exchange transactions. One implication, he said, would be a tilt in the geopolitical balance in Europe's favor.
"Ultimately we're going to have to have a more cooperative and less U.S.-led process in things like international financial crises, where clearly the European voice is much less than its weight in the world economy," Mr. Portes said.

CORRECTION-DATE: May 11, 1998, Monday

 CORRECTION:
An article in Business Day on April 28 about potential effects of the euro, the planned common currency for Europe, misstated a study's estimates of its impact on the economies of the United States and Europe. The study, by Richard Portes of the London Business School and Helene Rey of the London School of Economics, said Europe could gain as much as four-tenths of 1 percent of gross domestic product (not half a percentage point of growth), while the United States could lose as much as a quarter of 1 percent of G.D.P. (not half a percentage point of growth).

HEADLINE: Lulu and Her Friends Are, Therefore They Shop

BYLINE: By JENNIFER STEINHAUER; Jennifer Steinhauer covers retailing for the Business section of The New York Times.

DATELINE: MANCHESTER, Conn.

 BODY:
LULU LEDERMAN sees the world of style in gradations, small subtle characteristics that distinguish a hip piece of clothing from a truly awful one. "There is good preppy and bad preppy," said Lulu, who is 14, as she made her way through the Buckland Hills Mall here.
"Bad preppy is snobby," she said, explaining that striped shirts, khakis and baseball hats from J. Crew are bad preppy, whereas the catalogue's fitted T-shirts and pastel skirts scored points for good preppy. 
 A pair of white chiffon drawstring pants hanging on a rack in an Express store drew further scrutiny. The style was a hit; the fabric, a miss. "You would look, like, 40 in these," Lulu said.
Her friend, Morgan Sokolow, a 16-year-old, nodded in agreement. "Old Navy did them in T-shirt material," Morgan said. "Those were cool."
Red is good, but it has to be really dark red, otherwise it is just horrible. Butterfly patterns are cool, but little flowers are over. Platform shoes are great, but they have to be the right style, otherwise they could, like, make you throw up or something.
Lulu, Morgan and their friend Sarah Thomas, all students at the Loomis Chaffee prep school nearby who gathered recently for a shopping trip, are what Teen-Age Research Unlimited, a market research firm in Northbrook, Ill., refers to as "influencers," a crucial group of teen-agers that embraces fashion trends early on, spends lots of money on them and takes other teen-agers on board with its utter coolness.
This group is not to be confused with the "edge" group, those youngsters who constantly change their look, consider themselves to be antifashion and drop trends as soon as influencers pick them up. Edge teen-agers are the first to pierce. They are moving targets. Adults with products to sell to them cannot hope to keep up.
The research firm identifies two other groups, the "conformers" and the "passives," who also pick up fashion trends, but much later than their more style-conscious peers.
"Conformers are very insecure," said Michael Wood, the director of syndicated research for Teen-Age Research Unlimited. "They make up the bulk of the teen-age population. They are looking for brands and badges to make them feel better about themselves and to get them to the next level."
The passives are last to the party, the sort of students who might be in the chemistry club and are dying to get this high- school business behind them so that they can move on and rule the world a la Bill Gates. Look for them in track pants next year. "A passive is much less interested in fitting in," Mr. Wood said. "They buy trends mainly because that is what is available."
Then there are the Lulus of the world, the bread and butter of marketers.
"These are the channelers, the first cool kids to try these things," Mr. Wood said. "Socially, they are the cream of the crop. They know their status and revel in it. If you can attract them, you have scored. The biggest kiss of death is for you to be a cool brand in the mind of the conformers and not the influencers."
Selling fashion to teen-agers is no small business affair. Generation Y, as this group is sometimes known, spent roughly $4 billion last year on cosmetics, clothing and other fashion items and is considered the most spend-happy generation of young people to come along -- ever.
Teen-agers are savvy and numerous: there are about 30 million in the United States now, and that number is to hit 35 million in 2010, according to the Census Bureau. They have learned how to spend at the sides of their baby-boomer parents. While they may not know which marketing category they fall into, they are happy to hold court for the market researchers who come calling to interview them and thereby place them in a shopping subgroup. In fact, this reporter found these three girls through Delia's, the catalogue company that sells teen-age clothing.
According to surveys, teen-agers make more household buying decisions than their parents' generation did, mostly because Mom and Dad are working and need shopping help. But this group also spends plenty on itself. For every espresso maker and sport-utility vehicle that their parents buy, teen-agers acquire skateboards, wide-legged jeans and little black handbags.
Just look at the rising earnings and stock prices of companies like Wet Seal-Contempo, the Buckle, Pacific Sunwear of California, all of which sell clothes to teen-agers. Even teen-agers in rural areas of the United States are onto the styles of their urban brethren -- and it is for this generation that Delia's and the catalogue Zoe were created.
Unlike the much smaller contingent before them, Generation X, which came of age during a recession, these teen-agers are far more willing to embrace consumerism as an indoor sport. "The message this group is receiving is that the country is in great shape," Mr. Wood said. "The economy is not an issue for their parents, so therefore it is not an issue for them. They are not being told no as much as Generation X was."
Lulu is the kind of girl grandparents refer to when they say "handful." Short with blond hair and a gait that resembles a dance step, she favors tiny tank tops, velvet jackets and Indian-print shirts.
Her friend Sarah is 16 and a German exchange student. She keeps her words to the point and has recently dumped her preppy look for ankle-length synthetic skirts. Don't be surprised to see her reading dark poetry. Morgan has an athletic build and walks through the mall with long, tan legs spilling out of a turquoise, butterfly-patterned mini-skirt.
Here were Lulu's pronouncements on the Express store: "Look at this line," she said, waving her hand toward denim skirts. "It is, like, younger. But then, like, there is the 50-year-old lady stuff over there," gesturing to tunics. "In fact, I've gotta get out of here; it is really starting to bug me."
Out in the mall, the girls pass the Piercing Pagoda. "I pierced my own bellybutton," Morgan said offhandedly.
Lulu added: "I wanted to do my nose.
I already asked. The answer was no."
A stop at Saint Cinnamon, where cinnamon buns were scarfed. Two teen-age boys passed by, and the girls scrutinized them. The guy in the checkered shirt and shorts was deemed cool, but his friend in black jeans failed. "A guy shouldn't wear colored pants," Lulu explained.
Next visit, Sam Goody. "I like everything from Notorious B.I.G. to the 'Little Mermaid,' " Morgan said, while Lulu perused the Jimi Hendrix section. Another boy walked by, this one in giant jeans and a striped shirt. "I like that look," Lulu said. "But not his hair" -- a bottle-dyed blond. "So really, he's not that hot."
She whipped out her parents' American Airlines Visa card to buy two CD's and said: "My parents won't mind. I'll pay them back."
Off to the Gap. Morgan quickly picked out a pair of boot-cut jeans. "For some reason, I like this shirt," Sarah said, touching a simple button-down cotton number. "This is one of those things you buy and it stays in your closet forever."
Back in the mall corridors, they gave American Eagle a miss. "They're a little too earth-crunchy for me," Lulu said. Abercrombie & Fitch, a considerably more expensive version of American Eagle, fared better, although the girls said it was too pricey and better, really, for boys.
Next stop: Contempo Casuals, an outpost of blaring hip-hop music, polyester dresses and trendy hair accessories. The winsome T-shirts, flowing dresses and butterfly-patterned skirts scored points but not purchases. In fact, the girls prefer to buy clothes from the Zoe catalogue, which they can peruse in their dorm rooms. The quality of Zoe clothes is superior, they said.
Sarah nonetheless bought a little pink knit hat and a small purse with a Chinese pattern on one side and an Indian sari fabric on the other.
Over to Hot Topic, a store that sells music-related merchandise and heavy-metal-style clothing and T-shirts that say things like, "If we are not supposed to eat animals, why are they made of meat?"
"OHMYGOD!" screamed Lulu, grabbing a slippery, bright blue metallic dress to try on. Morgan considered a pink marabou bra. And Sarah contemplated black imitation-leather pants.
Standing by the dressing room were three other girls, conformers, who eyed the three influencers with suspicion and a bit of contempt. The conformers' toes were painted sky blue (that was last spring). They were wearing low-slung jeans and carrying plastic Contempo bags. Lulu, Morgan and Sarah ignored them.
Morgan emerged from a dressing room in a red tube top and the fake-leather pants Sarah had eyed. She seemed completely at ease in her PG-13 outfit and looked both totally beautiful and inappropriately sexy. She chewed on a fingernail, walked back into the dressing room and changed.
Sarah came out next in a little white tank top and a black mini-skirt. She crossed her arms and looked more self-conscious. Lulu passed on a pink halter top.
It wasn't cellulite or wrinkles or upper-arm issues that bedeviled these girls -- these were simply not school clothes. But the thrill was shopping beyond the Gap, at a time when a pink marabou bra is somehow a choice. Only if it is, like, perfect.

GRAPHIC: Photos: TRYING IT ON -- From left to right in top photo, Sarah Thomas, 16, Morgan Sokolow, 16, and Lulu Lederman, 14, work their way through the Gap, Contempo Casuals and Hot Topic at the Buckland Hills Mall in Manchester, Conn. Retailers and clothing companies pay close attention to the likes and dislikes of such "influencers," teen-agers with the savvy, money and status to make trends stick. (Photographs by George Ruhe for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: The Pop Life;
Mike Tyson Faces the Music

BYLINE: By Neil Strauss 

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES

 BODY:
From boxing to wrestling to the record business? Mike Tyson is starting his own music company, Tyson Records. In an interview this week, he said the label was putting together a family of rappers, rhythm-and-blues groups and producers.
So far, Tyson Records seems likely to include Protege, a singing group from Baltimore, and Donnie, a 16-year-old singer formerly signed to LaFace Records, a leading rhythm-and-blues label run by Babyface and L. A. Reid. The rap pioneer Eric B. and the former Jodeci member Devante Swing are to be among the musicians working with these artists. For a cohesive, distinctive sound, Mr. Tyson said, he plans to have the producer and writer Bryce Wilson oversee the label's musical production. 
 "I'm into the music scene so intensely," Mr. Tyson said. "For 20 years, I've known about all these rappers: Grandmaster Flash, Grand Wizard Theodore, people a lot of rappers now aren't even aware of."
"I must have been 10 or 11 when I was put away," he continued, referring to his imprisonment for purse-snatching. "And all we had was music. We battled with people and entertained ourselves."
Speaking of the rap world, he said, "We made the industry, but we have no control over the destiny of the music."
At first, Tyson Records was going to be a completely independent label, Mr. Tyson's associates said. But Mr. Tyson decided it needed the resources of an international major label, and has been having discussions with Sony Records, among other companies. Recently, Mr. Tyson hired as an adviser Irving Azoff, a music executive who has worked with the Eagles, Steely Dan and Brian Wilson, and as his lawyer, John Branca, who has worked with Michael Jackson and the Rolling Stones.
"When you talk about the company, people might think, 'What is Tyson doing?' " said Michael (Blue) Williams, a former member of Queen Laifah's management team who was hired to oversee Tyson Records. "But Tyson knows to go get music people to run his music business, and he has found confident people. He has a real vision on how big he can make Tyson Records. And his excitement transfers over to his whole staff."
Though some record labels said they would be eager to work with Mr. Tyson, others have shied away, because his label has yet to prove itself, they said, not because of Mr. Tyson's controversial reputation. Mr. Tyson, the former heavyweight boxing champion, has made news in recent years for a rape conviction, biting a boxing opponent's ear and high-stakes lawsuits over money.
Mr. Tyson and his associates "made some overtures," said Al Cafaro, the chairman and chief executive officer of A&M Records, which recently made a label deal with Shaquille O'Neal. "But we weren't interested, primarily because we've done a number of label ventures recently. And at some point, you have to pick your shots and realize you can't do deals with everybody, because they take a lot of work. This seemed like another situation of a start-up, albeit with a very powerful personality."
In a month or two, Tyson Records plans to showcase its artists to the rest of the music industry. Arrangements are already being made to lease a casino in Las Vegas, Nev., for the occasion.
"You know how Mike's fights are?" Mr. Williams asked. "He feels like everything needs to be that big. And he believes in showcases. The industry is about hype, and if Mike does nothing else he brings hype."
 
New Age, New Rules
To his fans, the New Age composer Yanni may be a peaceful, beautiful music hero. But to many in the concert business, he is a dangerous villain.
In a an open letter to Yanni placed in concert-industry magazines, Danny Zelisko, the president of the Phoenix promotion company Evening Star, wrote, "The very foundation of this business that was intact when you decided to join it is now being shaken by the likes of you."
Yanni's offense is that on his current tour he did not play by one of the cardinal rules. In most cases, a promoter will work with musicians, helping put on their smaller shows for a financial loss in order to reap the rewards if the act goes on to greater success. But on his current tour, Yanni decided both to book and promote most of his concerts through his own management company, cutting out the middle men and keeping their fees for himself. This has been particularly frustrating in a transitional time for the concert business; so far this year, Yanni's tour has made more money than any other act except the Rolling Stones.
"People said just ignore it," Mr. Zelisko, who had worked with Yanni in the past, said about being slighted by him. "But how do you ignore someone coming to your town and waltzing off with three-quarters of a million? We're not here to be swept under the carpet when it's convenient."
Promoters aren't the only ones in the business that feel this way. "Strictly from a building manager's viewpoint, to hear about what Yanni did was disturbing," said Michael Marion, general manager of the Alltel Arena in Little Rock, Ark. "There's an unspoken rule in this business: If I helped you when you were small, when you get big, you're going to help me. But as a building manager, I would not turn him down because of it. I would be unhappy about it, but I have an obligation to the public to book this building."
By not playing by the rules and getting away with it, Yanni's current tour raises questions about the efficiency of the concert business. His manager, Danny O'Donovan, a former concert promoter, said that booking and promoting a tour this way allowed him to set optimum ticket prices that would reduce scalping, to control Yanni's image, to create special stand-alone local advertisements for the concerts, to avoid the sponsorship tie-ins of some promoters and to reduce many concert costs, from hall rental to insurance to merchandise fees. He noted that Yanni worked with local promoters in a few cities where it was advantageous to do so.
"This is the most efficient way to do a tour of this nature if you have only the artist's interest at heart," Mr. O'Donovan said. "Everything is consistent from city to city, and the artist makes more money in every city."
Bob Grossweiner, an editor at the concert-industry magazine Performance, said that it was unusual for an artist of Yanni's stature to do everything himself, but that economically it made some sense.
"When acts reach the arena level, the manager gets something like 15 percent of everything, a lawyer gets maybe 5, a business manager gets 5, the booking agent gets up to 10 percent of concert profits, and everyone else is nit-picking at what they've got," he said. "So at some point, Danny O'Donovan thought Yanni was a sure sell-out, and he could maximize the profits."
"But it threatens the business," Mr. Grossweiner continued. "Why should a promoter or an agent take a risk on a young act if somewhere down the line the act is going turn its head against everyone? If every act did that, then you'd have no one taking a risk on young talent."

HEADLINE: Journal;
Lame Lame Duck

BYLINE: By FRANK RICH 

 BODY:
In his latest standup gig, at the Washington correspondents' dinner last weekend, Bill Clinton made fun of the press for its retraction-stained, three-month romp in Monicaland. He can afford to yuk it up more than he even let on. The scandal, not to mention the media's obsession with it, increasingly looks like the best thing that could have happened to him.
The longer the focus remains on such as Paula Jones -- still the belle of the media ball, astonishingly enough, weeks after her 15 minutes of fame were officially terminated in Federal court -- the less we notice the hollowness of Mr. Clinton's second term. The more we marvel at the sheer prowess of the President's 67 percent job approval rating, the less we ask how he might actually be using this sky-high popularity to lead the country to some national end loftier than deflecting an impeachment proceeding. We know what the press has been doing since January, when it dumped the Pope in Cuba to administer Mr. Clinton's last rites. What has Mr. Clinton been up to? 
 Certainly he has sharpened his already superb skills as an entertainer. The man who didn't show up to explain his ineffectual Iraq policy to a disastrous Columbus town hall meeting has been as ubiquitous as the late, great Henny Youngman as an after-dinner speaker at celebrity fetes, from the Gridiron show to the Time magazine gala to the correspondents' dinner. (He doesn't do press conferences, though.) He has been fearlessly courageous in slaying Joe Camel even as he solicits cash at a fund-raiser thrown by a lawyer likely to gorge on fees from tobacco legislation. He has toured Africa, where he delivered a cost-free apology for American inattentiveness to the genocide in Rwanda on his own watch.
But Mr. Clinton's disingenuousness may have reached a new apogee last week in a Presidential action (or, more accurately, non-action) involving domestic disease, if not distant genocide. Like other such Administration non-sexual affairs since January, this one was forgotten by Washington's revelers within hours, as soon as Paula traipsed back on stage.
The story involved the President's literally last-minute decision not to lift the nine-year-old ban on Federal donations to programs, now in more than 20 states, that reduce the spread of AIDS by providing clean needles to drug addicts. Mr. Clinton didn't make this decision on the intellectually honest basis that he disapproved of such programs or deemed them ineffective. Quite the contrary. In announcing the President's decision, his Health Secretary, Donna Shalala, was flanked by top Government scientists endorsing new research indicating that needle exchange does reduce H.I.V. infection, and does so without encouraging drug abuse. "This is another life-saving intervention" was how Ms. Shalala put it -- even as she simultaneously offered nothing but hot air to aid such interventions.
"It is like saying, 'We acknowledge the world is not flat, but we are not going to give Columbus the money for the ships,' " said Daniel Zingale, of AIDS Action. That's a kind analogy. David Satcher, the Surgeon General, says that 40 percent of new AIDS cases -- some 33 a day -- are due to contaminated needles, 75 percent in the case of women and children.
So why did Mr. Clinton at the last minute fail to put money where his Administration's mouth is? He didn't have the stomach for fighting conservative Republicans in Congress who would predictably demagogue needle-exchange programs as a Government endorsement of heroin use. Though Secretary Shalala had been prepared to defend Federal financing of needle exchange against such attacks by arguing that "science, science, science" had driven the Administration to take action -- according to a "talking points" memo uncovered by The Washington Post -- it was politics, politics, politics in the end that persuaded the Administration to do nothing.
Had President Clinton had the courage of his convictions he might well have faced a fight in Congress. Perhaps he would even have lost it. But if a lame-duck President backed by a 67 percent approval rating and a booming economy won't risk a political battle over saving lives at home in an epidemic, what will he spend his political capital on? Nothing, it would seem, except saving himself.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH
