HEADLINE: Business Travel;
For corporate fliers, airlines' alliances may mean more convenience but with higher fares.

BYLINE: By Edwin McDowell 

 BODY:
AS United Airlines and Delta Air Lines agonize over how to revive an alliance that was almost announced last week, until the Delta pilots' union demanded a seat on the board, business and leisure travelers are wondering what that alliance and two others that have been proposed will mean to them.
Depending on whom one believes, the answer is likely to be more convenient air travel or higher air fares, or possibly a combination of the two. 
In addition to the move by Delta and United, AMR's American Airlines and US Airways have proposed an alliance, as have Northwest Airlines and Continental Airlines.
Ralph Nader, the consumer advo cate, thinks the alliances are likely to shrink competition in the industry, and thus raise fares. So does the Business Travel Coalition, which recently said that businesses and communities "continue to pay dearly" for the 1987 merger between Northwest Airlines and Republic Airlines.
And yesterday Mark Johnson, president of the National Business Travel Association, reflecting a middle ground, said, "While these mergers may create avenues of seamless travel for consumers and marketing economies of scale for the carriers, we do not anticipate that these alliances will alleviate the high cost of air fares that business travelers experience."
While none of the three pending alliances involves a merger -- although that proposed between Northwest and Continental involves equity -- the American Society of Travel Agents also fears they will mean less competition and therefore higher fares. The society and the Coalition for Travel Industry Parity, consisting of 21 travel industry consortiums, are prime movers behind a Congressional bill that would require the Department of Transportation to scrutinize various airline practices that the society and coalition consider anti-consumer.
"But these alliances are not about fares, they're about convenience," said Kenneth P. Quinn, an aviation lawyer and former chief counsel for the Federal Aviation Administration. "Any airline can get you to a connection, if you don't mind how long you wait in the airport. But partners in an alliance time their flights for the convenience of connecting passengers."
When Air Transport World magazine named Northwest and KLM Royal Dutch Airlines co-winners of its airline of the year award for 1997, it cited the ability of those alliance partners to give travelers seamless connections to 400 destinations in 80 countries, in the process transforming the structure of the industry.
From three weekly trans-Atlantic flights a week in 1991 (between Minneapolis and Amsterdam), the partners now fly 162 weekly trans-Atlantic round trips, and this summer will fly 215. The global Star Alliance -- consisting of United, a unit of UAL; Air Canada; Lufthansa of Germany; Scandinavian Airlines System; Thai Airways, and Varig Brazilian Airlines -- integrates well over 6,000 flights a day to more than 600 cities. The United-Delta and American-US Airways alliances would each involve some 4,000 daily flights to hundreds of cities.
The major appeal of alliances to many passengers is the joining of frequent-flier programs, which allow members who belong to both carriers' programs to combine miles from each program. The alliances also bestow reciprocal access to the carriers' airport clubs here and abroad, and give frequent-flier members access to the alliance partner's destinations. Thus US Airways' frequent-flier members will, if the alliance goes through, have access to dozens of American Airlines cities in Latin America and the Caribbean, while American frequent fliers will be able to fly to dozens of cities along the Eastern Seaboard.
If the United-Delta alliance is approved, United passengers in Chicago could fly nonstop on United to Atlanta. If they then had business in Seattle, they could fly nonstop from Atlanta to Seattle on Delta, while still crediting those 2,181 frequent-flier miles to their United account. Otherwise, to collect those miles on United, they would have to fly to Seattle via a connection in Chicago, Denver or San Francisco.
What is behind the alliances is the airlines' determination to avoid another industry bloodbath, such as the one that saw United States carriers lose $13 billion between 1990 and 1994.
During those bleak years, according to Gary Wilson, the chairman of Northwest Airlines, airlines representing one-third of total United States capacity filed for bankruptcy-law protection and more than 100,000 airline employees lost their jobs. That, Mr. Wilson said, "forced all of us to refocus on the fundamental needs of our customers -- namely, convenience and reliability -- as well as the fundamental needs of the business itself."
Central to that refocus is the business traveler, particularly corporate travelers, who as the global economy becomes increasingly integrated will presumably choose the airlines or airline alliances that best satisfy their globe-trotting ways. Because they fly more often and pay higher fares -- sometimes 10 times as much for a seat identical to that of a leisure traveler sitting alongside -- business travelers account for as much as 60 percent of revenues on some airlines. In return, they expect more convenience and more flights, and alliances are an attempt to satisfy those expectations.
Both Delta management and its pilots realize that the carrier will be at a major disadvantage if the alliance with United falls apart, and either of the other pending alliances goes through. For despite being the nation's third-biggest and biggest airlines, respectively, both carriers have notable gaps in their route structures. Delta, for example, has relatively few routes in Asia, which after its economic woes subside is expected to become a huge market for air travel, while United service is thin between the Northeast and Florida, as well as throughout the Southwestern United States. American is the nation's second-largest carrier.
No doubt in recognition of Delta's vulnerability, Leo Mullin, the carrier's chief executive, told delegates to the International Airline CEO Conference in Orlando, Fla., two days ago that Delta would consider the pilots' demand for a seat on the board. "It doesn't mean yes," he said, then quickly added, "But it doesn't mean no."

HEADLINE: 3M's Earnings Decrease by 2%

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: MAPLEWOOD, Minn., April 28

 BODY:
The Minnesota Mining and Manufacturing Company said today that its first-quarter earnings had dropped 2 percent because of Asian economic problems and the strong dollar.
The company, which makes Scotch brand tape, scouring pads and industrial and electronics products, said it earned $400 million, or 98 cents a diluted share, in the three months that ended March 31, compared with $410 million, or 97 cents a share, a year earlier. That was a penny a share below Wall Street's expectations, according to a survey of analysts by the First Call Corporation. 
 "Earnings benefited from solid international growth and effective cost controls worldwide, while the strength of the U.S. dollar and the economic slump in Asia had substantial negative effects," said L. D. DeSimone, chairman and chief executive. Changes in currency exchange rates reduced earnings by an estimated 10 cents a share.
First-quarter revenues slipped to $3.7 billion from $3.71 billion a year earlier.
Shares of 3M rose $2.3125 a share, to $92.50, in New York Stock Exchange trading.

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: Market Place -- A Red Scare in Hong Kong;
China's Prominent Public Stocks Lose Their Luster

BYLINE: By MARK LANDLER 

DATELINE: HONG KONG, April 28

 BODY:
China may now be the undisputed ruler of this former British colony. But some of China's most prominent public companies are feeling less welcome here today than they did before last year's handover.
A year ago this month, investors were surging into banks and brokerage firms to snap up shares of mainland Chinese companies newly listed on the Hong Kong Stock Exchange. Today, the bosses of some of these companies could not draw a crowd if they stood on street corners and hawked the shares themselves. 
 Red chips, as these stocks are called, have caught the blues.
From Citic Pacific to Beijing Enterprises to Guangdong Investments, Hong Kong companies controlled by China have fallen into disrepute among investors. The Bloomberg index of red-chip stocks has plunged 44.37 percent since last October, when the Hong Kong markets began their downward spiral. That slide is even steeper than the broader Hang Seng index, which dropped 29.87 percent in the same period.
"Investors want to see more focused companies," said John D. Pinkel, the head of China research at Merrill Lynch (Asia Pacific) Ltd. "They don't see why there should be a handful of diversified conglomerates in Hong Kong that are in the brewing business, the tannery business and the cement business."
Mr. Pinkel said the success of China Telecom, a cellular-telephone company that went public last October, suggests a new model for red chips -- one focused on a single business rather than straddled across many industries. Today, the company extended its reach by agreeing to acquire a cellular carrier in Jiangsu Province for $2.9 billion.
In a way, the woes of the traditional red-chip companies simply reflect a more realistic view of the Chinese market. Last spring, in the supercharged atmosphere of Hong Kong's impending return to China, investors here clamored for any company even remotely connected to the mainland. Foreign investors joined the rush, buoyed by the belief that China was on the fast lane to becoming an economic superpower.
Now, though, the handover is old news and China's growth prospects seem shakier than they have for several years. In this more sober environment, investors here and in the United States are starting to put Chinese companies under the same microscope they would use on I.B.M. or General Motors.
"The initial rush to buy red chips was from people who were really buying G.D.P. growth in China," said Joseph Ravitch, a banker who heads the communications and media practice at Goldman, Sachs in Hong Kong. "Now, managers and investors appear to be focusing on real earnings and shareholder value."
By those standards, many of the red chips are a sucker's bet. The typical mainland Chinese company based in Hong Kong is less a corporation than a grab bag of assets. Although some show impressive earnings growth, it is usually because they are continually acquiring new assets -- power plants, breweries, toll-road franchises and the like -- from their corporate parent across the border.
The financial reporting standards used by red chips still fall short of American companies. While most are audited by Big Six accounting firms, their balance sheets are frustratingly opaque. Analysts argue that when the supply of fresh assets dries up, the red chips will look like Chinese versions of the ITT Corporation and other conglomerates that went out of style in the United States in the 1970's.
The rationale for starting red chips is still alluring: Register a company in Hong Kong and use it as a repository for assets owned by the Chinese Government or various Chinese municipalities. Then take the company public and drop broad hints to investors that the company's politically well-connected managers will obtain more valuable assets at bargain prices. With China privatizing tens of thousands of businesses, such companies would seem to be a no-lose proposition.
For China, they were also a sure-fire way to raise capital. At the height of red-chip mania last summer, the total market capitalization of mainland Chinese companies on the Hong Kong Stock Exchange was $60 billion. One of the hottest stock offerings, Beijing Enterprises, went public last May at $12.48 a share (Hong Kong), or $1.61 (United States), and promptly zoomed up to $64.75 a share (Hong Kong), or $8.41. It has since settled at $17.20 a share (Hong Kong), or $2.23.
As with so many other red chips, investors simply began to take a closer look at Beijing Enterprises. The company, which is controlled by the Beijing city government, owns everything from hotels and food companies to the airport highway. But the businesses have little relation to one another and scant prospects for synergy. Moreover, analysts said the Beijing city government was rife with corruption and had mismanaged many of these assets.
"This company is going to disappoint people," said one analyst, who like others, requested anonymity for fear of offending Chinese officials.
Then there is the problem of management. Typically, the parents of these companies -- whether they be the Beijing Government or the State Ministry of Posts and Telecommunications -- chose the top executives of red-chip companies based on personal ties rather than their skills.
Such connections, called "guanxi" in Chinese, are essential in the early stages of a company's life, when it is essentially cherry-picking assets from its parent. But as the supply of fresh assets is exhausted, the company cannot rely solely on connections; it must actually begin to manage its businesses.
Some of the more mature red chips have tried to get around this challenge by buying into companies outside the mainland. But that can be expensive, since the companies borrowed heavily to make acquisitions before the Asian currency collapse and are now paying sky-high interest rates.
Consider Citic Pacific, one of the most established red chips. The company's parent, the China International Trust and Investment Company, was set up by the Beijing Government in the late-1970's with the support of Deng Xiaoping. The parent company, in turn, started Citic Pacific in the 1980's and listed it on the Hong Kong Stock Exchange. Citic Pacific rolled up seven consecutive years of increased per-share earnings by buying property, Hong Kong car dealerships, and a stake in Cathay Pacific Airlines. But, analysts said, it took on too much debt.
Last month, Citic Pacific reported 1997 earnings that fell well below the expectations of most analysts because of its increased debt payments. Citic's chairman, Larry Yung, said in a news conference that the company would face a "difficult" year in 1998. He declined a request for an interview.
Mr. Yung himself symbolizes the changing world of red chips. When he took the helm of Citic Pacific in 1987, he was the epitome of a well-connected mainland Chinese. His father, Rong Yiren, founded Citic's parent company and left in 1993 to become vice president of China. Mr. Yung became the steward of the Hong Kong Jockey Club.
But Mr. Rong retired from China's vice presidency earlier this year and Mr. Yung suddenly found himself fielding questions about whether Citic would thrive without a father in high places. Moreover, Citic's parent company, which had enjoyed the status of a state ministry ever since its founding, was stripped of that status in a recent Government reorganization. Now, Mr. Yung contends that Citic's success has very little to do with its connections to China.
Several of the red-chip companies are pinning their hopes on the ambitious economic changes being proposed by China's new Prime Minister, Zhu Rongji. Some Chinese officials have indicated that Beijing will spend more than $750 billion over three years on roads, bridges and other infrastructure projects. The red chips, with their vaunted connections, are hoping to garner lucrative contracts to build or manage these projects.
Cosco Pacific, for example, could benefit from increased cargoes flowing between China's ports, according to Kelvin Wong, the deputy managing director. Cosco, which is controlled by China's Ministry of Communications, owns stakes in container ports in Shanghai and three other cities. Mr. Wong recently traveled to the United States with other red-chip executives to sell investors on the idea that a free-spending Beijing will restore the luster of red chips.
But some experts have their doubts. "I would be skeptical of the notion that China will spend lavishly on infrastructure projects, given the fiscal constraints," said Miron Mushkat, an economist at Natcom Asia in Hong Kong.
Beyond Government spending, some experts wonder whether Beijing will rethink the whole concept of red chips. Instead of championing conglomerates as South Korea did with its "chaebols," China may instead encourage the development of many streamlined, single-industry companies -- as Taiwan has done.
"It is essentially a debate between the Taiwan model and the Korean model," said Gary Greenberg, the managing director of Van Eck Global Asset Management in Hong Kong, "And right now, the Taiwan model looks a lot better."
For proof of this, analysts point to the success of China Telecom, which owns the cellular telephone franchises for Guangdong and Zhejiang provinces. The company, which is controlled by the Ministry of Posts and Telecommunications, went public last October in the teeth of the Asian currency crisis. Nevertheless, the stock has risen 20 percent since the beginning of the year, and the company currently has a market value of more than $24 billion.
China Telecom offers nothing but mobile-phone service. But it has more than four million customers and the prospect of millions more. The franchise in Jiangsu, which China Telecom agreed to buy today, will give the company an additional 450,000 subscribers and a potential market of 70 million people. Goldman, Sachs, which raised $4.5 billion in China Telecom's initial public offering, predicts the company will have 10 million subscribers across China by 2001.
At the rate China Telecom is growing, Mr. Pinkel of Merrill Lynch said, it could eventually compete head-to-head on its home turf with major overseas cellular companies, such as the AT&T Corporation or Germany's Deutsche Telekom. That will be critical, since China will have to open up its telecommunications and other domestic markets if it hopes to gain entry into the World Trade Organization.
Mr. Pinkel said at least one red chip was mimicking China Telecom's model of simplicity. Guangdong Investments, a diversified conglomerate controlled by the Guangdong provincial government, recently spun off its brewing and tanning companies. Guangdong Investments still owns transportation assets and is building roads and bridges in its home province.
Few things happen overnight in China, of course, and several analysts said the Government had not decided whether to phase out diversified conglomerates in favor of more focused companies. But investment bankers in Hong Kong said China was considering offering stock in one of its most prominent single-industry companies, the state broadcaster, Chinese Central Television.
Given the success of China Telecom, such an offering would probably take off. And it would lend credence to the argument that red chips need to look more like blue chips if they want to win back investors.

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: BONDS;
Continuing Fears About Rates Send Treasuries a Bit Lower

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
The prices of Treasury securities were slightly lower yesterday, keeping the yield on the 30-year bond at the highest level in nearly two months, as investors remained wary of a possible interest-rate increase by the Federal Reserve.
Donald Fine, chief market analyst at Chase Asset Management, said "bond market feathers remain ruffled" by reports that the central bank at its March meeting on monetary policy adopted an inclination to make its next move on rates an upward one. 
 The 30-year Treasury bond slipped 8/32, to a price of 100 26/32, while its yield, which moves in the opposite direction, rose to 6.07 percent, from 6.05 percent on Monday, when bond prices posted their biggest losses in more than a half year.
Yesterday, the Treasury sold $13 billion of new two-year notes, following a heavy flow of new corporate supply last week. Today, it will sell $11 billion of five-year notes, which were yielding 5.766 percent in when-issued trading late yesterday.
The results of the two-year auction were solid, as expected, analysts said, with the notes awarded at a yield of 5.667 percent.
At first glance, two economic reports yesterday may have provided some evidence for those who fear that the Fed will raise rates in an effort to slow the economy and head off inflation.
The Commerce Department said that orders for durable goods rose four-tenths of 1 percent in March. But the rate of increase was lower than in February, and Mr. Fine noted that for the first quarter as a whole, orders were actually lower.
While durable goods orders may not be roaring ahead, the same cannot be said about consumer confidence, which rose in April after dipping a bit in March.
Other data coming this week include the Government's release tomorrow of its estimate of first-quarter growth in gross domestic product, as well as the Employment Cost Index, the broadest measure of changes in wages and salaries.

HEADLINE: Japan, Sensing a U.S. Slight, Is Soothed a Bit by Albright

BYLINE: By STEVEN ERLANGER 

DATELINE: TOKYO, April 28

 BODY:
With Tokyo upset that President Clinton will not stop here on his trip to China in June, Secretary of State Madeleine K. Albright went out of her way today to emphasize firm United States-Japanese ties, toning down American criticism of Japan's economic recovery efforts and praising the Government's for what she called its boldness and courage.
Ms. Albright made a point of visiting Japan herself en route to China, aides said, and is considering a visit here to brief Japanese officials after Mr. Clinton's talks in Beijing, the first visit by an American President to China since the 1989 crackdown in Tiananmen Square. 
 In February 1989, when President Bush went to Beijing, he also visited Tokyo. But the Chinese asked Mr. Clinton to stop only in China, because President Jiang Zemin stopped only in the United States when he went to Washington last October, and Mr. Clinton agreed.
Japanese officials have politely made their unhappiness clear to Washington, and the American Ambassador, Thomas E. Foley, has stressed to Ms. Albright and the White House that Mr. Clinton's pursuit of a new relationship with China should not come at the expense of Japan, America's loyal Asian ally.
American officials have explained that Tokyo also wants better relations with Beijing, and has encouraged Washington to strengthen its own ties with China. They also note that Mr. Clinton has visited Japan twice, in 1993 and 1996.
Still, Ms. Albright's position today was intended to bolster Japanese confidence, and at a news conference and a speech to students at Sophia University, she concentrated on the United States-Japan relationship and its importance for Asian security.
Ms. Albright's praise for Prime Minister Ryutaro Hashimoto's $128 billion package of economic stimulation contrasted with Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin's more measured reaction on Friday.
"Generally we see this is as a bold and important step forward by the Government," Ms. Albright said at a joint news conference with Foreign Minister Keizo Obuchi.
When it was suggested that her endorsement seemed "lukewarm," Ms. Albright objected, saying: "I in no way hope that my sentences or words were taken to be lukewarm. I used 'positive' and 'important' and 'courageous' and 'bold.' That's about as hot as I can make it."
But off the record, senior American officials express concern about Japan's economy, saying the program of Government spending, tax cuts and incentives is too small, comes too late to reassure the financial markets, and will not provide all the growth the Hashimoto Government estimates.
While Japanese officials say the package should stimulate growth of about 1.9 percent in the gross domestic product this year, American officials say the figure will be closer to 1.2 percent. Without the package, however, they fear Japan will slip into a recession.
Mr. Rubin and other American officials are calling for deeper structural changes, including, as Mr. Rubin said, "measures to strengthen Japan's financial system and open and deregulate its economy, to help establish a basis for long-lasting, domestic, demand-led growth."
Ms. Albright made similar points in private, her aides said.
But she also emphasized that she would meet Mr. Obuchi again next week in London, at a meeting to prepare for a summit meeting of the Group of 7 industrial countries, plus Russia. Mr. Clinton and Mr. Hashimoto will meet a week later at the summit meeting, she noted.
On Wednesday, she flies to Beijing to prepare the Clinton visit.
In her speech today, she criticized Chinese rights policies, saying: "We must continue to speak with clarity. For while some Chinese dissidents have been released to exile in recent months, the Chinese Government's repression of dissent and religious freedom has not ceased."
At the same time, she noted that "this year has seen hopeful stirrings of a dialogue among China's students, scholars and officials about the need for political and economic change to go together."

HEADLINE: Rise Seen in Durable Goods And Consumer Confidence

BYLINE: Bloomberg News 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 28

 BODY:
Consumer confidence rose to nearly its highest level in three decades during April, and orders and shipments for durable goods increased in March, according to reports released today.
The Conference Board's consumer confidence index rebounded to 136.7 points in April from 133.8 in March, putting it just below the 137.4 reported for February, which was the highest since October 1968. 
 And the Commerce Department reported that factory orders for durable goods increased in March, led by record demand outside the volatile transportation industry. Total orders rose four-tenths of 1 percent in March, after falling a revised eight-tenths of 1 percent in February, while orders excluding automobiles and airplanes rose nine-tenths of 1 percent last month.
The reports suggested that the economy's growth remains robust -- perhaps so robust that the Federal Reserve will move to head off inflation by raising interest rates.
"The economy is red hot," said Sung Won Sohn, chief economist at the Norwest Corporation in Minneapolis. "The U.S. economy is going to cool down, or the Fed is going to have to do it for us."
Based on today's durable goods report, economists at Standard & Poor's MMS in Belmont, Calif., raised their estimate of first-quarter gross domestic product growth to a 3.7 percent annual rate, just below the 3.8 percent growth rate posted for all of last year.
Concern increased this week that Federal Reserve policy makers might raise the overnight bank lending rate because they see few signs of an expected slowdown in growth. At their meeting on March 31 they reportedly adopted an inclination toward increasing the overnight bank lending rate.
The Fed has been counting on growth to slow as Asian demand for American products declines. Though exports have slowed, so far there is little evidence that the broad economy has cooled.
The consumer confidence numbers suggest that the economy could gain strength. A Conference Board index measuring consumer expectations for the next six months rose to 115.5 in April from 107.9 in March.
The durable goods report showed that factory shipments of such goods, those designed to last three or more years, rose 1.4 percent in March after increasing 2.9 percent in February.
Business spending on capital equipment rose 2.5 percent in March, following a 1.8 percent increase in February. Shipments of production machinery rose by more than 30 percent in the first quarter of 1998 -- the largest gain in almost 15 years, according to MMS.
Transportation orders, when adjusted for seasonal fluctuations, fell in March, though unadjusted figures showed growth, suggesting the seasonal figures were skewed. And the Boeing Company, the world's largest aircraft maker, reported a rise in March orders to 48 planes from nine in February.

HEADLINE: PUBLIC LIVES;
Labor Leader as a Fiery Country Preacher

BYLINE: By DAVID FIRESTONE 

 BODY:
SOMEONE handed James Butler a megaphone the other day when he was preaching to a rally of freshly laid-off union members, and a few old-timers smiled. He held it up, awkwardly, but it was obvious that a few batteries added nothing to his own extraordinary amplification, and he quickly dropped it.
"My voice is like one of those trumpet voices you hear in the churches in the clay hills of Carolina," he explained later. "You can't get up there with that soft tone. You've got to dramatize to get across. You've got to be a trumpet." 
 For the last few years, Mr. Butler has been pretty much the entire brass section of the municipal labor movement in New York City, using his position as president of Local 420, the hospital workers' union, to blare at the muted strings of the other city unions. He has condemned District Council 37, the umbrella group of municipal unions, for selling out his local at the behest of Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani, and last week his dire predictions seemed to come true when the Mayor laid off 905 hospital workers, most of whom belonged to Local 420. Another 600 layoffs are scheduled for this summer.
His fury renewed by the Mayor's move, Mr. Butler has rediscovered some of the old fire that has long set him apart from less-colorful labor leaders. He has led daily pickets at Harlem Hospital and at the home of Dr. Luis R. Marcos, president of the city's hospitals agency. And last weekend, standing tentatively with his two artificial knees, he took many of his members to a dawn protest vigil at Gracie Mansion. The rallies are reminiscent of his heyday leading protests in the 1970's against fiscal-crisis layoffs, and he is fully in his element at their helm.
"The Mayor is only thinking about a new Yankee Stadium, or Shea Stadium with an electric roof," he shouts in a blurry Southern accent bred in Tampa, Fla., shaving off the tops of consonants and mashing together vowels. In full cry, "Giuliani" becomes "Jewel-yana," as in "We're going to bring Jewel-yana to his knees," an unlikely vow that nonetheless generates a huge response.
Mr. Butler, 65, seems so comfortable leading the rallies, in fact, that many labor leaders wonder how much he really did to stop the layoffs that produced them. The Giuliani administration put the union on notice in 1995 when it reached agreement with District Council 37 not to lay off any city employees except the hospital workers, because it was hoping to sell several city hospitals. When Stanley Hill, the council's executive director, agreed to exempt the hospital workers from the no-layoff pledge, an enraged Mr. Butler said Mr. Hill had double-crossed his local.
BUT in the intervening three years, Mr. Butler did little to prevent the layoffs or to prepare his workers for them. City officials say they offered to make severance payments to his members if they would resign, or to transfer them to other agencies, but Mr. Butler refused.
"His union is in deep trouble," said Howard Berliner, a professor of health policy at the New School for Social Research. "It's been pretty obvious that Giuliani has wanted to downsize the hospitals, and it seems to me that Butler could have prepared his union for this a little bit better."
Mr. Butler said he rejected severance as bribery, concerned that many of his members -- who work as nurses' aides, housekeepers and kitchen employees -- would reach for the lump-sum payments without realizing they were giving up their jobs. "I was not going to take severance pay," he said. "I told them, you do what you've got to do, and I'll do what I've got to do."
Municipal labor in New York City has been so docile in recent years that Mr. Butler's stubbornness seems almost quaint by comparison, and his methods are inspired more by the civil rights movement than by more modern union efforts to retrain for a changing economy. The son of a high school teacher and a nurse, he grew up in Tampa hoping to become a doctor, but instead rose through the union's ranks after working as a nurse's aide at the old Fordham Hospital, acutely sensitive to life at the bottom of a hospital's ladder. Almost all of Local 420's 11,000 members are black or Hispanic, and his is certainly the only municipal union with its own gospel choir and "Freedom Bus" that travels the country to help out workers in difficulty.
His union's offices are on 125th Street in Harlem rather than at the council's headquarters downtown, and he has set his union apart from the others for his 25 years in office, a policy that has often led to clashes with his superiors. In 1979, Victor Gotbaum, Mr. Hill's predecessor, charged that Mr. Butler had abused his expense account; the charges were withdrawn, but Mr. Gotbaum still says Mr. Butler -- who makes $120,000 a year, five times more than most of his members -- is authoritarian, and questions his ethics.
Mr. Butler says Mr. Gotbaum never liked him because he kept his local independent, and says Mr. Giuliani is out to get him because he supported the Rev. Al Sharpton and then Ruth W. Messinger in last year's election.
"My members are being lynched because Giuliani doesn't like Jim Butler," he said, his eyes drifting downward in contemplation. "But Jim Butler is not a yes-yes man. I'm not going to sit back while my members are at the kitchen table, seeking out help, reading the 27th Psalm."

GRAPHIC: Photo: James Butler, head of Local 420, the hospital workers' union, at its office in Manhattan. (Librado Romero/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: 8% Revenue Growth Seen for Chips in '98

BYLINE: Dow Jones 

DATELINE: SAN JOSE, Calif., April 28

 BODY:
The worldwide semiconductor market is experiencing its third consecutive lackluster year, with industry revenue projected to grow 8 percent, to $159 billion, this year, according to Dataquest.
In 1997, revenue rose 3.5 percent, to $147 billion, after declining 6.3 percent the year before. 
 One of the main culprits remains low prices for dynamic random access memory chips, or D-RAM's, according to Joe Grenier, vice president and director of Dataquest's Semiconductor Device programs.
"The Asian financial crisis, low D-RAM pricing and D-RAM overcapacity, and the general overcapacity and pricing pressures that exist in other product segments will hamper semiconductor revenue growth this year, although some product segments will achieve robust double-digit growth rates," he said.
In 1999, however, the worldwide semiconductor industry will return to strong growth, with revenue expected to rise 18 percent from projections for 1998, to more than $188 billion, Dataquest said.

HEADLINE: From Welfare Roll to Child Care Worker

BYLINE: By TAMAR LEWIN 

 BODY:
Many day care centers, especially for-profit chains, are hiring welfare recipients to care for young children.
In a survey of 158 centers in five cities, the Center for the Child Care Work Force, a nonprofit research and advocacy group in Washington, found that 35 percent of the centers employed welfare recipients. 
 But 80 percent of the for-profit chains, the fastest-growing segment of the day care industry, employed welfare recipients as against 40 percent of the independent, nonprofit centers, 30 percent of the church-sponsored centers and 20 percent of the independent, for-profit centers.
"For-profit child care has a history of going after low-wage workers," said Deborah Phillips, an author of the study. "They used to go after grandparents, and now they're going to welfare recipients. They are hired primarily as the lowest-paid assistants at centers with the lowest wages. Absent training and anything like a decent wage, this solution to welfare reform and need for child care is unlikely to provide women a route to self-sufficiency."
The Federal welfare overhaul law requires recipients with young children to find work, and that has increased the demand for both child care services and jobs for many women with little education or job training. Many states have responded to those dual needs by putting those women to work in child care centers, raising concerns both for the women hired and the children they care for.
The centers that pay the lowest wages are the ones most likely to employ welfare recipients. Fewer than half the centers that hire welfare recipients offer on-site training, and only 1 in 5 offers the kind of college-credit training required for the better-paying child care jobs.
"These women are just thrust into classrooms," said Ms. Phillips, who is also director of the National Academy of Science's Board on Children, Youth and Families. "If this was first-grade teachers we were talking about, I don't think we'd consider doing this."
The new study is based on a sample of child care centers in Atlanta, Boston, Detroit, Phoenix and Seattle. In 1988, the center did an in-depth study of staffing and quality at 227 centers in those cities, finding that the best child care was in nonprofit centers, and those with the highest wages, lowest turnover, best training and fewest children assigned to each teacher.
The centers in the new study represent those from the original sample that were still operating and willing to participate in the follow-up. The 70 percent that survived and were included in the new study, the center said, tended to be the better ones.
But even among these centers, wages and turnover remain problems. The average turnover rate was 31 percent, with one-fifth of the centers reported losing half or more of their teaching staff in the past year. Turnover in the for-profit chains was 45 percent, compared with 20 percent in the centers accredited by the National Association for the Education of Young Children.
Centers that pay higher wages have less turnover. But the average child care worker earns less than a parking lot attendant. Entry-level child care jobs pay $6 an hour, or $10,500 a year, a rate that, adjusted for inflation, has increased just a penny an hour over the last decade, and one that keeps many child care workers below the Federal poverty line, $12,830 for a family of three. Even child care teachers in the highest-paid category -- mostly people with college degrees and early childhood training -- earn $10.85 an hour, or $18,988 a year, less than the $19,656 average salary of working women with a high-school diploma.
The decade between the two studies was a time of increased public funding for low-income families' child care, through the Child Care and Development Block Grant. For-profit chains tripled their revenues from public subsidies over the last decade, the study found, while independent nonprofit centers experienced a 4 percent decrease in revenue from public subsidies.
The center would not say which for-profit chains were included in the study.

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
French-German Feud Dampens Euro Enthusiasm

BYLINE: By EDMUND L. ANDREWS 

DATELINE: FRANKFURT, April 28

 BODY:
With only a few days remaining before European leaders approve the final preparations for a single European currency, the institution that is supposed to be in charge is still waiting for its leader and its marching orders.
Based in a Frankfurt skyscraper called the Euro Tower, the European central bank does not yet officially exist. But it already has a staff of 400 economists and technical experts, who have been diligently laying the groundwork for a pan-European monetary policy. 
 If the currency, the euro, is introduced on schedule next Jan. 1, the bank will become the European equivalent of the Federal Reserve in the United States. It would have supreme authority over the new currency, which means that it would control interest rates and, to a great extent, the growth rates of 11 nations from Spain to Germany.
Right now, though, the future bank remains caught in a crossfire between France and Germany, the two anchor members of the new monetary union. After months of bickering, the two countries are still fighting over rival candidates to lead the bank and over basic questions on how it should be run.
Both matters were supposed to have been settled long ago. Instead, they have festered and now threaten to sour the celebration of hard-won unity in Brussels this weekend.
If European leaders fail to announce a bank president by Saturday, the deadline they set for themselves long ago, they are likely to undermine the new currency's credibility and raise questions about the project's future. Even if they do reach a last-minute deal, which is likely, the underlying tensions are likely to reappear time and again as the bank gets down to business.
"For politicians, this kind of thing can be very, very embarrassing," said Holger Schmieding, an economist who follows euro issues for Merrill Lynch in London.
Behind the scenes, economists, statisticians and computer experts are rushing to set up the structure for the new central bank. They work at a forerunner organization called the European Monetary Institute, and their number has increased over the last year to 400 from about 250 and is expected to reach 500 by January. Yet before it can make act, the central bank can only wait for a president and a council who will make the real decisions.
Created four years ago by the European Union, the institute has hired staff members from central banks around Europe. They have prepared reams of studies on policy questions, supervised the designing of new bills and coins, and overseen the existing system under which European countries coordinate their exchange rates.
Now the pace is accelerating. Under the plans for monetary union, the institute will be legally converted into the new central bank later this year and will set monetary policy for all 11 countries. National currencies will remain in circulation for three more years, and will be entirely replaced by euros in 2002.
The new institution will control the basic money supply. It is also to develop a new system for conducting foreign-exchange operations. And it is to be responsible for setting up a Europe-wide system for payments and settlements between financial institutions.
Finally, it will supervise the production of euro banknotes, which will be printed by mints in each country.
But feuding over the presidency and the bank's priorities has gotten worse as European leaders have gotten closer to the point of no return. Almost every country except France has long assumed that the first bank president would be Willem Duisenberg, known generally as Wim, who was head of the Dutch central bank until last year and is now president of the European Monetary Institute.
But France has insisted that a Frenchman get the top job, as compensation for allowing the central bank to be based in Frankfurt rather than Paris. The French candidate is Jean-Claude Trichet, currently head of the Bank of France.
Both men share the basic economic philosophy of the Bundesbank, and their monetary policies have moved in lockstep with those of Germany. Indeed, Mr. Trichet followed that policy even when it pushed the value of the French franc so high that it crippled exports and increased unemployment.
"In terms of economic ideology, they are almost indistinguishable," said David Miles, professor of economics at the University of London.
Political leaders in both countries are convinced that the issue is important. French officials, arguing that they are entitled to name a bank president, have insisted that the central bank needs to be more accountable politically and that it should place a priority on increasing employment as well as simply preserving price stability.
That is anathema to German leaders, and the Maastricht treaty that established the ground rules for monetary union seems to solidly take the German view. Under the treaty, the new central bank is to be totally independent from political influence and is only allowed to consider the issue of price stability when it charts monetary policy.
German central bank officials gave icy approval last month to the introduction of the euro, but not before firing off a long list of criticisms at countries like Italy and Belgium for failing to push through real fiscal reform.
The choice of a bank president is not the only subject of dispute. European leaders must also name members of the central bank council. Under the law, the council will consist of the central banker from each of the 11 members as well as 6 other people. Depending on which countries receive the seats, the balance of power could tip slightly in favor of Germany or France.
Finally, the countries are sparring over a separate advisory group known as the Euro-X council. This group, conceived at France's insistence, consists of central bankers from each country and is to meet regularly to evaluate the bank's policies.
Financial analysts are watching for signs that the Euro-X council will turn into a standing organization, with a staff that could generate popular and political pressure on the bank.
"My guess is that Duisenberg will be named president, and that it will be for eight years," Mr. Schmieding of Merrill Lynch said. "But the French will have to be given a lot in return."

HEADLINE: Surging Economy Is Lifting Social Security, U.S. Finds

BYLINE: By RICHARD W. STEVENSON 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 28

 BODY:
Even before the Clinton Administration and Congress settle on the politically delicate steps needed to insure Social Security's long-term health, the strong economy has bolstered the retirement system's financial condition, the Government reported today.
The Administration said the latest review of Social Security's books showed that higher-than-expected economic growth, low inflation and low unemployment had combined to extend the retirement system's solvency by three years, to 2032, from the date projected last year. 
 President Clinton said the news, while welcome, should not divert the nation from taking whatever steps are necessary to make certain that Social Security can withstand the coming retirement of the 76 million strong baby boom generation.
Today's trustees report does not fundamentally change Social Security's outlook, which is being shaped by the aging of the population more than by short-term economic performance. But it shows how sensitive Social Security is in the long run to unanticipated economic changes, and provides ammunition to both defenders of the current system and advocates of broad changes.
Even with the new projections, Social Security will be able to pay only three-quarters of promised benefits after the system exhausts all its reserves in 2032.
But defenders of the current Social Security system said the news, contained in a report by Social Security's trustees, suggested there should be no rush to adopt any sweeping changes, like the proposals to create private investment accounts as part of Social Security, an idea that has emerged as the main ideological issue in the debate.
John Rother, the legislative director of the American Association of Retired Persons, said the report confirms that Social Security "can be strengthened with relatively modest reforms provided we act prudently within the next few years."
The Government also reported that Medicare, the health care program for the elderly, would remain financially sound until 2008, reflecting changes made to Medicare's hospital insurance program as part of last year's balanced-budget deal.
Before the budget deal, the Government projected that Medicare's Hospital Insurance Trust Fund would be depleted in 2001. Today's report was slightly less optimistic about Medicare than studies by Medicare's chief actuary in September and by the Congressional Budget Office in January, both of which said the hospital trust fund would remain healthy until 2010.
Since January, when Mr. Clinton proposed that the nation spend this year debating how best to address Social Security's looming problems, a wide-ranging debate has emerged over whether the system can be shored up through incremental steps like raising the retirement age and limiting cost-of-living increases, or whether Social Security should be transformed to allow individuals to seek higher returns by investing some of their payroll taxes in the stock market.
The report said that the 12.4 percent payroll tax, split between workers and employers, would more than cover benefit payments through 2013, rather than through 2012 as projected in last year's report. Payroll taxes and the interest paid on reserves accumulated in Social Security's trust funds will cover benefits through 2021, today's report said, an improvement of two years from last year's projection.
In the ensuing years, as the ratio of working people to retired people declines, Social Security will eat into its reserves, exhausting them by 2032 under the new projection, rather than in 2029 as predicted last year.
The projections are based on a middle-ground set of economic assumptions that the trustees said provides their best estimates of the program's financial future. A more optimistic set of assumptions showed the program remaining solvent indefinitely, while a more pessimistic scenario showed the trust fund being exhausted by 2022.
To proponents of changing Social Security, the report was more evidence that the current system is unsustainable and that now is the time to shift toward a more market-based system that gives individuals more opportunity to reap higher returns.
The report "confirms that Social Security is in crisis and cannot be sustained in its present form," Daniel J. Mitchell and Gareth G. Davis of the Heritage Foundation, a conservative research group, said in a statement. "The only solution is to give workers the freedom to set up private accounts."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Among officials discussing the new Social Security data with President Clinton yesterday were the budget director, Franklin D. Raines, second from right, and Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin, right. (Stephen Crowley/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Commercial Real Estate;
'Twas a Looker in Its Day And Still Draws the Eye

BYLINE: By DAVID W. DUNLAP 

 BODY:
"Look," the building said.
Needlessly. In 1950, there was no way to miss the new office tower at 488 Madison Avenue, across 51st Street from St. Patrick's Cathedral. With two miles of ribbon windows sandwiched between undulating, round-edged layers of glazed white brick, it was a dazzling wedding cake quite unlike anything New York had ever seen. 
 Enormous letters affixed to the summit of the 23-story tower spelled out "Look Building," for the prime tenant, Look magazine, the mass-circulation flagship of Cowles Communications.
The letters are long gone (though they survived longer than the magazine, which folded in 1971), but otherwise 488 Madison Avenue has regained much of its gleam under a three-year exterior renovation by the architectural firm of Hardy Holzman Pfeiffer Associates that is nearing completion.
What makes the renovation noteworthy is that a white-brick postwar facade has been rejuvenated instead of being obliterated in the name of modernization, as almost any owner would have done not long ago.
Malcolm Holzman, the partner in charge of the renovation, said the goal was to "keep the building as it is and not make it into something it can never be."
Ned Kaufman, an associate director of the Municipal Art Society and a leading advocate of the preservation of modern architecture, said the renovation would be "a very valuable and important model." The society's headquarters are at 457 Madison Avenue, cater-corner to the building.
"The owners and architect saw past the grime and reached back to the real character of that building," Mr. Kaufman said. "The changes they made were small, but they brought it back to life. It just gives pleasure to everyone who walks past."
What most bedeviled the building were water leaks, particularly around the terraces formed by the many setbacks. About 20 percent of the brickwork had to be replaced. The original slate windowsills, some of which had crumbled, were replaced with aluminum.
In the interest of economy, the original steel window frames were replaced with aluminum. As a distinctive touch, the window mullions are in the form of tapered fins.
The decision to renovate the facade rather than rebuild it appealed to the owners for more than esthetic reasons. "They didn't want to spend a ton of money," Mr. Holzman said. "They wanted something that was adequate, appropriate and looked good."
"Since the building has lasted 50 years," he said, "we decided we could leave most of it, because it might last another 50 years."
The building has been owned since the early 1970's by a partnership of the Feil family and the John D. and Catherine T. MacArthur Foundation.
Other properties in the Feil portfolio are 7 Penn Plaza, at Seventh Avenue and 30th Street; 250 Park Avenue South, at 20th Street; the Gramercy Park Building, 257 Park Avenue South, at 21st Street, and the former Roosevelt Building, 841 Broadway, at 13th Street.
The family keeps a low public profile. Jeffrey Feil, president and chief executive of the Feil Organization, deferred to Mr. Holzman for comments on the renovation. He would not disclose the cost of the project.
Perhaps the biggest and best known tenant is the monthly publication Institutional Investor, which is aimed at top executives of banks, brokerage houses, money management firms, insurance companies and other corporations.
Institutional Investor, which was bought last year by Euromoney Publications P.L.C., occupies most of the 12th, 14th, 15th and 16th floors, and has subtenants on half of the 17th floor. Its lease runs through 2002.
Abbeville Press, a book publishing company, occupied two floors, but recently moved downtown to 22 Cortlandt Street.
Publishing companies have always been a part of 488 Madison Avenue, which was developed by Percy and Harold Uris. Look magazine was an original tenant. So, too, was Esquire Inc., which filed suit to prevent the tower from being designated the Look Building. Esquire feared that the public would mistakenly assume that its publications -- Esquire, Coronet and Apparel Arts -- were connected with Look magazine. But the name went up anyway.
Look occupied six floors at 488 Madison Avenue until 1971, when it succumbed to rising postal rates and declining advertising, despite its circulation of 6.5 million. The "Look" sign lasted at least until 1980.
Another renowned tenant was the industrial designer Raymond Loewy. During the time he was at 488 Madison Avenue, from 1951 to 1957, Loewy designed the double-deck Scenicruiser bus for Greyhound. It is not hard to see a link between its streamlined profile and that of the Look Building.
In his exhaustive survey of modern architecture, "New York 1960" (Monacelli Press, 1995), Robert A. M. Stern ranked the Look Building as perhaps the best postwar work by the firm of Emery Roth & Sons.
But with the proliferation of other white-brick towers, followed by glass-walled buildings, 488 Madison Avenue looked less and less pioneering.
In the late 1980's, the storefronts and base of the building were given a uniform treatment of tinted glass and black aluminum panels in a renovation designed by Horowitz Immerman Architects.

GRAPHIC: Photo: The former Look Building at 488 Madison Avenue, dressed in its recent renovation, which rejuvenated rather than obliterated its old appearance. (Angel Franco/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: THE NATO DEBATE: THE OVERVIEW;
SENATORS REJECT BID TO LIMIT COSTS OF ENLARGING NATO

BYLINE: By ERIC SCHMITT 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 29

 BODY:
The Senate today overwhelmingly rejected a measure to limit the cost to American taxpayers of weapons to the three Eastern European countries nominated to join the NATO military alliance.
The vote, 76 to 24, was the first of as many as two dozen amendments the Senate may tack onto the resolution to add Poland, Hungary and the Czech Republic to the alliance. 
 At issue in the amendment, designed to cap United States costs, was whether the three nations can afford the armed forces required for NATO membership or will end up as wards of the wealthier 16 member nations.
The amendment, proposed by Senator Tom Harkin, an Iowa Democrat, would have restricted the amount of military subsidies the United States provides to the prospective members to 25 percent of total NATO aid to the countries. The aid would be in the form of grants, loan guarantees and surplus arms.
"Can they afford to bear the burden or not?" Mr. Harkin said. "We've been told they can. Now I'm hearing, well, maybe they can't, we'll have to give them subsidies for weapons. If that's the case, do they have the economic strength to join NATO?"
But opponents of the measure said that Mr. Harkin was confusing two separate issues -- NATO expansion and foreign arms sales -- and that the amendment jeopardized Washington's ability to supply arms to allies in times of crisis.
"The United States has not signed up to foot the bill," said Senator Gordon H. Smith, an Oregon Republican. "The amendment attempts to strangle NATO expansion," he added, by placing "unreasonable restrictions on expenditures."
In the second day of lively debate this week, Senators jousted on a range of issues, from NATO's future missions to a proposal that the three former Warsaw Pact nations now up for a key to the NATO club fully open their archives to help account for American servicemen missing from past wars or cold war incidents. Excerpts from the debate, page A12.
The Senate will vote on these issues later in the week.
Expanding NATO requires the approval of two-thirds of the Senate, but amending the resolution needs only a simple majority. Senate critics of expansion, conceding that they face an uphill battle to defeat the resolution, say their strategy now is to win some amendments. A final vote is likely later this week.
Few issues have raised more concern among Senators about enlarging the alliance than the cost.
The issue is confusing because cost estimates are all over the map, from $1.5 billion to $125 billion over a decade or more, depending on the assumptions. But the Pentagon insists that the best 10-year estimate now is $1.5 billion, with Washington's share at $400 million.
"There has been a lot of misinformation about the costs, some of it understandable," Defense Secretary William S. Cohen told reporters on Monday.
Mr. Harkin argued that the Administration and other supporters of NATO expansion were guilty of false advertising when they said enlargement would cost $400 million over a decade.
That figure covers only "common costs" shared among all members, like maintaining NATO's headquarters buildings in Brussels and the alliance's fleet of Awacs radar planes. It does not cover the various subsidies that the United States Government pays for when it sells or transfers weapons to foreign countries. A recent study by the World Policy Institute found that Government subsidies represented $7.8 billion of $12 billion in American arms exports in 1996.
"We're told it's only going to cost $400 million, but this could go up and up and up with subsidies," Senator Harkin said.
Senator Joseph I. Lieberman, a Connecticut Democrat whose state has several large military contractors, warned that the measure would "hurt American defense workers whose products will not be able to be sold in these three countries."
Mr. Harkin confronted Mr. Lieberman, saying: "This is a whole new venue in this debate about jobs. I thought this was about democracy." Mr. Lieberman quickly retreated.
"No, the overall debate is not about American workers," Mr. Lieberman said. "It's about the principles of freedom."
Senator Joseph R. Biden Jr., a Delaware Democrat and leading supporter of NATO expansion, said that competition for arms sales to fellow NATO members already existed and that the Harkins amendment would only hurt American companies.
"If you don't let Lockheed or Martin Marietta sell, with subsidies, to the Greeks or the Spaniards, you're just subsidizing the French," Mr. Biden said. "We'll lose our competitive advantage."
Moreover, Mr. Biden said, Mr. Harkin was confusing NATO expansion and foreign arms sales. "We are taking great big apples and putting them in a basket of small oranges," he said.
Senator John W. Warner, a Virginia Republican who opposes NATO enlargement, disagreed, saying there is an unwritten commitment by NATO members to help newcomers get a leg up.
"While there is nothing fixed in law for increased contributions for these three potential new members, there is a moral obligation," Mr. Warner said.
The scant support for his amendment did not dissuade Mr. Harkin from broaching an even more controversial point: Why have NATO at all?
"If something is born because of the Soviet Union, what are the reasons not only for continuing it, but for expanding it?" Mr. Harkin asked. "There are other means to promote democracy and economic markets."
He continued: "I'm really worried we're buying into a mentality of the cold war, and not looking to the next century. We're not about to see any headlines that say Russian troops are marching toward Poland or the Czech Republic."
"Europe is powerful," Mr. Harkin said. "Europe is wealthy. There's no Soviet Union. There's no threat. Europe has been rebuilt. Let's look ahead."
Throughout the afternoon, however, many Senators marched to the floor to back the treaty's expansion.
"If NATO doesn't enlarge, the Iron Curtain remains permanent, and the unnatural division will live on longer than the Soviet empire did," said Senator Barbara A. Mikulski, a Maryland Democrat, adding that NATO would remain "an alumni club for cold war victors."
"As a Polish-American, I know that the Polish people did not choose to live behind the Iron Curtain," Senator Mikulski said. "These countries are not asking for a handout. They are not asking for our protection. They are asking to be full partners in the new Europe."
Senator Chuck Hagel, a Nebraska Republican who supports expansion, urged caution when examining the concern that NATO's eastward push would antagonize Russia, saying: "Here we are, almost 10 years after the fall of Communism, with the Berlin wall, talking about, 'Well, I don't know, should we do this? We might offend our Russian friends.' "
Mr. Hagel continued: "I don't see an awful lot of sense in this. Yes, it is important to understand the Russians. Yes, it is important to engage the Russians. But we should not allow Russia, or any other nation, to dominate the final analysis and decisions of our nation's security interests."
On Wednesday and Thursday, Senators will debate amendments to require membership in the European Union as a prerequisite for NATO candidacy and to wait at least three years before considering the next round of new members.
For some Senate opponents of expansion, however, admitting the three countries under review will inevitably lead to more, and to a weaker alliance.
"This is just the beginning to more and more countries," said Senator James M. Inhofe, an Oklahoma Republican. "After the first three recruits, I don't see where there's an end to it."

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Stock Rebound Falters; Market Ends Mixed
An early comeback rally in stocks fell apart on renewed concerns about the strong pace of economic growth and the possibility that the Federal Reserve could raise interest rates later this year.
 The Dow Jones industrial average, which initially recovered more than half its 146.98-point loss Monday, reversed course to end down 18.68 points at 8,898.96, weakened by losses in Procter & Gamble, DuPont, Kodak and General Electric. But the technology-weighted Nasdaq index rose 11.46 points at 1,831.77, partly recovering from its 48.65-point plunge the day before. Page D10. 

Bond prices edged lower and yields remained at two-month highs. The 30-year yield rose to 6.07 percent from 6.05. D10.
 
Fresh Signs of Strength
Consumer confidence rose in April, and orders and shipments for durable goods increased in March, the latest reports suggesting the United States economy remains vibrant. D2.
 
In Japan, data showing the fastest rise in unemployment in 30 years have stunned even the most pessimistic forecasters. D3.
 
Big Selloff in Pepsico Stock
Pepsico reported an 18.5 percent gain in first-quarter earnings from operations but investors sold the company's stock anyway on worries over higher costs from making potato chips and slow growth at Frito-Lay, which accounts for 60 percent of Pepsico's profits. Pepsico shares fell $3.3125, or 8 percent, to $39.75, in trading that was triple the normal volume. D2.
 
3M reported a 2 percent drop in first-quarter earnings because of Asia's economic problems and the strong dollar. D3.
 
Insurer Limits on Viagra Use
Fearing huge payouts to millions of American men, several large insurers are adopting strict reimbursement policies for Viagra, Pfizer's new pill for impotence, requiring proof of a medical cause for the problem and setting limits on the number of pills. A1.
 
I.B.M. to Buy Back More Shares
Seeking to make its stock more attractive, I.B.M. will repurchase $3.5 billion worth of shares and increase its dividend by 10 percent. The company has bought back $20 billion in stock since 1995. D5.
 
Who Gains From Airline Links?
Depending on whom one believes, the planned alliances among a number of major airlines are likely to result in more convenient air travel or higher fares, or possibly a combination of the two. Edwin McDowell: Business Travel. D5.
 
Liggett & Myers to Aid Justice Dept.
The smallest of the nation's big cigarette makers, Liggett & Myers, has agreed to cooperate with the Justice Department in its four-year-long criminal investigation of the tobacco industry. The maker of L&M and other brands will provide Federal officials with company experts and documents. A22.
 
Woolworth to Sell Headquarters
Woolworth, which is reorganizing itself into an athletic footwear and apparel company, said it planned to sell its headquarters building, a landmark neo-Gothic tower in lower Manhattan. B7.
 
Response to Drug Shortage
A Federal advisory panel exploring a national shortage of intravenous immune globulin, a lifesaving drug derived from blood, adopted recommendations intended to alleviate the impact of the crisis on patients and to prevent another shortfall. Panel hearings also established that manufacturers had not contributed to the shortfall in order to raise prices and profits. A20.

HEADLINE: Alternative Public Schools Cost More but Do Better Job, Report Says

BYLINE: By JACQUES STEINBERG 

 BODY:
Seeking to resolve a contentious question surrounding New York City's small, alternative public schools, researchers have found that they spend more per pupil than traditional schools but get better results for their money by graduating more students in four years.
The research report, due for release today, supports the long-held view of critics of the alternative schools that they spend more for each pupil than traditional schools. But the researchers say their more significant finding is that, with higher graduation rates at the smaller schools, the cost per graduate is about the same. 
 The two-year study of 128 city high schools by researchers at New York University found the small schools (those with 600 or fewer students), spent $7,628 a year for each pupil, about $1,410 more than the larger schools (those with 2,000 or more students), based on 1996 budget figures. But they spent only $25 more per graduate.
The results of the study come just a few months after Schools Chancellor Rudy Crew raised questions about the efficacy of smaller schools, particularly their costs. By suggesting an answer -- that small and large schools each spend about $49,500 per graduate over four years -- the N.Y.U. researchers offer at least the possibility that a long-unresolved debate may be substantially quieted.
The small schools themselves had contended all along that they were as cost-effective as the city's older and more traditional large high schools. The larger schools had respectfully disagreed.
The research, until now, had been inconclusive.
While previous studies have shown that struggling learners, particularly those in low-income neighborhoods across the country, benefit from attending schools in smaller settings, the N.Y.U. researchers suggested that creating such schools should now be considered good economics.
"I think this means that the investment is well worth it," said one of the authors of the study, Norm Fruchter, the director of the Institute for Education and Social Policy at N.Y.U., "This is particularly true for poor kids and kids of color who have not done well in their previous education."
The researchers said that there were limits in the conclusions to be drawn from their work. Because the schools examined had to have several years' worth of graduation data, most of the dozens of alternative academies founded since 1993 were excluded. But the researchers said they were confident that the 10 older alternative schools examined -- including the Central Park East Secondary School (329 students) and the Choir Academy (426 students), both in Harlem -- were similar enough to the newer batch of schools to provide relevant data.
And they added that the preliminary graduation data at many of the newer schools, centered on such themes as the city's museums and its infrastructure, are even more impressive than at the schools that were studied in depth.
The results of the study come at a crossroads for the small-school movement in New York City, which has been viewed as a national model and a precursor of the nascent charter-school movement.
Last fall, Dr. Crew described small schools as sexy, but not necessarily the most scalable type of reform, by which, he explained, he meant that they were hard to replicate in a cost-efficient way that got results.
Indeed, a set of rules drafted by a mid-level Board of Education official -- but never endorsed by the Chancellor -- would have, among other things, restricted the size of small schools, raising the minimum enrollments to 400 for elementary schools, 600 for middle schools and 800 for high schools. Those numbers sent shock waves though a movement that believes that schools with as few as 200 students are the perfect foil to the factory-like high schools of old.
Since then, Dr. Crew has gone out of his way to express his support for small schools. And his spokeswoman, Chiara Coletti, said that the N.Y.U. study would only enhance that support.
"Popular wisdom has it that small schools are particularly more expensive than large schools," Ms. Coletti said. "If the popular wisdom proves not to be valid, this is wonderful news for us."
Champions of small schools predicted that the report would go a long way toward cementing the schools' place in the spectrum of educational offerings in the city.
"People are used to thinking about cost per pupil," said Heather Lewis, the executive co-director of the Center for Collaborative Education, a network of 40 alternative schools, none with more than 500 students. "But they don't think about cost per graduate. I'm not saying it's the ultimate measure. But it is another way of looking at what it takes to successfully get kids through school."
The N.Y.U. researchers, who used graduation rates and budget figures tabulated by the Board of Education, made clear that they could not have undertaken such an analysis until recently because it was not until 1996 that the board was able to identify how much of its budget was apportioned on a school-by-school basis.
The researchers calculated annual spending per student to be highest in the smaller schools. That $7,628, based on 1996 budget figures, was an increase of $1,410, or 23 percent, over large schools; $685, or 10 percent, over so-called smaller medium schools (600 to 1,200 students), and $780, or 11.4 percent, over larger medium schools (1,200 to 2,000 students).
But in terms of graduation rates, the smallest schools were found to be superior, with 63.2 percent of students in the class of 1996 graduating in four years, compared with 55.9 percent in large schools, 50.4 in smaller medium schools and 51.2 percent in larger medium schools.
When those figures, and a host of others, were combined, the researchers found the cost per graduate to be $49,554 in the smaller schools, $49,578 in the large schools, $70,133 in the smaller medium schools and $59,384 in the larger medium schools.

HEADLINE: The Economic Bubble Theory

 BODY:
The Dow's flight above the 9,000 mark and sudden 165-point retreat this week has triggered warnings of a "bubble economy" -- the ominous notion that markets have been swelled by speculative excess and the first pinprick in investor confidence will explode them. The repeated reference has been to Japan, whose stock and real estate markets soared to dizzying heights, only to crash and drag its economy down to a crawl for most of the 1990's.
But America today is nothing like Japan eight years ago. Japan's banks were awash with corporate stock and debt secured by bloated real estate prices. Inept regulatory, monetary and fiscal policies strangled its economy. The American economy today is nearly a mirror opposite. Its vital signs, with one possible exception, are healthy. Unemployment is at record-low levels. Inflation, properly measured, is near zero. Private debt and real estate markets are stable. Banks hold substantial reserves. None of this means that the economy cannot slip into recession. But a recession that might start now would reflect the ordinary ups and downs of a complex economy, and not the inevitable implosion following speculative excess. 
 The one possible exception to the ceaselessly good economic news is steadily rising stock prices. A 9,000-point Dow may or may not prove sustainable, and the Dow may or may not fall 20, 200 or 2,000 more points. A 2,000-point drop could prove traumatic for investors and cause many to cut back purchases. But even a drop this large would bring stock prices down to levels that were deemed high as recently as a year ago. When stocks plummeted by the equivalent amount in 1987, the Fed kept the economy growing.
Cassandras point to tight labor markets as another trouble spot for the economy. But Mickey Levy, the chief economist at Nationsbanc Montgomery Securities, points out that low unemployment no longer poses a serious inflation threat. Even if wage rates start rising, the Fed's tight lid on monetary policy can prevent businesses from passing them along in higher prices.
Pollsters point out that employers are fearful that if they raise prices they will lose sales. Workers are fearful that if they ask for higher wages they will lose work. The only fearless ones appear to be the economists, who, try as they might, cannot seem to find a bubble worth worrying about.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Editorial

INTERNATIONAL A3-12
 
In NATO Debate, Senate Rejects Cost Limits
The Senate overwhelmingly rejected a measure to limit the cost to American taxpayers of weapons for the three Eastern European countries nominated to join NATO. At issue was whether the countries could afford the armed forces required for NATO membership. A1 

Russia Plans Missile Sale
Russia plans to deliver advanced anti-aircraft missiles to the Greek Cypriot Government, despite American protests that the sale will inflame tensions and threats of military action from Turkey. A1
 
Senate Approves U.N. Dues
The Senate approved legislation to pay $819 million in back dues that the United States owes the United Nations. But the White House threatened a veto because the bill contains an anti-abortion provision that President Clinton strongly opposes. A10
 
Yeltsin Cabinet: Few Surprises
President Boris N. Yeltsin and his protege for Prime Minister announced the first round of appointments to Russia's new Government. But there were no surprises, no political concessions and hardly any change. Boris Y. Nemtsov, above, one of the two young champions of economic reform who have become targets of Mr. Yeltsin's restless opponents, will remain a Deputy Prime Minister. A3

The Japanese Government submitted legislation to Parliament to broaden its military ties with the United States, moving Japan closer to playing a larger security role in the region. A6

Deadly Gas Discovered in Iraq
The chief United Nations arms inspector for Iraq said that experts discovered active mustard gas in artillery shells at an ammunitions depot in 1996, raising new questions about more than 500 to 700 similar shells still unaccounted for. A10
 
New Greek Church Leader
Christodoulos Paraskevaidis was elected the new leader of the Greek Orthodox Church in Greece, replacing Archbishop Seraphim, who died this month after 24 years as the church's leader. Metropolitan Christodoulos, 59, who was elected in a secret vote by 77 bishops, is considered a reformer and has pledged to bring the church closer to the people. (Reuters)

The Canadian Government survived a parliamentary showdown with opposition parties that sought to expand a compensation package for people who got hepatitis C from a Government-supervised tainted blood supply. Prime Minister Jean Chretien came back from a visit to Cuba earlier than scheduled to be on hand for the vote. (AP)

An influential panel of the New York State Health Department is urging sweeping changes in the regulation of new fertility technologies, including more steps to reduce the incidence of multiple births, including a discussion with patients in advance of treatment of the possible need to abort one or more fetuses in a high-order multiple pregnancy.
