HEADLINE: Whitman Calls Giuliani's Warnings on Yankees a Ploy

BYLINE: By MIKE ALLEN 

 BODY:
Gov. Christine Todd Whitman of New Jersey accused Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani yesterday of trying to demonize her state as a ploy to build support for his plan to put hundreds of millions of dollars in city money into a new stadium for the New York Yankees.
Mrs. Whitman was responding to comments that the Mayor made a day earlier, suggesting that New Jersey was trying to lure the Yankees across the Hudson River and pledging that he would do the same to New Jersey's professional sports teams. 
 Governor Whitman said at a news conference in Trenton, however, that her state was not negotiating with the Yankees and that she expected the team to remain in the Bronx. She said the Mayor was trying to create a distraction with what she called "Mayor Giuliani's 'Wag the Dog' strategy.' "
"You get a virtual reality war going and nobody watches what's really happening," she said. "It's: 'I've got my own battles here, but I don't want anybody to see it. Let's make New Jersey the enemy.' We're not the enemy. We're not doing anything."
She went on: "The problem he has is with New York. It's with the Bronx."
The Mayor has repeatedly said that he wants to keep the Yankees in the Bronx, yet at the same time he has been aggressively promoting the notion that the financial promise of a new stadium on the West Side of Manhattan might be the only way to keep the Yankees in New York.
By painting New Jersey as the enemy, Mrs. Whitman suggested, Mr. Giuliani is trying to create a controversy to shore up support for his West Side plan.
Even though Mrs. Whitman denied courting the Yankees, Mr. Giuliani issued a statement later in the afternoon saying he had forced her to back down. "It's obvious that our strategy worked," he said. "It seems that the prospect of New Jersey losing professional sports teams has made Governor Whitman back off. She just made our job a lot easier."
The sharp exchanges were unusual for the Mayor and the Governor, both centrist Republicans who have had mostly friendly relations. The most notable exception was Mr. Giuliani's repeated suggestion that spending by the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey consistently favored New Jersey.
Howard J. Rubenstein, the spokesman for George M. Steinbrenner 3d, principal owner of the Yankees, said Mrs. Whitman and Mr. Steinbrenner had not discussed a move to New Jersey. But he said Mr. Steinbrenner had not decided among his choices of remaining in the Bronx or moving to Manhattan or New Jersey. "Steinbrenner is considering all three," Mr. Rubenstein said.
Mrs. Whitman acknowledged that her state's Commissioner of Commerce and Economic Development, Gualberto Medina, had left a message with Mr. Steinbrenner's secretary saying, "We're here."
"That's not negotiations," Mrs. Whitman said. "There is nobody in state government who is negotiating with the Yankees. I seriously don't want to see us get in the position of just making it more difficult for New York to keep a team that I think is going to stay there in the Bronx because that's where they've always been." Mrs. Whitman also said she would not rebuff an overture from the Yankees, but insisted that there were "no special deals being cut."
She added: "If there's a private-sector entity or person out there somewhere, I don't control them. Neither does Rudy Giuliani."
But this week, Mr. Giuliani seemed to ignite interstate dispute.
"We would like to acquire some additional sports teams," he said after reading a story to a third-grade class in lower Manhattan. "If they're in New Jersey, they're in New Jersey." And he noted that his proposal for financing new stadiums for the Yankees and the Mets also included money for a soccer field and possibly a football stadium. "Those things are not by any means, as somebody said, pipe dreams," he continued. "Those are realities. We've had meetings about it already."
Mrs. Whitman responded only by saying New Jersey wants to keep its teams. "I think what he ought to do is concentrate on his side of the river and keeping his teams in New York," she said. "We're not trying to take them away. If they want to leave, I can't stop that. But that's not anything that we're involved with."
Also yesterday, the City Council Speaker, Peter F. Vallone, released details of his drive to put a referendum on the Nov. 3 ballot that would allow voters to signal their preference between a stadium in the Bronx or Manhattan.
"I cannot see one man -- the Mayor, whoever he is -- imposing his will on the rest of this city," Mr. Vallone, a Democrat from Queens, said in an interview.
Aides to Mr. Vallone said that by July, the City Council plans to vote to amend the City Charter to prevent the Mayor from spending city funds for the purpose of building a Yankees stadium outside the Bronx. If the Council was able to override an expected veto by the Mayor, the issue would go to voters.
Earlier this week, Mr. Giuliani blamed Mr. Vallone and opponents of a West Side stadium for encouraging the Yankees to put out cross-border feelers. "I think if they end up in New Jersey, we'll be able to figure out why," the Mayor said. "I think we'll be able to prevent it, but we haven't gotten a lot of help from a lot of the politicians in this city."
Mr. Giuliani went on to say a referendum would be especially destructive. "If you put a team in a corner, they leave," he said.
The Mayor also signaled that he might lose the argument in the voting booth. "There are certain things that will just be voted against, and maybe momentary feelings have you voting against it," he said. "But then later on, it's in the best interests of the city or the state to move in that direction."

HEADLINE: THE NATO DEBATE;
Words of Warning in the NATO Battle: Senators Tilt at Europe's Future

BYLINE: By The New York Times 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, April 28

 BODY:
Following are excerpts from the Senate debate today and on Monday on a measure to expand NATO to include Hungary, Poland and the Czech Republic, as provided by The Congressional Record and Senators' offices. The passages on today's debate focus on a proposed amendment, which was defeated, to limit United States spending to expand NATO.
 
FROM TUESDAY'S DEBATE
TOM HARKIN Democrat of Iowa: If this amendment had been in effect 40 years ago, we couldn't have been in Greece. But that was during the cold war. That is when we were facing the Soviet Union. That is when we were facing, if I might say to the Senator from Delaware, facing a Europe that was on its knees, busted, broke, basically decapitated from World War II. There is no way that they could have done it on their own. 
 That is why I say with this whole NATO argument, that it just seems to me we are arguing about a world that existed 50 years ago. The Senator from Delaware in his impassioned pleas is arguing for a situation that no longer exists.
Europe is powerful. Europe is wealthy, and the nations' GNP's are going up. There is no Soviet Union. There is no external threat like Greece was facing. Europe has been rebuilt. The cold war is over. Let's look ahead.
What I am saying is that I don't believe, in the context of a Europe that we see now and in the foreseeable future, that our taxpayers ought to be liable for the national costs any more in excess of what they are liable right now for the common costs.
That is what this amendment says. Very simply, it says very forthrightly, "Any future United States subsidy of the national expenses of Poland, Hungary or the Czech Republic to meet its NATO commitments, including the assistance described in subparagraph (c), may not exceed 25 percent of all assistance provided to that country by all NATO members."
When it comes to tanks, planes or anything else, of course, we can still sell them. They can still buy from us. But our subsidy to this national effort cannot be more than 25 percent of the total amount of all the countries for that national effort. . . .
JOSEPH R. BIDEN Jr. Democrat of Delaware: The Senator from Iowa forthrightly responded, as he always does, that if we wanted to sell Poland, like we sell Greece or Germany or anyone else, a piece of American-made military equipment, as long as we did not subsidize more than 25 percent of what that was, then we could sell it.
I wonder why in God's sake would the French Government agree to come up with money for Poland to allow them to buy an American jet instead of a French jet? Why would they possibly do that? And does this not give a veto, a veto on the part of other NATO nations, over American foreign military sales? Because unless they come up with 75 percent of what any subsidy would be, why would they possibly do that?
Is it not true -- the Senator is on the Armed Services Committee -- is it not true that one of the core debates in NATO beyond burden-sharing has been who gets to sell NATO the equipment, whether they fly Mirages, whether NATO planes are Mirages or whether they are American-made aircraft?
Every other European country in NATO has been saying, "You Americans get too much of an advantage." Every time we talk about burden-sharing, don't they come back and say, "Yes, but you don't get it; you get to make all that money and get all those jobs because you are supplying the equipment that all the NATO uses."
So why in the Lord's name would we give a veto power over the ability of American manufacturers and American employees to keep their jobs to the French and the Germans and the Brits? . . .
GORDON H. SMITH Republican of Oregon: Mr. President, with all due respect to my friend from Iowa, I believe the Harkin amendment attempts to strangle NATO expansion because it cannot prevent this expansion from occurring. This amendment places unreasonable restrictions on expenditures by limiting our assistance to new NATO members to 25 percent of all assistance provided to these countries by current NATO members. . . .
Let me be clear on this point. In signing the protocols of accession with these three countries, the United States has not signed up to foot the bill for their membership in NATO, and Poland, Hungary and the Czech Republic understand that it is ultimately their responsibility to make the necessary improvements to their military structures. . . .
I also confess concern about the signal that would be sent if the Senate adopted the Harkin amendment.
Does approval of this amendment mean that the United States would only lead NATO 25 percent of the time, no matter what our security interests may be? Does it mean that the United States is interested in 25 percent of NATO's activities, exercises and planning processes? Does it mean that the United States should participate in only 25 percent of any potential NATO operations despite any potential threat posed to the alliance? . . .
BARBARA A. MIKULSKI Democrat of Maryland: I would like to discuss the benefits of enlargement and weigh them against the costs. The strategic benefits of enlargement are most important. NATO enlargement will create a zone of peace and stability that includes Eastern Europe. It will extend NATO's stabilizing influence to more of Europe and reduce the chances of aggression or conflict in Eastern Europe.
There are also economic benefits. Europe is America's largest trading partner, with $250 billion in two-way trade each year. Our new NATO partners will increase trading opportunities. They are building vibrant, free-market economies. NATO brings stability, and stability brings prosperity. We are creating a prosperity zone across Europe.
In addition, there are benefits for democracy. For example, the young military officers of new NATO members are learning from us what it means to be part of democratic military. It means civilian control of the military. It means a code of conduct. They are also learning English. When they leave the military, they will bring these skills. The training they get from NATO will be a permanent, stabilizing force in their societies.
It is clear that these benefits of NATO enlargement far outweigh the costs. . . .
CHUCK HAGEL Republican of Nebraska: Aside from the obvious defense purpose of the expansion of NATO, there are other issues involved. The obvious defense purpose of expanding NATO is to help assure stability and security in Europe, all of Europe, Mr. President. And there has been some debate on this floor about this issue, this fourth expansion, and, by the way, an unprecedented expansion. We have expanded NATO three other times to include West Germany, Greece, Turkey and the third expansion, to Spain and Portugal. This would be not an unprecedented action we would take to include three new countries. But I find interesting the fact that there has been some reference made to, we would split Europe. And I say, Mr. President, just the opposite, just the opposite. We would in fact do much to unify Europe. And why would that be?
Well, that would be, cause stability, security, economic development, development of democracy, market economies would extend across the continent of Europe, across the continent of Europe. And no longer would there be the Iron Curtain that fell at the end of World War II. NATO expansion would help assure that.
I also find the argument interesting from the perspective of -- I thought when the Berlin wall fell in 1989 that meant something. It was beyond symbolism. It was a witness to history that authoritarian, totalitarian government does not work under any name -- Nazism, Communism -- it doesn't work.
Here we are almost 10 years after the fall of Communism and the Berlin wall talking about -- I don't know -- should we do this? We might offend our Russian friends. Certainly any important decision must factor in every dynamic of debate and every dynamic of national security interests, relationships, future relationships and, in this case, certainly does factor in our relationship with Russia. But, my goodness, why did we fight for 40 years a cold war, and we won it.
Only 10 years later, to some extent to be held hostage to what the Russians want. You see, I don't see an awful lot of sense in that. Yes, it's important to understand the Russians. Yes, it's important to engage the Russians. But not allow Russia or any other nation to dominate the final analysis and decisions of our national security interest nor all of the collective security interests of Europe.
 
FROM MONDAY'S DEBATE
SENATOR ROBERT C. SMITH Republican of New Hampshire: With or without NATO, the United States can come to the defense of any European nation next week, tomorrow, next year or five years from now. Should Europe ever be threatened by Russia, or by anybody else, we can expand NATO. We can do it quickly.
But there is a lot to lose and very little to gain by expanding NATO now. We basically say to Russia: "Don't worry about it. Don't worry about NATO expansion. It is O.K. It is a defensive alliance."
But it does not matter what we say. It matters what the Russians think. They have stated clearly and officially they oppose expansion. It has been said by others on the floor, and I agree that we should not set our foreign policy based on what the Russians say, I will be the first to admit that, but we ought to realize there is a lot going on inside Russia and there is no threat to these nations from Russia.
So tell me what the threat is. Tell me what the urgency is. There is no urgency. There is no threat. It is an emotional feel-good thing to do. They earned it. They are free. Let us put them under the umbrella of NATO and protect them. From what? We are still going to go to their defense if anything threatens them. Every person in the Senate knows it. The most important requirement for the Poles, the Czechs and the Hungarians as far as their security is concerned is that America and Russia remain friends. That is the protection these nations need, that Russia and America become friends and remain friends. . . .
JOHN W. WARNER Republican of Virginia: Mr. President, will the Senator allow me to observe that the American taxpayers, since 1992, have contributed $2.6 billion in the spirit of that friendship to help Russia dismantle its weapons systems? And here this comes along and takes a red-hot poker and jams it right in their ribs. . . .
MR. SMITH It sure does. The Senator knows that. He knows the Senator has worked on this issue tirelessly in the Armed Forces Committee and has visited Russia to see this.
I don't think anybody could deny that in the very near future, Russia is going to be one of the, if not the strongest nations in that region of the world. The question is, whose side is it going to be on? Is it going to be on the Iranians' side? Is it going to be an alliance with the Chinese Communists? Or is it going to be on our side? If it is on our side, why will the Poles care or the Czechs or the Hungarians or anybody else? The point is they wouldn't.
What we ought to be doing again is keeping the window open, using the advantages that we have to draw that out, to draw them this way. Senator Warner has mentioned how they have reached out to do that. We are taking down tremendous numbers of weapons that have been aimed at the United States for decades.
But extending an alliance, which during the cold war the Soviet Union considered hostile, the countries that she doesn't threaten is basically kicking this former giant, like the Senator from Virginia said, poking them in the ribs. . . . That is exactly what we are doing, God knows. I have stood on this floor many times and in the House Chamber before that and extolled the virtues of the United States against the cold war, the Soviet Union, and voted trillions to defeat it.
But let's not walk away from the victory. Let's not walk away from the victory. History shows that it is unwise to treat nations like that, and it is highly dangerous for countries in the middle, because these are the countries that are going to suffer if there is a confrontation that takes place between the United States and Russia again. It is the nations in the middle in Eastern Europe that are going to get the squeeze. That is where it is going to be fought. Those are the people who are going to suffer. . . .

HEADLINE: Reputed Crime Family Head Indicted in Extortion Case

BYLINE: By BENJAMIN WEISER 

 BODY:
In a case that shows the persistence of the Mafia in New York's garment industry, Federal prosecutors announced the indictments of the reputed acting boss of the Lucchese organized crime family and 11 others yesterday in a broad scheme to extort millions of dollars from garment center businesses for the last decade.
Mary Jo White, the United States Attorney in Manhattan, said the Lucchese crime family had "exercised dominant extortionate control over legitimate businesses in the garment center." 
 Six years ago, city prosecutors in Manhattan said they had ended a Mafia-controlled trucking cartel in the garment industry in which the Gambino crime family used threats of violence to force manufacturers to use trucking companies they owned or controlled, in a scheme that added about 7 percent to the price of finished garments.
Reputed senior members of the Gambino and Genovese crime families were also among those charged in the cases announced yesterday. Lewis D. Schiliro, assistant director of the Federal Bureau of Investigation and head of its office in New York, said that with the new charges, "we have made great progress stripping the garment center from the mob's portfolio of assets."
The two racketeering and extortion indictments filed by Federal prosecutors charged the reputed acting Lucchese crime boss, Joseph A. DeFede, 64, of Howard Beach, with directing the garment district schemes. At least $30,000 to $40,000 a month, or a total of about $2.5 million, was extorted from garment makers as well as sewing, cutting and dye plants, prosecutors said.
"The means used were allegedly the classic means of organized crime, threats of physical and economic harm," Ms. White said. In return, the businesses were allowed "to do business in the garment center," were given protection and labor peace and, if they were not unionized, were allowed to remain so.
Howard Safir, the New York City Police Commissioner, said the influence of organized crime caused "a significant number" of garment center businesses to leave the city.
"It is estimated that this pervasive pattern of extortion has added hundreds of thousands of dollars each year to the cost of doing business in the garment industry in New York City," he said.
Mr. DeFede was ordered held without bond yesterday pending a hearing on Friday by a magistrate judge in Federal District Court in Manhattan. He said almost nothing in court, except to acknowledge that he understood his rights.
After court, his lawyer, Martin Geduldig, said that his client was innocent, but Mr. Geduldig would say little else about him, except that he worked for a New Jersey company.
The Government also charged that a lawyer in New York helped collect the extortion payments for the Lucchese family. In court, a Federal prosecutor said that the Government had secretly videotaped the lawyer, Irwin Schlacter, 73, of Yonkers, as he met with Lucchese family members in his Manhattan office, counted cash and threatened one trucker.
In explaining to a magistrate why the Government was seeking a $1 million bond for Mr. Schlacter, the prosecutor, Joseph F. Bianco, said Mr. Schlacter had told the trucker that he "would end up" like one Lucchese family member who was killed after he was accused of stealing garment center money.
The magistrate, Andrew J. Peck, eventually set bond at $750,000 after Mr. Schlacter's lawyer, Michael Rosen, said his client was a leading lawyer who had practiced in New York for 44 years and was therefore not a risk. "He's not guilty," Mr. Rosen said.
 Officials said that the Federal investigation of the Mafia's role in the garment district was continuing.
In an unrelated case in Newark, Faith S. Hochberg, the United States Attorney for New Jersey, announced the indictment of Joseph Gatto, a reputed captain in the Genovese crime family, and 13 other people in a loan-sharking and gambling case.
After a bail hearing, Mr. Gatto's lawyer, William J. DeMarco, said: "My client is innocent. He truly is."

HEADLINE: Topless, and Dancing on the Edge;
City's 'Quality of Life' Campaign Takes On a Strip-Club Tradition

BYLINE: By DAN BARRY 

 BODY:
The doorbell to his cramped office kept buzzing, pulling Milton Anthony away from the vital business at hand. First came the delivery boy, bearing a spaghetti dinner destined to congeal uneaten. Then came Pia, then Eve, then Tiffany, all wanting to know where they would be publicly removing their clothes in the coming week.
Is it the Mermaid on Tuesday day and Billy's Topless on Friday night, or Billy's on Monday night and the Pussycat on Thursday day? Simple questions of scheduling, perhaps, but Milton Anthony needed to concentrate. His livelihood was under siege. "Tiffany, sweetheart," he said to the woman at the door, his ancient face veiled in cigarette smoke. 
 "We're in the middle of a conference."
"But I was told to come between 3 and 6," Tiffany said. Mr. Anthony turned and searched for the time. There, on a wall cluttered with framed photographs of former clients -- Leslie Tulips, Bambi Vawn, Cleopatra -- was a clock saying 5 o'clock. "So," he said, shrugging hunched shoulders buried in a gray sweater. "Come back in an hour."
The clock is ticking for Mr. Anthony and the entire adult-entertainment industry of New York City. He owns the A P Variety Talent Agency, which specializes in "topless go-go dancers" ("serving the industry since 1966," his business card says). He also owns Billy's Topless, a corner bar in Manhattan's Chelsea section where women have been dancing, without tops or neighborhood opposition, since the mid-1970's.
Unfortunately for Mr. Anthony, the appeal of strip-club tradition is lost upon Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani.
Today, lawyers for the adult-entertainment industry are scheduled to appear before a three-judge panel of the Federal appeals court in Manhattan to argue against one of the Mayor's most ambitious initiatives: a zoning law that would prohibit strip clubs and other sex-oriented businesses from operating within 500 feet of residences, schools, day-care centers and places of worship. The administration says the law is an integral element of its effort to improve the quality of life in the city's neighborhoods; the industry says the law violates the constitutional right of free speech.
So far, though, the law has withstood several court tests, and the administration already has in place a plan to unleash squadrons of inspectors, all trained to find violations that would force most sex businesses to shut down. If the city prevails again, the industry would likely place itself at the mercy of the United States Supreme Court.
In the debate over the city's plan, most of the attention has focused on the adult-video and book stores peppered around Times Square, and on expensive establishments that hold themselves out as "gentlemen's clubs." Lost in the commotion are the small neighborhood bars that serve chasers of temptation with their beer. And among those smaller businesses, Mr. Anthony is the grandfatherly anachronism, a 78-year-old curmudgeon who sticks to principles -- such as they are: no breast enhancements, no lap or table dancing, no touching the dancers.
But a victory for the city would deliver a one-two punch to Mr. Anthony. Not only would it limit the number of venues for his dancers, but Billy's would have to shut down, move or put tops on its "topless" dancers. That is because the bar, on the corner of 24th Street and the Avenue of the Americas, is within 500 feet of a few apartment buildings.
Contemplating these options, Mr. Anthony reached for the handwritten notes on C-6-2, C-6-4X and other zoning designations that he keeps in his shirt pocket. He then took out a marked-up copy of a neighborhood map and ran a finger up the avenue. "I imagine we are at risk," he said finally. "I'm not kidding myself."
But Mr. Anthony says he plans to endure. Rather than close down or move away -- which city officials acknowledge is the intention of the law -- he will adjust by having the dancers wear bikinis that comply with the law. "But I don't know how much business we'll lose," he said.
For more than two decades, Billy's Topless has operated out of a four-story building with an appropriate history; it once served as a Gay 90's beer hall for a risque vaudeville house on 23d Street. The years have worn away whatever gaiety the drab building once conveyed. It is adjacent to a hardware store and faces a huge advertisement, several stories high, for a Masonic Hall: "God. Family. Country. Fraternity. Philanthropy. Community Service."
Such concepts are generally left at the door of Billy's. On Monday night, beneath billows of blue smoke, three topless women moved to the medium-paced tempo of a Fleetwood Mac song. No one sat in the 24 chairs arranged around the small stage; the dozen customers sat at the bar, some watching the dancing, others watching a basketball game on television. Now and then a customer would stand up, walk over, and hand a dancer a dollar bill. The night's free buffet, lasagna, sat in a tin above a Sterno can, untouched.
Several employees and two of the dancers described Billy's as more of a neighborhood bar than a strip club, a place where a Mets team photograph seems perfectly at home on the wall. The glitzy clubs in midtown charge exorbitant admission fees and make dancers pay for their stage time; in that environment, breast enhancements are de rigueur.
But his employees say that Mr. Anthony has resisted these trends -- at Billy's, a bottle of beer costs $4 -- and has taken pains to be a good neighbor.
Edward Kirkland, the chairman of the Chelsea preservation and planning committee for Community Board 4, agreed. "It's been there a long time, and, quite frankly, the board has never had any complaints about Billy's or its customers," he said. "It causes no problems, so why do anything about it?"
A dancer named Tara Rodriguez stepped down from the stage to defend Billy's, taking care to wrap herself in a towel. "You know why I work here?" she asked. "I'm married with two kids. There's no touching, there's no hustling, there's no champagne room. It's straight."
But she said she had no illusions about the economic impact that a strip of fabric across her chest will have. "If they see a girl in a bathing suit, they'll think that they could see the same thing at the beach," she said. "And they won't want to hand you money."
Another dancer, whose stage name is Amber, said that she thought the extra clothing would prove to be more challenging, and more erotic. Besides, she said, after a pensive drag on a cigarette, the nakedness is almost beside the point at Billy's. "People come in here for companionship. A lot of the regulars say that they don't even care that we get naked. And Milton? He's like a dad."
Whether Mr. Anthony is a father figure or an exploitative businessman is fodder for debate. One thing is certain: he is among the unlikeliest purveyors of pulchritude.
For most of his life he worked in the garment industry, specializing in costumes for entertainers. But he keeps few remnants of those days in his museum of an office in the Flatiron Building. Not far from the provocative publicity stills of Ginger Gale and Sunshine Day is an autographed photograph of the rubber-faced comedian Bert Lahr in a spaceman outfit ("I made the tights," Mr. Anthony explained). And whenever Mr. Anthony reaches for a new pack of Carlton 100's, he snips the top off with an oversized pair of scissors intended for cutting through bolts of fabric.
In the late 1960's he made a two-piece costume for a go-go dancer, the design of which impressed her agent, a man known as Mambo-Hy. His real name was Hyman Balick, and his birthplace was unexotic Baltimore, but Mambo-Hy's tales of profit seduced Mr. Anthony. Soon they were partners, booking 200 dancers in bars and clubs throughout the city.
He said he remembers almost all of his former clients, including many of those captured in the black-and-white photos on his walls. Some ended up well; others did not. Angelique, who billed herself as the "Femme Fatale," lives in the state of Washington with her mother. But Suzy, who posed with a rose for her photo, suffered severe burns in an accident. "Last I heard she was driving a cab in Staten Island," he said.
Mr. Anthony acknowledged that his mid-career detour unnerved his wife. But after visiting a few clubs and seeing the dancers treat her husband like a surrogate father, he said, Sylvia Anthony accepted the change. Hanging prominently in his office is a portrait of his wife of 52 years; it is four times the size of any publicity still.
Mambo-Hy died, and so did Mr. Anthony's friend Bill Pell, the owner of Billy's. By 1979, the garment worker had inherited both a talent agency and a topless bar.
The industry gradually changed, and strip-club behemoths, like Scores on the East Side, began drawing away his customers. But Mr. Anthony stuck to the old ways. The clubs and bars that he does business with still pay him $5 for every shift that one of his dancers works; the dancers get $50 plus tips.
Using the inventory skills of his previous profession, Mr. Anthony pencils in the names of his clients on a weekly chart that tracks their hours and venues. He also has a file for every woman who has worked for him in the last three decades. The active files that he keeps by his desk carry handwritten notes about work habits and payment; "has green card," reads one. The inactive files stacked in the corner have brief explanations: "Quit dancing." "Do not hire." "Went to Brazil."
But such meticulous paperwork did not prepare Mr. Anthony for a sudden change in zoning.
"I can understand what Giuliani did on 42d Street," he said. "He cleaned up a real lousy area, and he's brought in a hell of a lot of business. But that doesn't mean the whole city has to be shut down. We've been here 28 years -- no trouble."
The clock struck 6. Mr. Anthony set aside questions of the future to contend with the needs of today. The doorbell buzzed, and a woman he had never seen before entered the office. She said her name was Savannah.
"So," he said, reaching for a pencil. "You've danced before?"

HEADLINE: Clinton Raises $1 Million For Democratic Hopefuls

BYLINE: By ADAM NAGOURNEY 

 BODY:
President Clinton came to New York last night to raise $1 million for Democratic Senate campaigns across the nation this year, attending a high-priced fund-raiser that saw all three New York Senate candidates gather in the same candle-lit Upper East Side mansion.
The dinner, which cost $10,000 a head, was the only event that Mr. Clinton attended during a quick trip up to New York. 
 About 100 people joined Mr. Clinton at the dinner, at the home of one of the Democratic Party's most active fund-raisers.
The dinner was spread across three rooms in the private mansion, just off Fifth Avenue on 96th Street, and in a effort to guard against the appearance of favoritism, Mr. Clinton ate in a room on the second floor, while the Senate candidates ate downstairs.
In his remarks after the dinner, Mr. Clinton paid quick tribute to the three candidates for their "fidelity to their party," but took pains not to say anything that might be interpreted as suggesting support for one of them. Instead, President Clinton offered a generic argument that Democrats in Congress would assure a continuation of programs he has pushed, and that the money raised last night would at least assure that the Democrats hold on to their 45 seats in the Senate.
"They are making every effort to play it down the middle," said one of the three New York Democrats seeking the nomination, Representative Charles E. Schumer of Brooklyn, who ate in the same room (but not at the same table) with one of his rivals, Mark Green, the New York public advocate. The third candidate, Geraldine A. Ferraro, ate in another room across the hall.
Early in the evening, the three Senate candidates circulated among the wealthy crowd, each giving differing accounts of their political strengths -- "Like Rashomon," Mr. Green reported mid-course.
After dinner, guests were crowded into an upstairs drawing room, where Mr. Clinton, speaking in hushed tones and appearing fatigued, spent 10 minutes thanking a crowd so diverse that Ms. Ferraro found herself standing directly behind Donald J. Trump and Martha Stewart a few steps away from Mr. Schumer.
Mr. Clinton made joking, if admiring remarks, about New York as a singular source of his political, and financial, support.
Mr. Clinton said that he had calculated that 25 percent of his plurality in the 1996 Presidential election came out of New York -- and then made reference to one of the side effects of his visits, as evidenced last night by the intersections closed off across the Upper East Side.
"I was just leaning back against the wall thinking about how I might have done if I hadn't blocked traffic for four years," Mr. Clinton said with a chuckle.
With his arrival in New York last night, Mr. Clinton got a glimpse at what Democrats believe will be one of the most contested elections of 1998, given Mr. D'Amato's perceived vulnerability. There are 34 Senate seats up for grabs in 1998, and Democrats are focusing on 15 of them, and on none more than that of Senator D'Amato.
Mr. Clinton makes a practice of staying out of political primaries. Earlier this year, the White House chastised Mr. Schumer for broadcasting a television advertisement that showed Mr. Schumer leaving Air Force One with Mr. Clinton, saying it suggested that Mr. Clinton was supporting the Brooklyn Congressman.
Mr. Clinton's trip was also significant because it marked the first time he had come to New York to raise money since the chairwoman of the State Democratic Party, Judith Hope, had publicly complained in January that Mr. Clinton and national Democrats were siphoning money out of New York with their repeated visits to the state. Ms. Hope said they were taking money away that might otherwise go to New York Democrats seeking to unseat Mr. D'Amato or Gov. George E. Pataki.
Based on those complaints, the national Democratic Party agreed that state parties would get 7.5 percent of the net proceeds from fund-raisers in their states, including, White House officials said, the one last night.
Ms. Hope said she had been privately assured that the figure would be "substantially higher." But Michael Tucker, the communications director of the Democratic Campaign Committee, which collected the checks from last night's event, said, "I think all those decisions will be made later."
The fund-raiser was at the home of J. Shelby Bryan, a communications executive and one of the party's biggest donors. It was so exclusive that the Senate candidates were not permitted to bring their spouses or aides.
As is customary with these events, the dinner was closed except for the portion when Mr. Clinton spoke and a small group of reporters were escorted inside. Mr. Clinton offered up what was for him standard fare for these fund-raisers, warning against Americans being led into complacency because of good economic times. "Good times can be very deceptive," Mr. Clinton said.
"This country has some big, long-term problems," he said, referring to the deficits in Social Security and Medicare. "When things are good, you should bear down on these problems."

HEADLINE: Metro Business;
Cendant to Leave Stamford

BYLINE: AP 

 BODY:
The Cendant Corporation, one of the nation's largest franchisers and direct marketers, plans to move its headquarters and 500 jobs from Stamford to Wilton, Conn.
The company will consolidate its four Stamford centers into a 320,000-square-foot complex in Wilton. 
 The move comes nearly a year after the Stamford Planning and Zoning board rejected a zoning change that would have allowed Cendant to build a larger complex on a 28-acre site near a residential area.
City officials said the expansion would have contributed to an already bad traffic situation.
"Obviously they are one of our bigger employers with about 500 to 600 jobs," said Thomas Hamilton, Stamford's chief financial officer, "and it's never good economic news to have employers leave the city."
Chris Jones, a spokesman for Cendant, said Wilton was chosen "because we wanted to be in an area convenient to all of our employees who make Fairfield County their home."
"We can have a beautiful campus and be close for those employees," he said.
Among the well-known brands of Cendant, which also has offices in Parsippany, N.J., are Days Inn, Howard Johnson and Ramada hotels, Century 21, Coldwell Banker and ERA real estate and Avis car rentals.

SECTION: Section E; Page 1; Column 5; The Arts/Cultural Desk 

LENGTH: 1390 words

HEADLINE: Art Sales: Dazzling Brochures, Dim Hopes

BYLINE: By CAROL VOGEL 

 BODY:
With the first major fine art auctions of the year scheduled to begin next week at Christie's and Sotheby's in Manhattan, it's not the art people are talking about, but the catalogues. They are being criticized as overdesigned, gimmicky and hard to read; some even have poor color reproductions.
In what promises to be one of the dullest spring seasons in years, it seems that both auction houses are trying to disguise their slim offerings. The brochures, with their glut of gate-fold photographs and fancy typefaces, are "an attempt to make lesser things look more important and more like museum catalogues," said Susan Seidel, a private Manhattan dealer. "But they're totally unreadable." 
 Why, after a record-breaking autumn bolstered by two estate sales -- the modern and contemporary art belonging to the New York collectors Victor and Sally Ganz at Christie's and the Impressionist and modern works from the collection of Evelyn Sharp, the hotelier and philanthropist, at Sotheby's -- have things fallen flat? "The market is nearly always fueled by major estates," said Christopher Burge, chairman of Christie's in America. "But this season, they've been in short supply."
Without the great estates as bait, experts say, collectors are simply not ready to part with their art. "There's an awful lot of money in the world today," said Mr. Burge. "But many collectors are not keen to sell, either because they don't have to, or because they don't think we've hit the top of the market yet."
The biggest problem today, said Franck Giraud, who runs Christie's 19th- and 20th-century sales in New York, is one of supply and demand. "The collectors are out there, but great material is scarce," he said. Still, both auction houses have managed to cobble together sales, each with a few star works, with a total value of more than $350 million.
But to make matters more confusing, Christie's has reclassified its sales and Sotheby's has not. Traditionally, both houses have sold works made before the 1860's in their 19th-century sales. But the most valuable Impressionist paintings, drawings and sculptures, though created in the 19th century, were always grouped with 20th-century works made before 1945 in the sales of important Impressionist and modern art. That meant sales of contemporary art were dominated by American artists since 1945.
But starting next week, the year 1900 will be Christie's dividing line; officials there say it best reflects current buying patterns. On Tuesday night, Christie's will hold an important Impressionist and 19th-century sale featuring works by artists who came to prominence before 1900. Thus the auction will include names like Daumier and Boldini along with Monet and van Gogh. (This will be the only sale of the week scheduled for the evening, when the most important art is sold, upsetting the schedules of the many foreign collectors and dealers who traditionally come to New York to attend both Sotheby's and Christie's Impressionist sales, which were always one day apart. Now, Sotheby's sale is more than a week away from Christie's.)
Christie's 20th-century department will handle artists like Picasso and Matisse as well as Pollock and Rauschenberg, all of whose work reached maturity during the first 70 years of this century. A separate contemporary art department will sell work of artists who have flourished since 1970, and its sale is set for June 3.
Sotheby's, however, has kept to its traditional lineup, with an evening sale of Impressionist and modern art on May 13. Its sale of contemporary art, which encompasses works made since 1945, is on May 14, the same night as the much-publicized final episode of "Seinfeld," something many art experts are already grumbling about.
Much of the Impressionist material in both Sotheby's and Christie's sales comes from Japanese owners. Many of the works were bought by businessmen during the late 1980's boom, at overheated prices, and have been sitting in storage since the market took a nose dive in the early 90's.
The sellers today are mainly creditors. In Sotheby's sale, the most expensive work is Monet's "Grand Canal," a 1908 scene of Venice that is estimated to sell for $8 million to $10 million. The painting is being sold by the Fuji Gallery in Japan; and like many expensive works at both auction houses, it carries a guarantee (an undisclosed sum, generally close to the low estimate, promised to the seller regardless of the sale's outcome). Christie's will offer a fleshy Renoir nude, estimated to bring $3 million to $4 million, that is also from a Japanese consignor.
There are also more works from Europeans this season, art that in the past would have been sold in London. Most European sellers believe New York is where the big money is. Moreover, the high import taxes imposed on paintings that are brought into European Union countries like Britain, along with the flagging economies of countries like France and Italy, have made New York a more desirable place to sell artworks.
Take the star of Christie's 19th-century sale, a van Gogh pen-and-ink drawing, "Boats on the Beach at Saintes-Maries-de-la-Mer," from 1888. The drawing is being sold by a member of the Peter Nathan family, collectors and dealers who live in Switzerland, and is estimated to bring $5 million to $7 million. Christie's 20th-century sale features a brightly colored Fauve-period Braque, "Olive Tree Near L'Estaque," from 1906, which came from a French collector's home. It is estimated to bring $3.5 million to $4.5 million.
Sotheby's was able to snag one interesting collection, 10 paintings belonging to Rolf and Margit Weinberg, collectors from Zurich. Mr. Weinberg, who owns a clothing store, is retiring and wants to put some cash away for the future. Among the highlights of the $10 million collection is Courbet's "Portrait of Jo, the Beautiful Irishwoman." Painted in 1866, it shows the red-haired Joanna Heffernan, who was Whistler's mistress at the time, looking in a mirror. It is estimated to sell for $2 million to $3 million.
Also from the Weinbergs is a rare Picasso, "Casagemas in His Coffin," a haunting 1901 painting from the artist's Blue Period. It depicts the artist's dead friend Carles Casagemas, who shot himself six months before Picasso painted the work. "Art historically, this is an important painting," said Alexander Apsis, who heads Sotheby's Impressionist and modern art department in New York.
Another star in Sotheby's sale is a Degas, "After the Bath," painted in 1896. It comes from the estate of Wendell Cherry, the founder of Humana, the Louisville-based health care company, who died in 1991 and whose widow has been slowly selling off his art. The painting carries an estimate of $6 million to $8 million. Degas has performed particularly well at auction in the last two years, especially late pictures like this. Christie's also has a late Degas, "Dancers in a Studio," from 1900-1905, which is expected to bring $5 million to $7 million.
Today's taste also gravitates toward early Warhols. Sotheby's contemporary art sale has one of the best: "Orange Marilyn," a 1964 image of Marilyn Monroe based on a still from the movie "Niagara." One of the most important Pop works to come on the market in years, it boasts a provenance as pristine as the painting itself. It comes from the famous Stroher collection, a German trove of 20th-century art that was one of the first in Europe to include American Pop Art. Sotheby's has estimated it to sell for $4 million to $6 million, and experts there said they were hoping it would break the Warhol record of $4 million set when "Shot Red Marilyn" sold at Christie's in 1989.
Another possible record breaker in Sotheby's contemporary sale is Lucian Freud's "Large Interior W11 (After Watteau)," a 1981-83 painting inspired by Watteau's 1712 "Pierrot Content," which is in the Thyssen Collection in Madrid. It shows two of Freud's children and three friends in a rundown London interior. The painting should bring $2.5 million to $3.5 million and is being sold by James Kirkman, who was Freud's dealer from 1872 until 1992.
"These two paintings are world class," said Tobias Meyer, director of Sotheby's contemporary art worldwide. "Which is unusual for a market that just doesn't supply goods like these anymore."

SECTION: Section F; Page 1; Column 1; House & Home/Style Desk 

LENGTH: 1597 words

HEADLINE: Turf;
Lover's Leap Mentality Hits Co-ops

BYLINE: By TRACIE ROZHON 

 BODY:
IN Manhattan, fear and greed are fueling the white-hot real estate market. Deals are falling apart at record levels. Bidding wars occur every day. And even brokers and lawyers are starting to urge caution.
"There's a hysteria that's kind of crept into the real estate community," said Deborah Beck, executive director of the Real Estate Board of New York, a trade group that represents more than 1,000 Manhattan agents. "It's not good for brokers, and it's not good for buyers and sellers." 
 In this incendiary market, deals fall apart twice as often as they did five years ago. These are deals that fail before the contract is signed and way before the buyer gets near a co-op board interview.
According to a study released this week by the Halstead Property Company in Manhattan, broken deals below 14th Street have skyrocketed from 16 percent in the sluggish 1992 market to 32 percent today; uptown, they have gone from 10 percent to 24 percent. Agents blame sellers' reneging on an accepted offer to get more money elsewhere -- and equally buyers' cold feet, often the morning after.
Nick Brown, a Wall Street executive, promised himself he would buy his own apartment by the time he turned 30. Right after he bid $230,000 on a one-bedroom on West 79th Street in January, the Dow dropped 220 points, only to recover quickly. But he was scared. When his agent tried to get him to bid $5,000 more, he backed out. Now, he says, similar apartments are $275,000 -- and he has more or less given up looking.
There seems to be no relief in sight -- at least in Manhattan -- from the glassy condominiums of Battery Park City to the pre-World War II palazzos along Park Avenue and the landmarked brownstones of Harlem. The reasons given for the whopping price increases -- higher even than last year, a very good year -- are many. Playing a big part are Wall Street bonuses to stockbrokers, Dow Jones windfalls to the average investor and the health of the overall American economy.
But in interviews, buyers and sellers also credit the newly desirable reputation of New York City -- seedy is out, safety is in -- for bringing young and old here to experience what they perceive as the new glitter and glow. When they arrive, many find renting an apartment prohibitively expensive: even with rising co-op and condo prices, many still argue it's smarter (and cheaper) to buy than to rent.
Averaged together, sales prices below 96th Street for the first quarter of 1998 are up 20.7 percent over the same period last year, according to a survey to be released next week by the Corcoran Group. The market has risen 5 percent just since January, the company said, indicating that the steady upward trend continues. Other companies that are preparing their own market analyses report similar numbers.
Housing markets are strong throughout the region, brokers and statisticians alike say. "We do not have the Manhattan market madness," said Joseph E. Mottola, chief executive of the Long Island Board of Realtors. "But if you're thinking of taking your sweet time, it means you have no chance of buying a house."
In Brooklyn Heights, Christopher Thomas, who manages William B. May's Brooklyn offices, complained of broken deals and of pressures similar to Manhattan's. He said prices were rising so fast -- perhaps 15 percent over last year -- that he was worried about next year, "unless the stock market continues its epic climb," Mr. Thomas added.
In this accelerating market, significantly more deals in Manhattan are falling apart now than in the late 1980's, the last real estate boom, according to the Halstead company. While Halstead is the only one to have pinpointed the trend, other brokers do not question it.
"Deals fall apart because of cold feet and sticker shock," said Barbara Corcoran, owner of a Manhattan brokerage. "In the heat of passion, people promise anything."
Ms. Corcoran and Ms. Beck of the Real Estate Board attribute the splintering of deals partly to the inexperience of the bidders: many of today's buyers, they said, are first-timers and easily panicked. On the sellers' side, Ms. Corcoran and Ms. Beck blame greed, plain and simple.
Disappointed buyers are found at every level -- although few will talk publicly about their problems. According to Halstead's report, prices for properties that are traditionally the toughest sells -- studios -- rose 34 percent over the last year. Small two bedrooms, another tough sell in the nadir of the market, were up 30 percent. Town house sales, which many thought peaked last year, were up 17 percent.
Even successful bidders complain about spiraling prices and the resulting free-for-all. Gonzalo Pardo de Zela, 39, a lawyer, and his wife, Tatiana Pardo de Zela, 26, first-time buyers, had bid $551,000 in a sealed-bid auction for a two-bedroom downtown condominium with an asking price of $599,000. Then they were told they had lost out . Frantically, they considered bidding on a $750,000 apartment in the same building.
"We were ready to do it even though it was way more than we could afford," he said.
The Pardo de Zelas got lucky. The seller's first choice for the $599,000 apartment reneged -- typical in this market -- and they had another chance. But they also had to agree to $34,000 more than their first bid. The closing is scheduled for this week.
The number of crashing deals has spawned another phenomenon: the rise of the backup offer. Brokers say today they see backup offers in at least 50 percent of all deals.
"A year ago, I wouldn't advise an agent to show an apartment where there was an accepted offer," said Frederick W. Peters, president of Ashforth Warburg Real Estate. "Now, the only time I tell them not to show the property is when there are both an accepted offer and at least two backup offers."
In all ranges of the market -- from $125,000 studios to $3 million penthouses -- many sellers, faced with a line of frenzied buyers, choose only those whose offer is not dependent on getting a mortgage. They want buyers who are willing to forfeit their deposit (should a mortgage not come through) to grab the apartment of their dreams.
Until this year, agents agree, the vast majority of bids were contingent on the buyer's getting financing. Neil Bader, chief executive of Skyscraper Mortgage, one of the city's largest mortgage brokers, said that among his upper-end clients, 75 percent now sign contracts without having guaranteed mortgage financing. (He estimated that 40 percent of midrange deals are signed without a mortgage fallback -- the percentage was close to zero only a few years ago.) Many of these bidders eventually obtain a mortgage, but they don't make their offer contingent on it.
Making a bid on an apartment therefore often feels more like being caught in a cross-fire than in a friendly back-and-forth debate over prices. One apartment, at 22 East 88th Street, had 12 bids at the asking price -- in four days.
"In times of rising markets the standards of morality in real estate matters decline," Stephen Raphael, a Manhattan real estate lawyer, said sadly. "In a feeding frenzy, it's every man for himself."
Lawrence Sicular, chief statistician at Brown Harris Stevens, a Manhattan real estate organization, said that with the rising prices a lot of buyers are "balancing on the edge, taking a risk."
One couple fought the frenzy and took the risk. Neal and Jodi Elkin, who work at J. P. Morgan & Company, thought they had secured a two-bedroom apartment on 72d Street near West End Avenue for about $450,000.
The seller had even asked them to bring their chocolate Labrador retriever over to meet the neighbors and showed photographs of the dog to the board members, to make sure they didn't object. But "a week and a half later, we still didn't have the contract, and the ad was still in the paper," Ms. Elkin, 32, said.
The Elkins were nervous. Time was running out on their sublet. "Suddenly, he called and said he had an all-cash agreement from somebody else," Mr. Elkin said. (To this day, the Elkins don't believe in what they call the phantom bidders from Los Angeles, who had suposedly not even seen the apartment.)
Mr. Elkin, 33, said he felt violated. "I mean, we had a verbal agreement," he said. "We'd even shaken hands."
Despite their doubts, the Elkins raised their bid by $15,000 and made it noncontingent on getting a mortgage. This time, they went to contract in 24 hours.
So what are buyers and sellers to do in today's overheated market? Mr. Raphael tells buyers to calm down and to prepare a detailed financial statement in advance -- with a hard and fast bidding limit. No agreement is ironclad, he warns, until it is signed by both sides.
Clark P. Halstead, managing partner of Halstead, cautioned that buyers should ask agents to show them figures of recent apartment sales in similar buildings nearby to make sure they are not overpaying.
Nevertheless, "There's this group that feels if they don't move quickly, they'll lose it," Ms. Beck said. Like novice gamblers who put a chip on every number, she said, some of these buyers will run around town placing bids on two, three and four apartments.
Because not all bidders are serious, Ms. Beck says she advises apartment hunters to make a backup offer if they really like an apartment.
"It's like choosing a spouse," she said. "You don't do it because somebody is pressing you to or because you hear somebody else is engaged to the same person."
The market, she said, is "kind of pushing people to act in a way they'll later regret."

PUB-DATE: April 30, 1998

SECTION: Section G; Page 8; Column 1; Circuits 

LENGTH: 1454 words

HEADLINE: In a Contest of Tech Trivia, the West Beats the East

BYLINE: By CAREY GOLDBERG 

DATELINE: BOSTON, April 29

 BODY:
Much as it hurts anyone who loves the bow-tied, Burberried, cobblestoned Northeast and remembers the former glories of the high-tech corridor on Route 128 around Boston, there are times when one simply has to admit that primacy in the computer industry has long since passed, with the sun, to the West.
It was such a time on Friday night at the Computer Museum's 10th Annual Computer Bowl, when, for the fourth year in a row, a West Coast team of five prominent computer industry figures trounced their East Coast counterparts. Trounced them so decisively and quickly, in fact, that at one point, the score in the college-bowl-like game of technology-related trivia was 180 to 20. The final score was 230 to 70. 
 True, it was only a game, and it really doesn't matter that the West Coast team knew that there are now more than 300 million pages on the World Wide Web and that the East Coast team did not know that Silicon Valley has a street called Floppy Drive.
All that mattered was that the bowl has raised $4.3 million in the last 10 years to support the Computer Museum, a bicoastal nonprofit institution based in Boston and Mountain View, Calif., and that it netted $250,000 tonight.
But nonetheless, you came away with a definite sense that beneath the high-tech, high-finance fun ran powerful undercurrents of that big old river in Egypt.
Denial, that is.
"The conventional wisdom is that the West Coast is supreme, and I just don't feel it," said Paul Egerman, founder of IDX, a health-care software company with headquarters in Burlington, Vt. "We've got a very exciting and diverse software community with probably more small software companies here than in the Bay area.
"The reason you'll never be able to write off the East Coast is this incredible thing: our university system. Our biggest export is Ph.D.'s."
(One could not help but think that maybe they're all being exported to the West Coast.)
Philip Greenspun, an iconoclastic Massachusetts Institute of Technology computer scientist, said, "It was better in the old days when we were exporting VAX's" -- Digital Equipment's flagship minicomputers -- "at a million dollars a pop."
On pure technology, the West may be ahead, allowed Christopher Murray, president of the ABT Corporation, a software company with bicoastal projects. "But making it useful is the East Coast thing."
"Everything that happens on the West Coast, in the end, is providing a foundation," Mr. Murray said. "But without us creating the applications actually used by people to do real work, it has no economic value."
(But one couldn't help but wonder: Aren't the companies making the most popular applications these days, like Adobe, Oracle, Intuit and many game companies, also on the West Coast? Or in Texas?)
Even the West Coast victors condescended to defend the East.
"I figured out the theory of why they don't win," said one contestant, Bill Krause, president of Storm Technology. His theory is that the West Coast contestants, who included Denise Caruso, a contributing columnist for The New York Times, and Marc Andreesen, the 26-year-old golden Netscape boy, live close enough to one another that they could gather for practice sessions.
"We practice, and it makes a difference," Mr. Krause said. Also, the questions were weighted toward Internet business, he added, and that is concentrated on the West Coast.
(But, one couldn't help asking, isn't that the point? Perhaps the more densely packed the high-tech world on the West Coast becomes, the more it seems the place to be, and the more it seems the place to be, the more it actually is the place to be?)
Mr. Krause agreed. "You get a critical mass where it feeds on itself, and people say, 'I've got to be where the action is.' "
Still he and other contestants refused to write off the East Coast. Some argued that it was never clear where the next great idea or company was coming from -- remember when Seattle was known for Boeing? "The East will rise again," Mr. Egerman said.
It has a ways to rise, however, judging by its performance. In the old days, like, say, 1990, the East Coast team could stand against the likes of Bill Gates.
These days, the members of the East Coast team, looking cutely Colonial in tricorner hats and ruffled shirts, kept Mr. Andreesen jumping and fist-pumping in youthful glee at their sadly un-nerdly performance. In the last four years, the West has won 4-0, for a 10-year record of 7-3.
The East Coasters -- who included Seth Godin of Yoyodyne and Walter Mossberg, technology columnist for The Wall Street Journal -- even muffed the question asking which of the following was not a real World Wide Web site: The Cat That Looks Like Hitler, The Official French Fries Page, Driveways of the Rich and Famous or Underwater Baseball.
Mr. Godin hazarded a guess, choosing Driveways. The correct answer was Underwater Baseball.
Well, of course it was, Mr. Greenspun, a Web expert, kibbitzed. "I've been to the driveways site myself. Underwater Baseball makes no sense!"
The East Coasters did better on more true-geek questions, like the one about how much faster a new Motorola cable, providing up to 10 megabits per second to a user on a personal computer, is than a 56K modem. The answer from the multiple-choice menu: 178 times faster -- an answer that depended on a quick bit of simple arithmetic. (Actually, it's 178.57, which would be 179 if rounded up, but who's counting?)
But they often failed on current-events questions like the one asking how much faster Option White, I.B.M.'s new $85 million computer, would be than Deep Blue, the chess-playing computer. (It is 1,000 times as fast.)
One question posed by the contest's host, John Ratzenberger -- a technophile best known for playing Cliff Clavin, the mailman on the television show "Cheers" -- seemed especially designed to rub the East Coasters' faces in their technological inferiority.
It went: "Decision Analyst Inc. recently compared the productivity of the 50 most populous cities in the United States, based on such factors as employment rates, growth rates, use of technology, etc. Which city placed first? Was it Boston, Seattle, Minneapolis/St. Paul or San Jose?"
Unsurprisingly, it was San Jose, in the heart of Silicon Valley. But unmercifully, Mr. Ratzenberger added, "Boston placed 28th."
 
Panelists, Start Your Brains
The following are excerpts from the questions asked at the 1988 Computer Bowl:
 1. In the classic text-adventure computer game Zork, you are occasionally told, "It is pitch black." When that happens, will you be attacked by a troll, fall down a well, be attacked by a dragon or be eaten by a grue?
 2. Today 32-bit processors are common, with 64-bit processors just around the corner. But what was the first home computer with a 16-bit processor? Was it the Amiga 1000, the Apple Lisa, the Atari 520 ST or the Texas Instruments 99/4?
 3. In the 1960's, Bell Labs developed a paper computer. What was that computer called? Was it Brainiac, Cardiac, Similac or the Paper PC?
 4. There is some debate as to whether there are life forms on Mars, but there are now several microprocessors on Mars. How many microprocessors are currently on the surface of Mars? Is it 2, 5, 10 or 100?
 5. Most people have heard of I.B.M.'s chess-playing software called Deep Blue. But there is also a world champion checkers-playing program. What is it called? Is it Chinook, Alberta, Checker King or Jumpmaster?
 6. In a famous sci-fi movie, the following line: "My instructor was Mr. Langley, and he taught me to sing a song. If you'd like to hear it, I can sing it for you." Who spoke that line?
 7. When you start up Windows 95, by default it makes a distinctive musical sound. That short piece of music was created by a well-known musician. Was it David Bowie, Mick Jagger, Brian Eno or Elton John?
 8. Veronica is the name for an Internet search tool. The name Veronica is in fact an acronym. What do the letters in Veronica stand for?
 9. One of the dreaded occurrences in computing is when Windows crashes. When it's Windows NT, what is the popular slang term for what you see on the screen when Windows NT crashes?
 10. If you count the number of information technology jobs in the United States, the following states rank in the top three, in this order: California, New York and Texas. What state ranks fourth?
 
1. Eaten by a grue.
2. The TI 99/4, in 1980.
3. Cardiac.
4. Two: one in the Pathfinder, the other in the Rover.
5. Chinook.
6. The HAL 9000 computer in the movie "2001: A Space Odyssey."
7. Brian Eno.
8. Very Easy Rodent-Oriented Netwide Index to Computerized Archives.
9. The Blue Screen of Death.
10. Illinois.

INDUSTRY: SIC7372 PREPACKAGED SOFTWARE (61%); 

COUNTRY: UNITED STATES (94%); EGYPT (79%); 

STATE: MASSACHUSETTS, USA (94%); WEST USA (94%); CALIFORNIA, USA (93%); VERMONT, USA (79%); 

CITY: BOSTON, MA, USA (94%); SAN FRANCISCO BAY AREA, CA, USA (93%); SILICON VALLEY, CA, USA (79%); BURLINGTON, VT, USA (53%);

HEADLINE: Essay;
Alice in Euroland

BYLINE: By WILLIAM SAFIRE 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
To most Americans, the euro (the common currency to replace the franc, mark, lira, etc.) is a MEGO (My Eyes Glaze Over).
On Saturday 11 nations will celebrate a marriage of money that they hope will make the Continent they call "Euroland" more competitive with North America and Asia. 
 The new lockstep stability, goes the dream of European bureaucrats, will attract investors and tourists. The device they have created to bring about this new efficiency is a central bank that sets interest rates and determines the money supply.
Shouldn't we be pleased to see Europe adopt the American model?
No. Europe is not one country, but individual peoples with different languages, cultures and politics. They wisely cooperate in a common market, but to give up political sovereignty through an economic back door -- that's not what the citizens of those countries are bargaining for.
The first symbolic strain shows in the naming of their top central banker. The Germans, to whom discipline and austerity come first, want a hard-line Dutchman; the French, to whom welfare and employment come first, want one of their own.
They'll compromise this time, but soon the internal contradiction will manifest itself. One nation, doing well, will want tight money; another, bound to its union demands and welfare culture, will want easy money.
Suddenly the loss of national control of national destiny will come clear to those told to suffer in the name of Continental discipline. Result: resentment, ethnic unrest, bankerphobia, riots and worse.
These stresses don't tear apart America because we live in one country and can move around. In the Depression, poor Southerners moved north; as the Industrial Belt rusted, tens of millions moved to the Sun Belt. But are hungry Spaniards going to migrate to Denmark, or desperate Danes to Italy? No; they'll be forced to sit tight and suffer.
Many Britons understand this, which is why Tony Blair's Labor Government says wait and see to the euro. Thanks to Thatcherism, that country is booming, and the new Prime Minister is disinclined to give up sovereignty if it risks recession.
The Conservative Party, or what's left of it after being routed last year, is no longer split between Europhiles and Euroskeptics. Last week in the House of Commons I spoke to William Hague, the 37-year old Yorkshireman elected to resuscitate the Tories, and unlike "cool Britannia" Blair, he doesn't straddle at all:
"We want to be in Europe, not run by Europe," this future prime minister, a slam-bang orator, likes to say. He opposes British entry into a single currency in this Parliament and intends to oppose it in the next election. If Hague should win (and Tory political stock is a good gamble at current lows), that would keep Britain free from domination by bankers in Frankfurt until at least 2006.
Where does the U.S. Government stand? Aside. Clintonites deliver knee-jerk responses favoring European union, as if the common currency were just another step in getting along together -- and not a divisive act inviting political backlash. Nasty nationalists are already on the upswing in both France and Germany; the winds of the euro will fan the flames of populist resentment.
The only U.S. pol to address the sovereignty giveaway (in speeches appreciated only overseas) is Newt Gingrich. He rightly sees economic diktats from Frankfurt creating crises that could lead to political disaster and angry disunion. He suggests that Britain ally itself with Nafta.
The coming rise and fall of the euro is America's business, whether we like it or not, and despite the none-of-your-concern attitude of Eurocrats. The Times of London, in a leader grateful for Gingrich's attention to the coming blunder, writes: "Belated American intervention to rescue Europe from itself has been the story of this century."
Democracies must put the political horse before the economic cart. First the U.S. colonies formed a political union; decades later came national banking, and a century later our Federal Reserve central bank. The Eurocrats are putting the cart first, using a supranational bank to impose political union.
That's why American supporters of an expanding NATO say to our allies: Beware the euro. It's bad for union, dangerous to democracy.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: New Rumblings in Cyprus Raise Specter of War

BYLINE: By STEPHEN KINZER 

DATELINE: KYRENIA, Cyprus

 BODY:
A television station in the self-proclaimed Turkish Republic of Northern Cyprus scooped the world recently by broadcasting the film "Titanic" to an amazed and delighted audience.
The film is still doing huge business at theaters around the world, including northern Cyprus, and has not been licensed for television broadcast anywhere. Distributors want to sue the station that pirated it but are not sure they can. 
"We're an illegal country," a Turkish Cypriot official said, his voice dripping with sarcasm and resentment. "We should be entitled to a little illegal entertainment."
Ever since Cyprus was divided in 1974 when Turkish troops invaded, the north has been isolated. In a series of overwhelming votes, the United Nations Security Council has reiterated its request that Turkey withdraw its troops and allow reunification. But Turkey refuses, saying it fears that the Greek Cypriot majority will overrun the north and destroy its Turkish character.
Bitter intransigence on both sides has frustrated many would-be mediators over the years, most recently Richard C. Holbrooke, the special Cyprus envoy of President Clinton. He has described the differences between Greek and Turkish Cypriots as much deeper than those that have separated Serbs, Croats and Bosnian Muslims. Mr. Holbrooke's visit here in April, and his planned return on May 2, reflected growing concern that this conflict is entering a dangerous new stage.
On March 31, the European Union opened talks aimed at admitting Cyprus. Turks fear it may admit the Greek-run south alone. That could leave the Turkish north and its allies in Ankara to fend off an enemy backed by all of Europe, a potentially explosive situation.
"The Turks were badly treated by the European Union in December, when 12 countries applied for membership and 11 were told they could apply, with Turkey being left out in the rain," Mr. Holbrooke said in an interview. "One of the 11 was Cyprus. That produced an inevitable Turkish backlash, which has resulted in them telling North Cyprus to cease all cooperation, all search for common ground.
"The only way this can be reversed is if the European Union opens its doors at least a little bit to Turkey and treats it as it should be treated, as a European nation. The Turks, for their part, have to ease up on their negative approach in Cyprus."
Also raising tensions, the Greek Cypriots have announced that they are buying a battery of S-300 antiaircraft missiles from Russia. Turkey, at whose planes they would presumably be aimed, has threatened to bomb them. The Greek Government says it would consider that an act of war.
"If Turkey attacks Cyprus, we shall fight," Foreign Minister Theodoros Pangalos said on a recent visit to Washington. "Yes, that will mean war between Greece and Turkey."
For millenniums, Cyprus has been a strategic prize guarding access to rich trade routes between Europe and Asia. Greeks have lived here since antiquity.
For nearly a century the island was a British colony. It won independence in 1960 with a Constitution that guaranteed the Turkish minority a strong say in government. The Greek majority later curtailed those rights, starting what Turkish Cypriots say was a violent campaign to intimidate them into fleeing. Many can still remember Greek paramilitary units preventing them from leaving their villages to reach their land, go to beaches or attend school.
In 1974, after a rightist coup here engineered by the military dictatorship then ruling Greece, Turkish Cypriots appealed to Turkey for help. Turkish troops landed, seized the northern one-third of the island and set up a Turkish enclave. The United Nations declared the invasion illegal and refuses to recognize the enclave, which in 1983 declared itself an independent state.
Today the island is home to roughly 200,000 Turkish Cypriots and nearly 600,000 Greek Cypriots. United Nations troops patrol the "green line" that separates them, and the capital, Nicosia, is a divided city.
Nicosia reflects the contrast between the island's sides. The Turkish sector is poor, rundown and frozen in time. On the other side, Greeks run a bustling and successful economy.
Many Turkish Cypriots, however, are convinced that they could not live safely under Greek rule. "We're not doing so badly," said Salahi Dervish, 65, who runs an auto repair shop in Kyrenia. "Of course we don't have much money, but we have enough. More important, we have security."
Greeks assert that Cyprus has always been Hellenic, and that while a Turkish minority can and will be tolerated, Greeks must rule.
For their part, Turks perceive the island as home to two distinct native communities with equal rights and insist on running their own affairs. They say bombings and attacks by Greek gunmen in the 1960's and early 70's prove that they can never trust Greeks to govern them.
"There were thousands of deaths, and the wounds are very deep," said Osman Ertug, a senior Turkish Cypriot diplomat.
Greek Cypriots have little incentive to compromise because they already control two-thirds of the island and are recognized by every country except Turkey as its rightful rulers. They are so much richer than the Turkish Cypriots that they could simply buy out the Turks if the island were unified. Perhaps most important, they have the unconditional backing of Greece.
In the chambers of the European Union, Greece has threatened to block the admission of Poland, Hungary, the Czech Republic and other countries if Cyprus's application is not considered expeditiously.
Turkish Cypriots, on the other hand, are international outcasts, subject to an array of official and unofficial embargoes. Their per capita income is one-third that of Greek Cypriots.
Turkey and northern Cyprus have warned that they will move toward "full integration" if the European Union admits the Greek Cypriot state. But short of complete union, they could hardly come closer than they already are.
The currency of northern Cyprus is the Turkish lira. Its international mail flows through Turkey, and it shares Turkey's telephone code. Turkey maintains 30,000 soldiers here and gives the government about $200 million in aid each year.
Both Cypriot parties live under the wing of their motherlands, and many foreign diplomats are convinced that answers must be sought not only on the island itself, but in Greece and Turkey.
In both countries, however, many people feel a deep emotional connection to their Cypriot kin. For politicians, advocating serious compromise on Cyprus-related issues is tantamount to suicide. As a result, hard-liners and demagogues dominate the debate.
Last month Greek Cypriot leaders offered Turkish Cypriots a place on the team that is negotiating the island's membership in the European Union. Turks refused on the ground that taking part "under the Greek umbrella" would be accepting a subservient position.
This attitude has upset both Europeans and Americans. The United States is pressing the Greek side to cancel deployment of the Russian missiles, and is encouraging the Turkish side to join in negotiating Cyprus's entry into the European Union. It has had no visible success.
"The Turks have everything to lose from boycotting the talks," a senior American policy planner said. "If they could get into the E.U., even as a minority in a Cypriot delegation, they would become the first Islamic voice in the E.U. and also a voice for Turkey. It would be a great step forward, not only for Turkish Cyprus but for Turkey itself."
In interviews, Turkish Cypriots said they were alarmed by developments they interpret as being aimed against them.
"If south Cyprus gets into Europe, that will mean a crack-up is coming," said Mustafa Ozkal, a Nicosia shoemaker. "The people in the south are doing fine economically, and they aren't trying to get in for economic reasons. They want to get in for political reasons. Their plan is to get all of Europe behind them, and then accuse us of occupying European Union territory and crush us."

GRAPHIC: Photo: The dispute between Greek and Turkish Cypriots appears to be entering a dangerous new stage. In Famagusta, in the north, Turkish soldiers posed with a church, converted to a mosque, as a background. (Staton R. Winter for The New York Times)
